THE RED PAGE.
IN THE NAME OF THE ARTIST
THERE is no denying that in N.S-
Wales the art of painting is still in a
very bad way. Year after year one
sees exhibitions in which work full
of interest and promise is mingled recklessly
with work that is utterly bad in conception
or execution, or with work in which real
merit is flawed and wasted by curious blunders
or shortcomings. But the promise never
reaches a really fine performance. The stan-
dard of the Art Society’s present exhibition is
no higher than that readied at the exhibitions
of five years ago or ten years ago. We are
merely marking time. There are possibly more
students painting up -to a tolerable level;
but the lack of any picture of outstanding im-
portance is as obvious as ever. This year
there is not even a picture good enough to
be purchased for the National Gallery.
Certainly, the Gallery trustees are willing and
eager to purchase any great work exhibited—
and if they were not there is a sufficient
public interest and public opinion to compel
them. Certainly, judging by the monstrous
purchases they have made in past years,
they are not difficult to please. But
what picture is there in the exhi-
bition that deserves to be hung per-
manently in a National Gallery worthy the
name ? The trustees, like other visitors,
have failed to find one; so, “ not to be
thought disagreeable,” they have spent a
hundred pounds in pleasant trifles without
any particular value.
It is open to serious question whether
public money should be frittered thus. The
Gallery endowment is not a very large sum,
considering what needs to be done with it; and
if the trustees decided to save every possible
penny in order to purchase works that would
be of undoubted value fifty years hence, we,
at least, should not feel inclined to blame
them. If those works were not Australian,
that could not be helped. The satisfaction
of the general public, and the education of
artistic students, might be held to compen-
sate for the loss of temporary assistance to
individuals. If the Gallery is to become a
receptacle for the inferior work of beginners,
on the ground of “ encouragement to local
art,” the public will be right to complain.
On the other hand,"considering to what an
extent the trustees have wasted the money
spent on foreign woik, it seems unreasonable
that local men should be robbed of the drib-
lets that fall to their share. The blots on
the Australian Court are obvious ; but the
blots on the ot her Courts cost a great deal
more money. E. du Faur, who was one of
the old men who complacently -patted each
other at the Art Society’s banquet, might
have reflected, before he joined in the
chorus of self-laudati6n, how often , the
public funds spent by authority of him-
self or his colleagues have purchased
rubbish at high prices. It is true
that the London board has been chiefly re-
sponsible, and that in more recent purchases
there has been a relation between price and
value more profitable to us. That does not
alter the fact that the Gallery has been
duped with pictures that are doubtfully worth
a tithe of the money paid for them ; and we
do not remember hearing or reading E. du
Faur’s indignant protest. His enthusiasm
and knowledge have been of public
service; but the blunders made during
his term of authority are glaring. The
Lond. St. James’s Gazette, recently bemoan-
ing the fall in gallery-picture prices, said the
evil results to Academic purveyors had been
postponed for some time by utilising the
colonial galleries ; but now, alas ! even
“ these last strongholds of ignorance ” could no
longer be relied on by the poor British artist.
&
The malefic influence of the old men is one
of the reasons for the stagnation in N.S.
Wales art. We should be last to refuse the
honour and credit due to the founders and
organisers of twenty years ago, and they
should be first to agree with the necessity of
their retirement from the field when their
inspiration is obviously exhausted. Early
paladins such as Lister and Ifaplyn and
Ashton have done useful work in their
day. Lister’s honest, conscientious painting,
within the limits of his talent, is still a
lesson to the bright youths who fail to
recognise that everything worth doing in art
is done at the cost of hard preliminary
labour. And it would do Lister and local
art a world of good if he would take
a year’s holiday for study in Europe
and America. Daplyn has occasionally led us
into pleasant sentimental landscapes touched
with a hint of the dexterity of his French
models—and KXfl. a year that he draws from
the Govt, subsidy would be better spent in
improving the instruction given to students.
A paid secretary isn’t needed all the year
round for one exhibition in September ; and
the register of students’ attendances could be
as easily kept by the instructor. Ashton
has organised and painted and taught and
caballed, and his pictures have been sold to
the Gallery at good prices, and we should give
some of them back to him with very great
pleasure for nothing. Of his three notable
students—S. Long, H. Ashton, and G-.
Lambert, the first has apparently discovered
that it is of little use painting for exhibition
until technical rudiments have been mas-
tered by the toil of assiduous years ; the
second has relinquished painting, having
possibly gained an inkling 'of the same
great truth ; and the third, it is reported,
found in Europe that lie had to unlearn
his methods and begin anew.
A Russian visitor who travelled over
Australia not long ago came to the conclu-
sion that this country needs a Prophet.
By “prophet” he meant a man with the
brains, character, and emotional energy
needed , to preach national ideals into the
heads of the people, a man who could sway
the crowd to follow him along a lofty path.
Art in N.S, W. wants a ptophet too a pro-
phet of sure knowledge, high ideals, and
courageous enthusiasms—-to unite scattered
forces and set twilight-groping disciples
on the right road. Lacking a pro-
phet, one could be satisfied with a
teacher. It is common to hear that
Bernard Hall’s ■ methods . are cramped,
taut he imposes his own ideas upon his
Victorian pupils, and so on. Well, one can
say this in return : that lie does seem to
have grasped the elementary truth that exact
drawing, sincere colouring, sound composi-
tion, and correct perspective are the founda-
tion of good painting : and that he has the
strength and pertinacity to try and make his
Students walk before they run. Once they
have learned their craft, obviously they can
develop their art in any direction to the
extent of their capacity. The trouble in
Sydney is that the young men are all
4 ‘ Artists ” and none of them are craftsmen.
Apparently the best teacher, in the cur-
rent notion, is the man who in the
shortest period can get his students’ work
hung at an exhibition, with a Gallery
purchase in prospect. It won’t do : there is
no salvation that way. The last ten years in
A.S. Wales are strewn with our promising
young men’s “failures”—and much more
with their teachers’ failures. One idea after
another has been tried : any idea but the
idea of hard, steady, persistent work at the
rudiments of the craft. As Wiegaud said of
his organ pupils, “Australians are too im-
patient. They want to practise two hours a
day for a couple of years, and then to be
masters.” It is the same with painting. One
often hears talk of the poor reward ; but
how many have earned any reward ?
JS
There is no prospect of improvement unless
the Government takes a hand. One can give
no decisive reason why the Government
should take a hand, unless this : that it pays
400 L a year for the benefit chiefly of a select
circle of the Art Society, and it might as well
get honest value for its money in a National
School under an instructor able to drive his
students through the elementary work, and
capable of inspiring them to something
better when the time comes. Or there
is no decisive reason except this : that
the National Gallery receives a subsidy
partly for the promotion, of local art,
and an efficient National School is
the best instrument by which local art
can be promoted. Apparently the matter
depends upon the initiative or inertia of
Education-Minister O’Conor. It will cost
some trouble to make the required change,
and need some judgment to get the right
man to direct the new scheme of operations ;
and these have not been forthcoming.
\v hen Rat McDurmer, in Lawson’s rhyme,
stood for Parliament to represent the town
of God-Forgotten, Lis nominator declared :
The art of God-Forgotten has no represen-
tative in Parliament. Seemingly Minister
O’Conor hasn’t “ got the pluck.”
It is easier to do nothing—and it is more
“agreeable.” The Art Society is proud of
being “ agreeable. ” It merely wants to be
let alone in its incompetency, and to spend
the public money as tlie Council pleases, and
it would never quarrel with a soul. It will
hold its exhibitions yearly, with the work of
the Council and the Oouucil’s private pupils
hung in excellent places ; it will ask the
Governor to open the exhibitions, and ex-
press genuine sorrow when he does n’t come,
because the Governors patronage “helps
the sales, you know ” ; and it will give
musical tea-parties among the exhibits till its
last gasp. All these deeds are confessedly
admirable : why then should peoole be
“disagreeable” to the Art Society—so
affable, so anxious to please ? Well, it seems
a singular thing, but some people are still
under the impression that the Art Society
exists and is subsidised from the public
treasury in order to promote Ait; and if the
Art Society won’t give us Art we don’t give
twopence for its royal charter and its
musical tea-parties. In its excess of agree-
abilifcy, the Art Society this year held a
banquet which, as the President carefully
pointed out, was (this time) paid for out of the
banqueters’ own pockets. We don’t want
banquets either, unless we can get Art along
with them : we had rather see the Art Society
starving and painting —painting pictures that
are really worth while. Give us pictures to
praise—pictures of Australian life with the
strong sincerity of Millet; pictures of Aus-
tralian scenery with the transporting rapture
of Turner pictures of heroism and beauty
to inspire and ennoble the citizens of a new
nation, and oh ! how gladly we shall dispense
with tea and patronage.
RECFIVED
Glenanaar, by P. A. Sheehan (Lond., Long-
man’s; 2s. (id.) Glimpses of Irish character, with
rather an Irish than a literary appeal. The story
hinges upon peasants’ hatred of an informer. A
page of history is taken in the “Doneraile con-
spiracy,” and truth apparently is but little varied
iu the author’s recital of the evil that befell
“Cloumper Daly’s” daughter and her son. By
way of America and a woman’s heart (?) the book
closes comfortably.
A Robin's Song and Other Verses, by Mary
Scott (Lond., Constable; 2s. (id. net.) Verses of
sentiment, for any girl’s dreaming en attendant
A Doctor in Corduroy , by Max Baring (Lond.,
Greening ; 2s. 6cZ.). A realistic story of Eng.
village life, with its share of vernacular humour.
The first half, based on childish reminiscences, is
simple and good ; in the second half the author
leaves memory for inferior invention. (The Red
Lion’s landlord speaks of the Enemy : “ Water in
the sea be wondrously magnificent, and it move
ab,:ut like a field of corn ; water in the river be
useful, it turns Stiggin’s mill; water in a pond is
all right to wash with, if it ain’t tew muddy ; hut
water in a man ! —well, it’s water, and it’s out of
place.” This remark was greeted with general
cheering.)
A Marriage in Burmah, by Mrs. M. Chan-
Toon (Lond., Greening; 2s. (id.) This is a fresh
kind of story, with “ cocktails with gin, chartreux,
crushed ice, and cayenne pepper” in it—and
numerous other drinks. Some time after the
drinks, the hero sucks “many limes covered with
salt.” The heroine, in presence of all the drinks,
reflects that “ a fustian order of mind was, per-
haps, Fate’s kindest gift.” When they part, he
has no money to give her :
“ That does not matter,” she interposed, quickly : “ but
you can do one thing much more important, if you will.”
She glanced at him earnestly.
“ What is that?” he enquired.
“ Promise me to limit .yourself to two wh’sky-and-sodas
in a day* however much you may want more.”
There was.a silent pause, broken only by the bells of a
parsing hansom.
“ I promise,” he answered.
“ Solemnly V” she asked, and she held out her hand in
confirmation.
“ Solemnly,” he echoed, and clasped her fingers tightly
in his. She smiled a little then, and they sat on in silence
for awhile, each busy with many thoughts.
This passage isn’t “comic relief,” but a very
serious episode. The unusual hero is a Burman
who comes,to London and studies law, succeeding
brilliantly, and taking the unusual heroine (a wild
Irish girl, apparently) back with him to Rangoon.
There the herd’s brilliancy is lost in mixed drinks,
and the heroine’s sad experience points the book’s
moral against mixed marriages. The picture of
life in Burmah is curious, and the heroine’s
naivete is charming.
The Origin of the Universe, or Revelation Up-
to-Date, by Theo. Gum (Melb. ; Co-operative
P.rint). Another pamphlet by Our Prophet; but,
despite the title, the revelation is in greater part
two years old at least. The Prophet’s dogmas
seem to have become fixed, and he exhibits a
regrettable tendency -to repeat preceding pam-
phlets. God had already been pigeon-holed as
“not omnipotent in the absolute sense,” though
“for all necessary and useful purposes he is
practically omnipotent.” It seems a pity to ram
a deity farther into a pigeon-hole in successive
pamphlets. If an Australian Prophet wishes to
keep his disciples solid, his prophecy must exhibit
continually-no v.el features. It is of no use running
last year’s Cup over again this year. We want a
fresh Cup every year, and a fresh prophecy.
«.
In The Red Funnel A. McArthur tells stories of
Maoriland magistracy :
A steamer was at a wharf, and a young woman put her
head out of a porthole. A man promptly kissed her. The
police arrested somebody, who protested that he was not
the guilty man. The Bench, however, fined him 201.,
remarking that females must be protected, and that if
they had only been sure he was the culprit they would
have fined him 50 1.
In committing a patient to a lunatic asylum, the M.L.
magistrate calls to his assistance two medical men who
. certify to “ (1) Indications of lunacy observed by myself.”
Under this heading a doctor wrote, “I have-been his
medical adviser for three years,” etc.
A fireman had robbed a man of his watch and chain.
The occurrence was seen by another man, who gave
evidence. Accused asked witness where he was when the
watch and chain were taken. The reply was “ three or
four ya r - away.” “ That’s a lie,” said the accused, “ for
there ■ ~s not a living soul in sight when I took the
water ' Three months ! ,
A M.L. judge, with his wife, was visiting a lunatic
asylum. They entered the part set apart for female
patients, and immediately one of these ladies threw her
arms around the judge, exclaiming “ Oh, my dear ; it’s a
long time you’ve been jn coming, but I’ve got you now 1 ”
The judge’s wife promptly tapped him on the shoulder
and said. “ Come away out of this ; I see this is no place
for you.”
Ever heard of Vincent Van Gogh, a mad
Flemish painter, who died a suicide in 189 G, aged
37? French magas. remind us of him; French
critics speak of his dazzling genius ; an Amsterdam
publisher issues a collection of reproductions of
his work. This little anecdote is of a savour. In
a gay fit he cut off one of his ears, cleaned it, put
it in an envelope, and carried it to a house where
you can find, lacking a sweetheart, an acquaint-
ance every time. Said he to the chap on watch :
“Here you are: take this in remembrance of
me ’’—and went gaily home again. Fancy a
painter like that living in... Dunedin, for example.
FLOTSAM
A new French poet, Valentine de Saint-Point,
gives us a passionate cry of Youth to Life :
Je veux vibrer A tout: au vent Uger qui passe,
A I’eau qui coule et bruit...
Je veux mordre aux fruits m(irs, me griser de soleil,
De clartis, to’ alanguir aans toutes les ivresses...
Mon corps ardent frissonne et tremble de desir,
S’cirque vers V inconnu, aide de toutes jievres.
Or very freely:
I long- to thrill to all, to all: to the loitering breeze,
To the stream that sings as it flows...
I long to bite in the ripest fruits, to be drunk with the
sun
And the wide clear spaces, to languish in every ecstasy...
My burning body quivers and yearns with desire,
Curving towards the unknown, greedy of every flame . .
And then, “ h la Mort ” :
Un jour, lorsque j’aurai tout aime, tout connu,
Su la suSlimite du mat de la Caresse,
En superoe cadeauj’irai vers Vlnconnu,
Te porter ma beaute, te doniier via jeunesse.
And then one day. when all that is I’ve loved and known,
Known every pang sublime of all the world’s embraces,
I ’ll fling myself in gift superb to the unknown,
And bear thee, Death, my youth, and dower thee with my
graces.
That, now, is how Youth should feel and talk—
with insatiable appetite, with ecstatic aspiration
(one reflected iu washing a yellow local Art student
leaning against the Pitt-street air and whiffing a
contemptible cigarette from lungs that one
good, deep breath would have paralysed).
The Lond. publishing firm of Grant Richards
was lost recently in a pond of troubles, with T.
W. Crosland ihoaning loudly in the adjacent
mud. Mrs. “E. Grant Richards” has now
founded a new firm, with Grant as manager.
The Adelaide, branch of the Geographical So-
ciety of Australasia maybe congratulated on its
purchase of the famous York Gate Library of
geography and travel. There are 6000 volumes
and pamphlets, many of them relating to this
country. The collection was formed by S. W.
Silver, and was the subject of a spedal catalogue
by E. A. Petherick, whose work in connection
with Australasian bookselling and bibliography
will long be remembered.
Once more... Frederic Harrison, away from Posi-
tivism and his pets, is soundest of English critics.
In the July Fortnightly he discusses Art, and adds
authority to the contention that a mere pattern,
however admirably painted, is not a picture,
though it is the essential foundation of a picture.
A picture has not only to be painted well, it must be a
thing that is worth painting—interesting, original, beau-
tiful) imaginative. As Tennyson said, you might write a
very correct Wordsworthian line—a Mister Wilkinson, a
clergyman—but this is not poetry. An old man with
sticks, a sailor boy in a boat, a girl feeding a bird, are
honest facts, which you may honestly paint—but they
don’t make a picture. Millet’s Angelus has gone round
the world, because it is more than an old peasant and his
wife. It is a solemn and pathetic poem. To make a work
of art something more than “ values ” is wanted,
“ Painters wamt cultivated brains as well as nimble
fingers.”
“As an author,” says G. B. Shaw, “all I ask
from a publisher on the royalty system at six
shillings is a modest twenty per cent, or so, a three
years trial, an agreement drafted by myself, and
an unaffected bookseller.” And he continues :
I don’t want a compulsory partner for life. I don’t
want a patron. I don't want an amateur collaborator.
I don’t want a moralist. I don’t want a Telemachus. I
don’t want a pompous humbug, nor a pious humbug, nor
a literary humbug. I cap dispense with a restatement of
the expenses, disappointments, trials, and ingratitudes
that pave the publisher’s weary path to a destitute old
age in a country house, with nothing to relieve its mono-
tony but three horses, a Mercedes automobile, and a flat
in London. I have heard it so often !
I don’t expect absolute truth, being myself a professional
manufacturer of fiction ; indeed, I should not recognise
parrect'truth if it were offered to me. I don’t demand
entire honesty, being only moderately honest myself.
What I want is a businesslike gambler in books, who will
give me the market odds when we bet on the success of
my latest work.
A paragraph from Motoring Illustrated :
One of the consequences of the low birth-rate in France
is that there are not so many children to play “ last
across ” on roads frequented by motor-cars.
How’s that for pure cynicism ?
Torn Roberts’s alleged picture of the opening of
the Commonwealth Parliament received some
sharp criticism in the Senate. The Govt, pre-
sented a bill for four prints of the picture, 92Z., to
the order of too-good-natured Barton. According
to Syd. Telegraph report, Senator Neild protested
against the extravagance of paying 23d each for
prints which had been openly offered in the
Senate for 3gs.
He would not pay for a frame for the picture, because
it did not represent what took place.
Senators : Hear, hear.
Senator Matheson : Simply a fictitious picture.
Senator Neild said it was simply a fancy picture, which
reminded one of a band of smugglers trooping out of a
cave. A feature of the picture was that persons who
were not present on the occasion it referred to were
represented in the picture. He would like to know who
was going to pay the freight and insurance on the origi-
nal picture and the fare of the persons in charge during
its tour round the States, which was for the benefit of a
syndicate which was afterwards to sell prints. That was
what it meant. (Hear, hear.) He should be sorry to see
the Commonwealth being made an advertising medium
for any syndicate. (Hear, hear.)
Surely Senator Neild is n’t “ in the picture ” ?

Indigestion & Rheumatism.
A THOROUGH CURE.
The Case of
Mr. J. TYE,
(By a Special .Reporter.)
The public will be interested to
learn now it came about that Mr.
Jacob Tve threw oil' a serious illness
that had overtaken him, and full de-
tails of the fortunate event will be
found in the following conversation,
which took place at his residence, JNo
19 Victoria-street, Alexandria :
Mr. Tye : “Two years last month l
was laid up with rheumatism and in-
digestion, and if over a man felt that,
his term on this planet was just about
ended it was I. Such acute pains as
those which had entered my shoulders
and arms you could not imagine, and
1 felt so still' and sore that it took
me all my time to get out of bed in
the morning, let alone work."
Repo ter : “Had you been suffering
from those complaints for any length
of time ?”
Mr. Tye : “Yes ; far too long tor my
liking, and I earnestly hope that 1
shall never have such experiences
again. In one way 1 would rather be
dead than go through the same
agony, for it was really too dreadful
for words. I tried all sorts of lini-
ments for the rheumatism, but even
temporary relief did not come from
them ; and as for taking medicine in-
ternally —well, it was not my fault
that 1 did not pet better, for 1 took
plenty of it. For months my diges
tive organs were on strike' —wouldn t
do their work at all, in fact—and
there is no doubt that the nossibilitv
of getting well again seemed very re-
mote. It was an extraordinary thing
if for half a day my head was not
aching, and 1 do not think anybody
could be more troubled with giddiness
than 1 was. It was a peculiar feeling,
as if I was on the point of fainting,
and what also assisted in adding to
my physical troubles was a sensation
in my chest as if the breathing tubes
were clogged up. Of a morning the
taste 1 had in my mouth was enough
to turn one sick, but I had enough of
that as it was, for after every meal 1
vomited the food up again almost di-
rectly, and getting as thin as 1 did
was only a natural consequence.”
Reporter : “Perhaps you ate too
heartily, and took more food than the
weakened condition of your stomach
could withstand ?”
Mr. Tye : “There was no fear of that
for my appetite was too poor. JNo ; if
i only took a couple of biscuits I
would vomit them up again, so you
can see what a critical state 1 was in.
One day I was seized with cramps all
over me, and 1 had to come home, and
lie up for a couple of weeks. Oh, I
thought J. was done for then, and the
way my loins used to ache was some-
thing fearful. 1 was so restless that I
could not get half as much sleep as I
wojokted—iuo+. dozing off in fits and
starts tine —and I never slept but what
1 had dreams of a most repulsive char-
acter ; that my nerves were all broken
down was quite evident from the way
in which 1 used to tremble if anything
out of the way occurred, and I became
the victim of an uncontrollable cranki-
ness that used to arise on the least,
provocation. Truly, 1 got excited and
bad tempered sometimes for nothing,
although 1 tried hard enough not to,
and the longer I sutiered the more dis-
mal in mind I became. I felt that 1
had no desire for anything—no amuse-
ment, no work, no company —nothing
at all, in fact, except to lie down and
remain as quiet as possible. Many a
day 1 came home with a sinking feel-
ing, which induced me to say that I
Wanted some nourishment, but directly
it was placed before me I’d turn
against it. But there was something
else that worried me a great deal.”
Reporter : “Yes ; what was that ?”
Mr. Tyo : “My heart, for the way it
palpitated, especially if I had been ex-
citing myself in the Jeast. was some-
thing- terrible, and when I retired at
night, after lying down a little while
it would start fluttering in a most dis-
tressing manner. Naturally enough L
thought l had some form of heart dis-
ease. but soon after accepting a
friend's advice, and starting on a
course of Clements Tonic, all doubts,
on that question were settled, for the
heart's action became as regular as it
could be. That was a great, relief to
tnv mind, 1 can tell you, and didn't I
feel delighted to know that at last L
had got a remedy that was really able
to do me some good ? Don't lor a mo-
ment think that L am overdrawing my
story, as 1 have no reason lor doing
so, and it is nothing but the plain
truth when l tell you that Clements
Tonic actually saved my life, for when
l started on it 1 was almost too weak
to move, and I bad certainly nothing
to expect from any other medicine!
Nothing J had hitherto tried could
lessen in the slightest degree my rheu-
matic pains, but I can honestly say
that Clements Tonic gave me relief in
a few weeks, and by continuing with
the same remedy 1 was freed from
those torments altogether. Oh, how
thankful 1 was for all that, yet other
benefits still awaited me."
.Reporter : “In respect to your di-
gestion, 1 suppose V"
Mr. Tye : “In every possible way I
benelited by the use of Clements Tonic,
and I reckon there is nothing in crea-'
tion to equal it for giving an appetite
to a sick person. Why, Clements Tonic
made me eat so that 1 never felt satis-
fied, and it was a healthy appetite,
too, for 1 never felt uncomfortable af-
ter eating. And weren’t my people sur-
prised at the way I was coming round
again—right back from the grave. 1
might say—and what a treat it was
to feel some vigour and strength run-
ning through my frame once more. My
nerves were settled and invigorated to
a remarkable degree, and it was not
long before 1 ceased turning about at
nights instead of sleeping soundly, and
my sleep was not marked by frightful
dreams as was previously the case. No;
in every way 1 had improved beyond
recognition, my sunken cheeks having
filled out, and there was an air of ac-
tivity about me that had been lacking
for months past. Constipation had
troubled me considerably, but by tak-
ing Ur. Fletcher’s pills with the Cle-
ments Tonic 1 soon got. over that dif-
ficulty permanently, and from the day
1 was cured over two years ago my
health has been as sound as a boll. 1
ought to make special mention of the
promptitude with which Clements To-
nic stopped the vomiting after meals,
and when that was done I made
wholesale progress, and soon lost
every pain and ache.”
Reporter : “Well, if you are agree-
able your narrative will appear in the
papers
Mr. Tye : “And I trust every sulferor
in the land may read it. Certainly,
publish the facts I’ve given you how
and when you please.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring Song.
I am the Vision and the Dream
Of trembling Age, and yearning Youth;
I am the Sorceress Supreme.
I am Illusion ; I am Truth.
I am the Queen to whom belongs
The royal right great gifts to give ;
I am the Singer of the Songs
That lure men on to live and live.
There is no music like to mine ;
I sing in green, and gold, and red ;
I pour from secret casks the wine
That cheers the cold hearts of the dead.
My harp it has a thousand tones,
And makes the world with joy aflood ;
The old men feel it in their bones,
And life leaps laughing in their blood.
The sourest mortal all in vain
Shall try from me to keep apart;
I have no commerce with his brain—
I storm the fortress of his heart.
I am the Soul of things to come ;
I make a lover from a log ;
I make a poet of the dumb ;
I make a seraph of a frog.
The lover with a wrecked romance,
The gambler by misfortune struck,
I bring to them another chance —'
New life, new times, new love, new luck.
My names are all the names impearled
In all the songs my singers sing;
I am the sweetheart of the world—
I am Carissima—the Spring !
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.
Marching By.
O have you seen them passing ?—nearly twenty
millions strong !
Hark! their battle-drums a-beating, and the
cadence of their song !
Hear them tramping down the bye-ways where the
lonely scouts have gone :
’Tis the tramping of our army—Labor’s army
marching on !
O their tools of trade are glist’ning, and their flags
are all unfurled ;
Yea, and brain and heart have sought them from
the walks pf all the world !
And the slaves who groped in blindness through
the darkness of the past
Have seen the light with open eyes, and join our
ranks at last.
From the grandest halls of knowledge, from the
palace and the slum—
The prince beside the pauper—O ! it’s splendid
how they come !
From the peaceful home of plenty, and from
hovels raised in dearth,
Come the army of the workers—and the best of all
the earth !
And the hopes that light the future, and the
dearest dreams that were—
They’ll waken in the bosoms of our faintest com-
rades there.
See the vanguard still advancing, and the standard
waving high—
Hark the drums, the song, the tramping !—lt is
Labor marching by !
Ah, that song shall not be silenced, nor that cease-
less tramping stayed,
Till the birthrights of the People at that army’s
feet are laid ;
And those drums shall not be deadened, nor those
waving banners furled,
Till their owners march in triumph through the
highways of the world !
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
The Sea-Witch.
She reaps the curling surf, unharmed,
Green seas break o’er her,
An evil Sprite, repellent, charmed,
Death quails before her.
A plunging ship with shattered gear
Swirls in the breaker,
Her storm-deck blanched with faces drear,
The gods forsalce her.
The sea-witch hears the prayers in scorn
And cruel laughter,
And lashing fast the wings of Dawn
Flies swiftly after.
She spreads her black hair o’er the sky,
Swart night grows darker ;
Green seas obey her flashing eye,
Winds ravage starker.
The breakers, whipped to savagery,
Gnash, snarl to rend her.
And whelm the ship in lustful glee
And awful splendor.
The goaded waves, with sullen roar,
Strain plate and anchor ;
The sea-witch skirts the frothy shore,
Her dank hair danker.
’Mid crash of quivering wood and steel
White foam seethes whiter,
The livid rocks crunch through the keel,
None may requite her.
The sea : witch frees the wings of Dawn
And seeks cave-shelter,
And rage-spent waves with Death forlorn
In anguish welter.
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

transmission of MS.

THURSDAY. SEPTEMBER 7, 1905.
PEACE IN THE NORTH.
The American eagle screams exultantly, the dove
of peace in its talons, and it is now established as
a proved fact, what has been for some time obvious
to persons of intelligence (except the editors of
the Australian daily papers), that Japan had
reached in the struggle with Russia a point of
exhaustion at which even a great patriotism, backed
by Asiatic phlegm, threatened collapse. There
is no other explanation of a peace in which Japan,
after a series of notable victories, gains hardly
anything but renown, and Russia cedes nothing to
which it had at any time a clear right. That ex-
planation is in no sense derogatory to Japan.
Rather otherwise. The Yellow Nation has
proved not only its capacity to organise
and to fight, but its capacity to show a
brave and united front to the foreigner when
at the point of desperation. There is nothing
really finer in the Japanese campaign than the
sturdy “ bluff” of the first peace proposals, when
the nation had not a card left in its hand. To
demand Saghalien, the dismantlement of Vladivo-
stock, the surrender of the interned remnants of
the Russian fleet, the future limitation of Russia’s
naval power in the Far East, and an indemnity of
£200,000,000, was magnificent, when Japan was
prepared to make peace with the very shadowy
concession of one-half the frozen island of Sagha-
lien. And the national spirit which enabled this
bluff to be kept up so long as there was the
slightest hope of twisting anything more from the
Russians was an admirable example of unity
and secretiveness. An occasional visitor came
away from Japan with a vague idea of the des-
perate straits to which the people were put to
maintain the war, but from the Japanese press, the
Japanese politicians, or the Japanese people there
was not a whisper. At the time when Togo’s
fleet was in wait for the Baltic Armada, and the
escape to Vladivostock of even a portion of the
Russian fleet meant probable ruin to the Japanese
cause, there was shown to the outside world no
trace of uneasiness; and it was not until after the
decisive naval battle that Europe was allowed to
know for certain that the previous loss of two
Japanese warships had made the cause of Asia
very uncertain. Suoli unity and courage compel
respect. It is almost unexampled in war, and
would probably be impossible in any European
democracy, unless the people were fighting a
purely defensive war for their homes.
* * * *
Splendid as was the crowning achievement of
Japanese diplomacy it need bring no recruits to
the cult of blind Jap-worshippers. The Yellow
men have proved grit, capacity, and solidarity. If
genius is an infinite capacity for taking pains,
they have shown genius. All that the European
has set down as the theoretic perfection of method,
the Japanese have copied faithfully, and made of
it a practical attainment. What, for instance, a
European war officer knows should be done in
regard to medical service and sanitation in the
field, and believes also never will be done in actual
service, since it is so difficult to impress upon
every unit the importance of small precautions,
the Japs have actually done. The long-established
rules as to the proportion of loss that will cause a
regiment to stop have been swept aside by soldiers
who seemed to nave little of the ordinary human
respect for Death—in fact, they have been swept
aside by Russians and Japanese alike. Withal
the Jap has not established as an axiom his
superiority to the European. He has shown the
particular Asiatic type of virtue to an extraordi-
nary degree, but it has been throughout Asiatic
virtue. There has been furnished no reason
why the Caucasian should count his future
days as limited to the length that the good-will
of Japan may allow. In a campaign against
the least developed of the great European Powers
at its weakest point—the point farthest from its
main resources—and at a time when that Power
was attacked at the heart by internecine warfare,
Japan has triumphed ; but in gaining the triumph
has given no proof—indeed, no indication—that
against even a small European people, fighting
under circumstances of national enthusiasm which
would give the European spirit a chance of show-
ing itself, it would have any sure hope of success.
There is no need to load a recognition of the great
national qualities Japan has shown with the
absurdity of Japiolatry.
* * * ' *
The Nippon generals and admirals have been
careful to ascribe each successive victory to th 6
“glorious virtues of the Mikado,” and if the
Mikado be taken as typifying the nation, the state-
ments were true enough. It is the courage and na-
tional spirit of Japan, rather than any special genius
for military or naval warfare, that have made vic-
tory. Japan is intensely national. Every step in
its development is taken with the single idea of
advancing Japan. It is vexed with no theories
that international fraternity is best served by
treating the manufacturing foreigner as a brother.
It doesn’t look at a native manufacturer as an
habitual criminal. It has no Stinking Fish party to
misrepresent its position abroad. It has no political
leaders whose eyes are fixed on gaining some foreign
decoration ; no Freetrade party to tell it that it
is too mean and poor a country to make its own
engines, ploughs and rifles. It allows no absentee
landlords, and keeps Japanese soil for the
Japanese. It does not believe in “encourag-
ing foreign capital to come and develop the
natural resources of the country” and to
remain a leech on the country for ever after.
Its fiscal policy is stringent Protection, and
everything a Japanese can make is made
in Japan by Japanese; the foreign immigrant is
tolerated only so long as he lias something to
teach that Japan has not yet learned. Its
financial policy before the war was to keep out of
foreign debt and to buttress the scanty revenue
drawn from a bitterly poor population by
Government monopolies of profitable trades, such
as that in tobacco. Further, the Japanese citizen
has a keen sense of civic duty and accepts it as an
obligation to train himself for the defence of his
paper house and his patch of rice field. Protec-
tion, universal military service, honest and
scrupulously home-controlled finance—those have
been the principles which the adaptive Japs
thought best to erect as pillars of Nationalism.
And each victory has justified the value of each
and roared to Australia a message of advice and
warning.
« * * *
Here in Australia we have a country of
enormous possibilities—far greater than those of
Japan. We have a population drawn originally
from the best and hardiest types of Europe, and
fit now to cope in individual character with any
people on earth. But .national organisation and
national spirit are lacking. A careless habit of
mind has allowed men who are foreign and craven
in spirit to usurp authority. A great proportion of
our politicians look to gain a living by destroying
and hampering Australian industries, and to gain
honor by winning approval abroad. The majority
of the influential newspapers are bitterly un-
patriotic—the bond slaves of the city im-
porting magnates—and ever preach the doc-
trine of the hopelessness and helplessness of
Australia. The nation is neither trained to manu-
facture in times of peace nor to the use of arms in
times of war. Thus there is not a nation in Aus-
tralia —only the raw material for a nation. Yet
there is nothing more certain than that, to hold
Australia, Australians must organise into a nation.
Japan, the victor in the war just concluded, the
teacher by whose example we could learn so much,
is also the living threat hanging over the head of
this continent. For some while, poverty now
should keep Japan quiet. It has spent nearly
£200,000,000 on the war, and will get none of it
back in indemnity. It gains no territorial con-
cessions of any great financial value, and must,
probably for some few years, submit to the quie-
tude of exhaustion. But for a people with such
splendid national organisation, the process of
recovery will be speedy. Within a very short
time the Japanese menace will be as real to Aus-
tralia as is now the next Melbourne Cup. What
do Australian statesmen propose ? What, in par-
ticular, do Alfred Deakin and John Christian
Watson propose ?
A GREAT WASTE OF MONEY.
Bigjohn Forrest, who is perhaps as ordinary and
un-original a Treasurer as any Commonwealth
could ask for, got through the jungle of his
Financial Statement without throwing any dazzling
light on the question of the transfer of the State
debts. The principal thing he did say was that it
was difficult, if not impossible, to work out a
feasible scheme whereby the Commonwealth
would act as borrower for the States and serve as
their agent in contracting new liabilities, which
remark was very wide of the target. The ques-
tions that are urgent to-day do not concern more
borrowing and the creation of more separate State
liabilities, but refer to the consolidation and pay-
ing off of liabilities now existing. The hope-
lessness of John Forrest lies in his inability
to realise _ even this elementary fact; his
one financial idea is something about more
borrowing. Probably there will be this, year
the usual conference re the taking over of
the State debts by the Australian Parliament,
and the usual deadlock. Yet there is just now a
fine opportunity to actually do something, if
members were in earnest on the subject and if
Joe Cook would stop talking for a minute or two.
According to Coghlan, there are, during the next
three years (1906-7-8), the following loans falling
due:—
New South Wales .. ■* £9,591,918
Victoria • • 9,308,800
South Australia .. .. 3,912,050
Tasmania .. .» *• 884,626
£23,697,394
Even allowing for the fact that a portion of these
amounts is merely borrowed trust funds, there aie
still £18,000,000 or £20,000,000 to be renewed in
the open market. It is the duty of the Australian
Parliament to take possession of these debts and
all succeeding debts as they mature, and to pro-
vide for their renewal, whether the formal and
complete taking over of the State IO U has been
arranged for or not. If the Australian Government
takes over the maturing debts informally it will
be, until some definite arrangement is arrived at,
simply the creditor of the States, collecting
interest from them like any other bondholder, and
paying interest to its own creditors, if it has any,
out of the proceeds, and retaining the difference.
When a general taking over and consolidation is
arrived at, this arrangement can be simplified, but
the larger the proportion of the State debts held
by the Commonwealth the easier the consolida-
tion will be.
» • * *
There are various ways in which the work of
turning the State debts into a Federal liability
can be commenced
(1) By adopting the Canadian banking system, whereby
the banks are required to hold at least 40 per cent, of
their cash reserves in Federal paper money (which would
give the Commonwealth command of, say, £8,000,000),
and by putting aside at least half that amount (say
£4,000(000) as a fund for the buying up of State
debts. The interest on the State bonds acquired
(about £140,000 or £150,000 the first year, and more each
succeeding year) to be applied to buying up more State
debts.
(2) By setting aside a small sum annually (£lOO,OOO, or
£250,000, or £500,000) out of Federal revenue for the
purchase of State securities.
(3) By raising Federal loans (with a sinking fund
attached to each) and using the proceeds to buy up State
securities.
With the money thus raised the Commonwealth
could do two things. It could offer, whenever a
State loan fell due, to take up the whole loan
and constitute itself the State’s creditor until a
general consolidation could be arranged, or if the
State was too grumpy to deal on those terms, it could
take up portion of the loan like any other ten-
derer. And it could steadily and systematically
buy up State securities in the open market—the
cheapest securities available—and thus again con-
stitute itself the States’ creditor until a general
consolidation could be arrived at. Provided the
Commonwealth established a sinking fund for
every loan it incurred, it could probably borrow
so cheaply that it could buy up State securities in
the open market at a considerable profit, the in-
terest it received from the States being con-
siderably more than the interest it paid to its own
creditors.
« * * *
The crowd of State miseries who can only re-
cognise in the Commonwealth the States’ bitter
enemy, and who never realise that the people
who form the States, and the people who
form the Commonwealth are the same people,
and that a man can’t lie in wait behind a hedge
with a gun to shoot himself as he goes past—this
crowd would probably fail to see any advantage
in a scheme which would gradually substitute the
Commonwealth for the foreign usurer as the
States’ creditor. The crowd of State miseries is
unfortunately past reasoning with, and the only
thing that seems likely to meet its case is execu-
tion. To reasonable people, however, it would be
apparent that, if the Australian people in their
Federal capacity buy up the debts they owe in
their State capacity, either out of the proceeds of
a Federal note issue, or out of Federal revenue, or
with the proceeds of Federal loans which are
gradually extinguished by a sinking fund, they
become to that extent their own creditors, and the
man who is his own creditor owes nothing. And
one thing is certain : If the Commonwealth,
supposing it attached a sinking fund to its
loans, could borrow at 3 or even 3J per
cent., and with the money thus obtained could
buy up State securities at a price which yields 3§
per cent., then every million that the Common-
wealth doesn’t borrow on these terms and for that
purpose is a wilful and purposeless throwing away
of £5OOO. a year. And its neglect to adopt the
Canadian banking system, which would enable it
to apply at least £4,000,000, raised without interest,
to the buying up of State securities, is, in like
manner, a wilful and purposeless throwing away
of £140,000 or £150,000 a year. Also the waste
and loss through failure to do these things is ac-
cumulating all thetime at compound interest. If
the Wilks and Conroy push would only drag its
attention away for a moment from its petty State
miseries, and try to look at some large national
questions, it might realise that this is a good deal
of money to sacrifice with no per contra to set
against the loss.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Spirit of Secesh.
Last Friday Sydney Telegraph came out in a
tentative sort of way as an advocate of the disso-
lution of the Commonwealth. “It is possible,”
it said, “to build too recklessly on the assumption
that the Commonwealth is indissoluble ” :
Federation is more or less unpopular throughout the
Commonwealth. It has ceased to inspire enthusiasm. It
is the target for much slighting remark. In each State
there are many electors who would gladly join a sincere
and capably-led secession movement. Why? It may be
frankly admitted that in pre-Federal days expectation
was pitched too high. It always is on such occasions.
Federation had to be strenuously fought for, and in the
heat of conflict the prophetic references to the advant-
ages of union were over-colored. Perhaps exaggeration
was unavoidable. Federal sentiment is now a feeble
thing, and the existing disappointment is the penalty of
past exaggeration. But that is far from a full explana-
tion of the position. It has become a platitude to say
that the Commonwealth got into the hands of a poor
crew when it was launched. It could not easily have
been more unfortunate.... The actual results of union
are viewed with feelings of amazement and dismay.....
Apparently, the belief was and is that the union cannot
be endangered by anything that the Parliament or
Government can do, that it is “indissoluble,” that the
people’s hope and patience are stronger than their dis-
appointment and exasperation. How else can we under-
stand the refusal to undo what ought never to have been
done?
And much more to the same effect—some of it
true but most of it ridiculous, As a sample of
the part that is true, Federation had to be
“ strenuously fought for.” To disabuse the popu-
lar mind of the amazing lies and misrepresenta-
tions and slanders which the Sydney Telegraph
and papers of its kind circulated in the anti-
Federal interest was an enormous task. The
Telegraph, probably about the most unscrupulous
rag that ever brought discredit on this hemisphere,
even got so far, during these strenuous days, in
its efforts to promote disunion, as to solemnly
publish a leading article about a purely imaginary
duty which the unbrotherly Victorian (N.S.W.’s
best coal customer) was alleged to levy on
N.S.W, coal to the alleged ruin of that
industry. It called on IS'. S. Wales not to
federate with a person who did imaginary
things like that, and it has been callipg in the
same strain ever since. And both then and since
it has required strenuous exertion to explain to
the public the true inwardness of the Telegraph’s
Petriana lie, and its Six Hatters lie, and the
other countless lies and slanders with which it,
and its remarkable following, have striven to undo
the great work of making a United Australia.
But, on the whole, the exertion has been rewarded
with success. The Australian Parliament cer-
tainly has not done all that was hoped from it. It
should have suppressed the miserable little gang
which the Telegraph and similar papers sooled
on to obstruct all business and block all
progress. It should have put its foot down
on that paper over some of its worst slan-
ders and meanest distortions, and compelled
it to preserve some semblance of the truth
and decency which do not come naturally to it.
As regards some of the especially malignant false-
hoods it should have made the Telegraph’s
responsible person either support his statements
with evidence, or, if he failed to do so, it should
have locked him up in a cool place to reflect. And
the Australian Parliament may yet do some of these
things. Meanwhile, the Telegraph makes a
grievous mistake in thinking that the time is ripe
for secession, just as it made a grievous mistake
five years ago in fancying that there was some
hope for its gospel of parochialism. It over-rated
then, and it over-rates now, the power of common,
crude, inartistic mendacity. Even in New South
Wales a referendum to-day would certainly give a
larger majority for Federation than did the refer-
endum of 1899, and a much larger one than that
of 1898. There is no “secesh’’ in the air. The
people of N.S.W. know that their food and clothes
cost no more because of the small measure of Protec-
tion they have got; that their industries are
moving ahead quite as fast as they did before; that
their State is gaining immigrants instead of the
departures exceeding the arrivals as they did under
the Reid regime; and even the Telegraph’s
frenzy can’t make them believe that Victorians
are all bushrangers and venomous quadrupeds;
also they are beginning to realise that,
though Federal progress is slow now, it may
improve when Cook, and Pumpkin Smith, and
Wilks, and Conroy are dead or suppressed.
They even know that the Six Hatters were not
put in gaol or thrown overboard. And they have
no real sympathy to waste on the Telegraph, how-
ever mad it may be over the loss of its free postage
and its free railway carriage. They recognise that
paper as a useful organ in the way of cablegrams,
and more or less handy for advertisements about
the wooden cottage that is for sale, but if it fancies
that it is a Jefferson Davis it has its mistake all
to itself.

The Queensland Land-Tax.
The Morgan Ministry deserves well of Queens-
land for many reasons, but most of all for its
resolve to be financially honest, despite the almost
impossible load of debt left on its hands by its
awful predecessors. For the coming financial year
the Morgan Government again intends to keep
absolutely clear of borrowing, thus putting up
the incredible record (for Queensland) of two
years without a loan. It further proposes to sot
aside the proceeds of land auction sales for public
works purposes ; it would certainly be better not
to sell land by auction, but when a State is
tottering under the burden, in proportion to
population, of the Biggest Debt on Earth, some
things must be done which might otherwise be
avoidable. And to fill the gap in the finances
the Government proposes to levy a graduated
land-tax on estates of over 2000 acres, beginning
with Id. in the £ on estates worth over £20,000
(the first £lO,OOO to be exempt) and ending at 3d.
in the £ with no exemption on these worth £llO,OOO
or more. The proceeds of this tax are expected to
enable Queensland to struggle to the end of the
financial year not only without borrowing, but also
without a shortage, and it certainly looks a care-
fully devised plan, which will yield some much-
required revenue and at the same time commence
a much-needed discouragement of the great land
monopolist. Certainly, it is miles ahead of
N.S.W.’s hopelessly undemocratic land-tax, which
falls alike on the man with £2OOO worth of land
which he farms himself, and on the rack-renting
absentee with £200,000 worth. Queensland has
never since the beginning of its history had so
good and so strenuous a Government as the
Mohgan-Kidston Administration has proved itself
up to date. It is a Government which Does
Things, while the Worker, which ought to be
helping it to do things, stands aloof and dreams
hazy dreams of “Socialism in our Time.”
“Socialism in our Time ” may not be obtainable,
but “Solvency in our Time” is a possible
thing, and the Morgan Ministry is reaching for it
with both hands.

N.S.W. Lands Scandals.
Justice Owen’s inquiry into the N.S.W. Lands
scandals continues without getting much nearer to
the question of most interest —did the strangely
winning land agents succeed so well with the
Lands Department by force of personal magnetism
or because they passed on some of the huge fees ?
Exactly how much W. N. Willis could do with
the Lands Department was shown by the evidence
in regard to a series of Bathurst-Orange leases.
These were annual leases converted into improve-
ment leases, and in every case W. N. Willis, or
somebody acting for W. N. Willis, was agent.
As it was important that there should be expedi-
tion to have the leases granted during 1903 (that
gave the lessees a chance of securing a re-appraise-
ment) the Department of Lands got a move on.
By a “rush ” telegram the local Land Board was
hustled into activity. Then an extraordinary
issue of the Government Gazette was printed so
that the leases might be gazetted during 1903.
This involved a special meeting of the Executive
Council, and the attendance until midnight at the
Government Printing Office of the printing
staff and Lands Department officials. Finally,
an official was sent specially to Moss Yale
on December 31 to secure the Governor’s
signature to the leases. He didn’t reach
Moss Yale until midnight, and the Governor did
not sign the leases until Jan. Ist. Nevertheless,
a Gazette notice showed that the leases had bsen
executed on Dec. 31st. Great, indeed, was the
power of the land agent! Evidence presented of
late has been giving attention more to the land
agency transactions of Messrs. Bath, Close,
R. D. Meagher and Crane, and W. N. Willis’
fee account has been added to by only £1565,
making it now total—so far as disclosed—£42,24s.
[ln the issue of August 24 The Bulletin included the
name of Mr.-E. MV Clark, M.L.A., in “the list of names of
members and ex-members of Parliament who have been so
far mentioned before the Commission as having received
money directly for land-agency transactions, or having
received money from W. N. Willis or other land agents


or legal or other services.” This paper is now assured by
Mr. E. M. Clark that it is not true that he has received
fees for any services in land-agency matters, and it gladly
corrects the mistake.]

“Consulting the States.”
The unfortunate habit of “ consulting the States ”
on matters about which the opinions of the States
don’t signify in the least seems to be growing
on the Commonwealth. The worst offender
in this respect was G. H. Reid, with whom the
habit became a disease, and who ended up by
submitting to a conference of State Premiers, at
Hobart, practically every item of Federal policy.
This is not, perhaps, a matter for surprise in the
case of Reid, the parochialist, but it is a matter
for surprise, and for more than surprise, that
Alfred Deakin should show signs of falling into
the same habit. No man knows better than he
does that, with some exceptions which arise out of
the peculiar circumstances of our Constitution,
the policy of the Commonwealth with reference to
Federal matters ought to be determined without
the slightest reference to the likes and dislikes of
the State Governments. And no man knows
better than Deakin that this is the only possible
way of making the Federal system work smoothly.
Yet we find him, when a question is raised in the
House as to the prohibition of the importation of
opium, asking for an adjournment of the debate
“ until replies had been received from the States.”
What, in the name of all that is rational,
have the States got to do with it ? If the Com-
monwealth wishes to prohibit the importation of
opium—and there seems to be plenty of reason for
doing so—the attitude of Bent or Carruthers
towards the proposal is in no way of more
importance than the attitude of John Smith, who
lives at Cobar. It’s a great pity that Deakin
doesn’t realise this, for apart from the weakness
and delay the consulting system introduces into
Federal policy, it encourages Bent and Car-
ruthers in the belief that their opinions on
Federal matters are really more important than
those of John Smith, of Cobar. Precedents
are quickly made in politics, and every bad
precedent of this sort made by Deakin will cer-
tainly be used to make a still worse one by some
weak Prime Minister in the future. It is difficult
enough to carry on the Government of the Com-
monwealth already; it will be impossible when
State Governments successfully assert a claim to
be heard on matters that the Constitution commits
to the exclusive jurisdiction of the Commonwealth.
In this particular case Deakin’s explanation is
peculiarly weak. Opium, he says, is grown in
some of the States, and the Commonwealth
could not successfully put down the evil
unless those States co-operated in the effort.
But he was not asked to put an end to
the use of opium in the Commonwealth, and, if he
had been, he couldn’t have done it. He was asked
to prohibit its importation, which the Common-
wealth has complete power to do. If, after doing
that, he likes to suggest to the States that they
should prohibit its growth, he can do so, at the
risk, of course, of being told to mind his own busi-
ness. However, the merits of this particular case
don’t matter'much. What is really important is
that the Commonwealth, in exercising its exclusive
powers, shouldn’t fall into the weak and dangerous
habit of officially consulting persons who have no
right to be consulted.
N.S.W. Cat Politics.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, a couple of years ago,
shrieked its approval of the “statesmanship” of
a little group of yelping N.S. Walera who wasted
the time of the Australian Parliament, and drove
that great public servant Kingston almost to his
grave, by stonewalling the tariff debate. The
intellectual grandeur of these men—the miserable
Wilks, the clamorous Conroy, the inane Nanny
Smith, and that public misfortune Lonsdale —
was held up for admiration, in that they had
proved their capacity to say nothing for hours at
a stretch, in order to stop the business of the
country and drive the country’s Ministers to dis-
traction. No other State but N.S. Wales, it was
proudly claimed, could produce such great tongues,
such mighty mouths, such untiring throats. The
great blank stonewall of N.S. Wales intellect was
something which was calculated to make Aus-
tralia gape in astonishment. Now the same tale
is repeated. These are extracts from the Sydney
organ of N.S.W. insectism last week
That kind of aptitude which members from New South
Wales have the credit in the Federal Parliament of posses-
sing above all the other legislators—the aptitude for
party manoeuvring—is being rapidly acquired by Mr.
Kelly. He has already, indeed, developed to an unusual
degree for a parliamentarian not through his second
session.
His colleague, Mr. Johnson, has not the same irrepres-
sive volubility. He has not the mental dexterity, not
quite so much of that restless anxiety for plunging head-
long into the thick of every Parliamentary move. But he
has other qualities not less serviceable." He has more
solidity, more sustained industry, a larger share of that
sort of determination which keeps an end in view and
makes steadily for it. It seems to be the easiest thing - in
the world—possibly it is—for Mr. Johnson to jump to his
feet and talk right on for two or three or four hours.
Mr. Johnson has always been at home in the Chamber,
and he has the instinct for party fighting, and when he
is wanted, he is never short of material.
“The kind of aptitude which members from N.S.
Wales have the credit of possessing ! ” The apti-
tude for making useless, irrelevant speeches; for
using the forms of the House and the absence of
effective Standing Orders to block public business ;
for interfering, between the will of the majority
and its expression, the barrier of bowlings and
lies and false accusations and blockhead speeches,
meaning nothing, and intended to mean nothing !
If that is something creditable, then a carbuncle
is a thing to make a man a proud soul, and con-
firmed idiocy is a matter of thanksgiving. The
Telegraph takes smug satisfaction in the reflec-
tion that this sort of “aptitude for politics” is a
special possession of the N.S.W. members.
Thank Heaven that it is so. Were all Australia
of the same mind, or want of mind, it would be a
poor outlook for the Commonwealth. The fact is
that N.S.W. politics have always been something
between a cat-fight and a South American revolu-
tion, with short lucid intervals of legislation. The
Parliament of that State met from the beginning
in the old Rum Hospital, and the reek of the rum
of Macquarie’s days has always been in its blood.
From the earliest days there were fierce riots, and
one legislator was hauling on his brother legis-
lator s beard. Run over the list of prominent
N.S.W. politicians, living and dead, and practi-
cally all may be associated, at one time or other,
with some fisticuffs exploit or some sordid
row. They were, perhaps, good fellows, all
the same, in their private capacities, and
The Bulletin must confess to a sneaking
fondness for some of the participants in the narrow,
crooked, rowdy, picturesque politics of the old
Rum Hospital—politics full of “copy” and rich
in color. But it was a bad school for legislation.
Whilst Parkes was being baited over the lunatic
O’Farrell, and Copeland’s cups were being dis-
cussed, and the Dean case was being tried, and
the Pope of Rome was being canvassed,
and the free fights were being fought,
Local Government and a hundred other neces-
sary things were neglected, and a tradition
was being established that it was the duty of a
Parliament to quarrel, and to stonewall when no
row loomed over the horizon, and to keep stonewall-
ing until a fresh shindy came in sight. That
tradition a big section of the N.S. Wales members
carried down to the Australian Parliament—a
tradition of reckless lying accusation, of miser-
able personal pettiness, of silly useless obstruc-
tion. And the Sydney Foreign-trade papers
approve of that conduct enthusiastically and call
it "statesmanship.” Fortunately for the credit
of the State of N.S. Wales, there is another
section of its representatives which somewhat
retrieves its reputation. Watson, Watkins,
Hughes, Spence, Thomas, and others of the
Labor party, are as well respected as any
members, and an occasional man of the stamp of
G. B. Edwards shows that it is possible even to
be a RsiD-ite and yet not a blatant, rowdy
obstructionist. But the bulk of N.S.W. re-
presentation in the Federal Parliament consists
of clawless old tabby cats in the Senate ; and
shrieking, spitting wild cats, with “a great politi-
cal aptitude ” and “a fine party sense”—such as
Reid, Wilks, Conroy, Johnson and Pumpkin
Smith —in the Representatives.
The Melbourne Trams.
Vic. Premier Bent having started to talk about
some electric State lines that will compete with
the Melbourne Tram Co., that fine fat monopoly
begins to squeal concerning an alleged "invasion
of rights,” and the right-thinking person (who
never really thinks) is being whipped up to
protest against “ interference with private enter-
prise.” Private enterprise ! Why the lines were
built with £1,650,000 borrowed by the munici-
palities, on the security of their assets and
revenues. All that the co. put into the business
was £250,000. Yet, while the co. has, during the
past two years, actually divided 24 per cent, on that
amount, and 15 years ago paid as much as 38 j; per
cent., the municipalities which put up the money,
and mortgaged their rates in order to get it, haven’t
fingered a cent in the way of profit. Besides
paying interest on the capital cost, and a sinking
fund to wipe off the mortgage by 1916, the people
who use the trams have, in the past five years,
yielded £488,124 of profit to the co. There have
been times when, as mentioned just now, the co.
paid 38£ per cent, per annum in dividends; the
past five years’ operations alone would have re-
turned to the co. the whole of the capital it put
into the concern, and left £238,124 for dividends
and such line repairs or renewals as hadn’t been
paid for out of revenue before profit was declared
—which probably are not many. During those
five years the revenues of the ten municipalities in-
terested have amounted to £2,367,236 on an aver-
age rate of Is. 9d. in the £; so if the munici-
palities had had the sense to build the cars, &c.,
and run the trams themselves as well as building
the lines, they would still have owned the whole
outfit and been able to knock 4d. in the £ off their
rates. That is where the amazing folly of the
average property owner shows up; municipal
enterprise would almost abolish the rates which
he always grieves at paying, but he will have none
of it because it is “ Socialistic.”
The N.S.T7. Engines.
The position in regard to the building of loco-
motives for the N.S.W. railways is this :
Foreign tenders are the lowest. They are open to the
minor objection that—as in the case of Yankee engines
before imported—they may prove unsuitable ; and to the
very important objection that the country which has to
go abroad for the manufacture of its locomotives doesn’t
deserve to have railways, and doesn’t deserve to be called
civilised.
The second estimates in point of price are those of
the State workshops at Eveleigh. And it is held out as a
reasonable probability that, if State manufacture becomes
an established thing, the cost will in time, as the men gain
experience, and the business expands and grows more
economical with expansion, be even less than the import
cost. Victoria now makes its locomotives in State work-
shops at a figure lower than any importer can quote.
The highest estimate of cost is from a N.S. Wales private
firm. But this is the one which Premier Joseph
Carrutiiehs proposes to accept, and in defence of which
he browbeats the Chief Commissioner for Railways, who
pleads that if the engines are made locally they should be
made in the Eveleig-h State shops.
Unless it is assumed that Freetrader Joseph
Carruthers is deliberately striving to cast dis-
credit on the principle of local manufacture, it is
hard to explain why he is so very anxious to accept
the local private offer, which is the dearer and the
less likely to prove satisfactory; and decline that of
the State workshops, since the State workshops
claim that they can produce the engines at a cost
very little, if any, greater than the import price,
and since the claim is supported by the fact that
the State workshops in Victoria actually produce
engines at a price lower than that at which they
can be imported. The obviously best course for
N.S. Wales is State manufacture. Why is Joseph
so keen on the more expensive private manu-
facture ?

Melbourne’s Brick Combine.
In the discussion re Vic. Premier Bent’s scheme
for State brick-works much stress was laid on the
fact that the Hoffmann Brick Co., the head of
the brick combine, has paid only six per cent, in
dividends for three years past, and its distri-
butions over a period of ten_ years do
not average three per cent. This is quite
correct. The experience of the brick companies is
deserving of the sympathy of anybody. In fact,
the history of brick-making in Melbourne during
the last 15 years is one of the most eloquent ex-
pressions of the advantages of State control that
could be found. Among its many failures, private
enterprise has seldom been guilty of more ghastly
fatuity than in this trade. Prior to the boom, a
number of brick companies existed in Melbourne,
and, by quietly and peacefully meeting the demand
caused by the natural expansion of the metro-
polis, they made a good living. Then came
the boom. Sky-scrapers went up in all
directions, miles of jerry cottages were
run up in the suburbs, and more sky-scrapers and
more cottages were projected to cover hundreds of
square miles. The brick companies caught the
fever. They increased their capital out of all
reasonable proportion; and, at the same time,
every man who had any clay in his back-yard
floated it into a new brick company. For a few
months they made merry—some of them never did
even this ; then came the smash, and scattered
over Melbourne from end to end were alleged
brickyards, unable to earn the cost of keeping
their machinery clean. During the years
of depression many passed into the hands
of bailiffs, assignees, and other financial
parasites. Then buildings began to sprout
again, and it was not long before the combine came
into existence. Started by two or three of the
big companies, it took in the little ones that were
still breathing faintly and, in order to effectually
block any future competition, bought up all the
odd clay holes and rusting machinery which lay
defunct in the outer areas. After that the price
was thrust up to 40s. a thousand, and it is still
there. A year or two ago, an enterprising firm
got ho]d of a new clay area and started to fight.
The combine responded in a manner worthy of the
Standard Oil Co., by informing every architect
who used the newcomers’ bricks that the combine
would refuse to deliver its products to any other
job he might have in progress. This threat fail-
ing, the combine sought pacific measures and in a
few months bought out its opponents. Then 40s.
again became the ruling price for ordinary bricks.
It is not a maximum, as the combine’s friends fre-
quently assert. Picked cherry-colored facing
bricks go up to double that sum. And all the
time bricks for consumption outside Melbourne,
where the country companies come into com-
petition, are sold for 10s. less. The position of
shareholders in the combined co. ’s has been un-
doubtedly hard, but there is no reason why the
community should suffer for their ill-luck or lack of
foresight any more than that the community should
be forced to maintain the greengrocer who starts
business where there are already too many green-
grocers. The shareholders have made a bad busi-
ness bargain, and, in accordance with the brutal
law that governs things, they must suffer for it.
It is such suffering that brings out those sturdy
qualities of self-reliance, etc., etc., that anti-
Socialistic orators so often mention. The concern
is still only a mediocre business proposition, owing
to the over-capitalisation it has to face; yet
there is not the slightest moral justification for the
State standing aside and permitting the growth of
Melbourne to be stunted and men kept out of
work on its account.
A Navigation Matter.
From a Federal pressman :
The Mcßeid Government did some peculiar things
during its short and precarious period of office. A
despatch arrived from Alfred Lyttelton, British Secre-
tary of State, on April 20th, asking the Commonwealth to
send four delegates to England, in conjunction with four
from Maoriland, in order to discuss the provisions of the
proposed Australian Navigation Bill. The Mcßeid
Ministry carefully dropped that document into a bag, and
did its best to lose the bag. When the Deai..n Govern-
ment came along it found the bag and rooted out its con-
tents, and immediately forwarded Lyttelton’s despatch
to the Navigation Commission. Lyttelton stipulates
that one delegate shall be sent to represent the ship-
owners and another to talk for the seamen, leaving the other
two open. The Australian Bill is bound to make large
departures from the Imperial Act of 1894, and Lyttelton
holds that if such departures are necessary then it is time
that the Imperial Act was dry-docked and scraped and
revised. The British Act of 1894 gives “ the Colonies ”
power to legislate as regards outside shipping coming to
their ports, and provision is also made to enable
“ colonial ” Legislatures to pass Acts dealing with shipping
within their own jurisdiction—that is, as regards vessels
in the coastal trade and other ships of local registration.
But Bills dealing with ships belonging to any other British
possession must contain a clause suspending their oper-
ation until the Imperial assent has been.handed in by the
flunkey. By the Commonwealth Constitution, however,
the British Parliament has granted the Federal Legislature
full powers to deal, without exception and in as drastic
a fashion as it may' please, with all shipping and navigation
in Australian waters—reserving, of course, the power of
veto. This may mean a lot, as Kingston showed in his
case against the P. and O. In that case the Privy Council
held that the Commonwealth had jurisdiction over all
ships from the time they entered Australian waters
until their final departure, and thus it will be seen that
there are many big questions to be talked over. The
Commonwealth’s Navigation Bill, as submitted to our
Parliament, prevents any ships not owned in this country
from carrying cargo or passengers between Australian
ports unless they take out a license giving the Common-
wealth the right to regulate their operations. This regu-
lation includes a stringent provision for the payment of
Australian rates of wages.
It would be a first-class idea to send four qualified dele-
gates to England, because there are so many points of
divergence between the laws now in force and those with
which it is intended to supplant them. It would be
infinitely better to have the proposed legislation discussed
at length, and perhaps settled satisfactorily, than to have
the Commonwealth’s Bill hung up by the Imperial autho-
rities, arid perhaps vetoed without Australia having any
say in the matter. The Maoriland Navigation Bill was
hung up for 2J years, and the slow British Parliament has
so pronounced a habit of hanging up “ colonial ” matters
that it almost amounts to a disease.
One point is worth stressing. The British shipowners
are, of course, actively hostile to the proposed Austra-
lian legislation ; and the great majority of Bull’s ship-
owners’ associations have arisen and implored the Board
of Trade to speak to Australia in a severe voice. The
Board of Trade, being a thoughtful animal, has had
expert legal advice upon every clause of the proposed
Commonwealth legislation, and has obtained the opinions
of the best men at the British Bar. Consequently, the
delegates that are sent should know their business, both
from the argumentative and the practical side.

A Bill for dealing with the lands attached to
Sydney Observatory calls some attention to a
valuable institution which is gradually being
muddled to pieces by the N.S.W. Government.
Sydney was always the centre of Australian
meteorology just as Melbourne was of astronomy.
Even now it is run by assuredly the ablest and
best known meteorologist in Australia —with an
“acting” appointment, and a: salary of £3OO a
year, as against the £7OO paid in Victoria and
£OOO in Westralia. Westralia pays a clerk in
the meteorological department better than N.S.
Wales pays its chief. The N.S.W. office is
also falling into decay by reason of the in-
creasing difficulty of making correct observa-
tions through shortness of money, lack of instru-
ments, and moth and rust generally. It is always
toiling under almost insuperable difficulties.
When the Commonwealth takes over the business
it will presumably value the Sydney Observatory
at the value the State puts on it, and make it a
small out-station, whereupon the spectre of State
Frights will arise when it is too late and say that
another great injury has been done. Meanwhile,
Adelaide has ideas of being the scientific centre of
Australia, and is said to be already arranging to
take over the business.
