“G.F.S.” on the immigration question:—
I think it is necessary to impress the necessity of indus-
trial and agricultural immigrants. I would favor a Pro-
tectionist tariff—heavy enough to make it impossible for
other countries to supply necessaries which we can easily
manufacture at home. In order to replace the people
who would naturally drift into the industrial cities thus
formed, I would send out responsible men to Europe—
especially to countries where military oppression is
carried on (Schleswig-Holstein, for instance)—and gather
in the oppressed. This would take time and care in
selecting those suitable, for all the oppressed will not
necessarily make good colonists (the Armenians have been
persecuted by the Turks until the English newspapers
have almost melted with grief, and the Armenian still
remains a parasite). Then, having called in all the land
let on improvement leases, and taxed all other land which
is being improperly held out of cultivation, I should'
acquire money by taxation to pay the passage of the
immigrant to Australia and keep him supplied with food
until he was settled on the land.
« —,
A proposed new standing order of the House of
Reps, goes a wrong way to a right end. Under
it the Speaker or Chairman of Committees will
be able to disregard notice of the absence of a
quorum in the House itself if he has reason to
believe that the requisite number of members are
“within the precincts of the House.” Tedious,
drivelling orators are the cause of most counts-out.
Men fit to fight Togo blanch before a prosy blighter
with a three-hours’ capacity, and fly from the
scene. But they don’t mean to quit the House
or break up business ; they only want to avoid
the abomination of verbal desolation. Therefore
when the number present falls below quoium
strength the Speaker or Chairman should not
have power to close up business, but should
have power to direct the talker to discontinue
his desolating utterances. Then the bell should
be rung, and, if a quorum is found, the next man
should start, but, if he also loses his quorum, he
should be closed down in the same fashion,
The proposed standing order will allow the
bore to go on filling Hansard and blocking busi-
ness. Now a sitting member within the pre-
mises is not a sitting member in the ordinary run
of cases. It is only while in the deliberative
chamber he can exercise his Parliamentary power,
and counting him a member of a quorum while he
is out of the chamber is taking the vote of a legis-
latively dead man. More than that, such a stand-
ing order is liable to all sorts of abuses. How is
the Speaker or Chairman to know what or how
many members are stowed away in distant parts
of the premises, or in what state of body or mind
they may be ?

Thus Parson Fitchett of the gory battle stories :
The incident of the Six Hatters was picturesque enough
to arrest attention throughout the Empire, and it pro-
foundly and justly shocked British susceptibilities. The
Six Hatters have probably cost Australia £1,000,000 each
by the damage they have done to the Australian reputa-
tion for common sense.
Will Fitchett give particulars, and clear definite
evidence of the loss of even £1,000,000 to Australia
through the incident he raves about. He isn’t asked
to support his yarn about a loss of £6,000,000, but
will he show a loss of one-sixth that sum ? Since
the Six Hatters incident Australia’s revenues
have looked up ; its States in the aggregate
have began to show a surplus instead of
the old regular deficit ; two States have
managed to leave off borrowing; the other four
are, in the aggregate, borrowing less than before ;
population is increasing faster than before ; and
there has been a wonderful accumulation of money
in the country, as shown by the banking returns.
Where is the loss of £6,000,000, or £1,000,000, or
even £lOO,OOO ? If Parson Fitchett can’t point
it out, then, by reason of his slanders, he is a
national discredit.
The Law in Victoria is just now in a glorious
tangle over the Chinese ticket-marking lottery
business. A while back some score of Chows
were fined by a P.M. about £lOOO altogether for
running a lottery. They appealed, and the first
three cases came before Justice Hodges, who
quashed them, on the ground that there was “ no
evidence that Ah Fat and his mates made any
promise or agreement to pay £75 or any other
sum if a person who marked a ticket succeeded in
striking out ten hieroglyphics corresponding with
the bank’s ten.” Justice Hodges admitted having
his doubts, but his nice legal mind wanted incon-
trovertible proof that Ah Fat aforesaid wasn’t
paying a large rent for a small shop, and spending
16 hours a day therein blotting out figures on
scraps of dirty paper, just for the pure joy of the
thing and for the pleasure of gazing on
hundreds of hard-faced individuals who handed
him 6d. apiece for the love of marking
similar scraps of dirty paper. Last week
14 more cases came before Justice a’Beckett—
who straightway dismissed the first appeal, putting
the matter briefly and lucidly thus: “A person
went to a place where someone was behind a
counter. Money was paid for a ticket. There
was a conversation as to the purchaser ‘ going for
a certain bank,’ and when the purchaser returned
he looked at something to ascertain the result.
Then, according to the number of corresponding
marks on certain tickets, he received a prize or
nothing. That being the evidence, the whole trans-
action explained itself as a lottery. The only other
explanation would be that they werelunaticsindulg-
ingin an act of lunacy.” This j udgment, lookedat
carefully, was considerably and justly severe on
brother Hodges. The rest of the appeals were
hastily postponed till next month, when broth 'r
Madden or Holroyd may have a cut in and
involve the subject in further gloom and mystery.
Meanwhile the defendant in the latest appeal—
Ah Lean, or words to that effect—threatens to
appeal to the High Court; but if that regrettable
heathen is well advised he will be satisfied with
such costs and fines as he has already sustained.
The High Court is a practical sort of' animal, and
is almost certain to uphold his conviction- —With
further costs.

Thus Senator Best, recently returned ftom
abroad, concerning the British howl at Australian
Socialistic legislation • :
The pessimistic complaints that I heard Seemed to me
all the more unreasonable when I looked round and ;s5/,v
in the management of British affairs far more advanced
forms of Socialism than we have yet attempted in'Aus-
tralia. Take, for instance, the enterprises of the Lon-
don County Council, not only its vast system of
cheap tramways (controlled with a special regard
for the needs of the poorest section of the work-
ing classes), but its housing schemes, to which it
is committed to the extent of over £5,000,000, anct its
recent expenditure of £200,000 or more in the construc-
tion and launching of a line of passenger steamers on the
Thames, to replace an inefficient service formerly carried
on by a company. There- is ho limit to the business
ambitions of the London County Council, and they are all
more or less socialistic in character and tendency.
According to recent statistics, the annual expenditure of
the local bodies of England and Wales works put at about
£122,000,000. That a very large proportion of this outlay
goes in the establishment and maintenance of trading
enterprises is, of course, well-known. The municipal
authorities have never hesitated during the last 10 years
or so to incur heavy financial responsibilities when it
appeared advisable to' do so in the general interests of the
community.
A yen is 2s. or thereabouts; and this list shows
the amazing growth of the Japanese debt in that
coin :
1871 .. 4,880,000
1877 .. 67,774,000
1878 .. 250,026,000
1894 .. 299,447,000
1897 .. 421,245,000
1899 .. 506,167,000
1902 .. 559,621,000
1904 .. 994,437,000
And the debt has been growing this current year
as fast as it did in the last two years in' this list.
Therefore to pull through the present disturbance
Japan has had to more than double its debt. The
figures are enough to make Australia think hard.
What would its chances, after exhausting its
credit in peace time, be of raising £50,000,000 or
£100,000,000 supposing it had to fight for its life ?
And what would be the condition of the Monkey’s
tail by now if, instead of keeping some of its
borrowing powers in reserve, it had drained the
market before the war of every pound that the
market would lend? Even as it was, Japan
would certainly have had a struggle to get the
money to carry on with if it hadn’t been so remark-
ably successful in the beginning of the row that
lenders began to look on an indemnity and
some new territory as part of the security ;
and Australia, r unprepared Australia, can t
reasonably expect to have that sort of help.
Almost as much as it needs gun-factories
and guns, Australia needs a reserve of Cohen s
confidence ; but whenever anybody mentions that
Cohen has got in a small stock of it, Australia
takes the hint and rushes him, and draws on the
confidence to the last copper; so its chance of
being able to double its debt, when it may be
necessary to do that or be wiped out, is kept
at a uniform nil. . '/

♦
“Magda” stands the test of many revi-
vals, and each fresh presentation strengthens the
impression that it is one of the notable plays of
last century—an epoch singularly barren of good
dramatic work. Sudermann treats his theme so
masterfully that the intellectual appeal saves the
emotional from becoming too distressing. The
conflict of human motives it presents is necessarily
tragic in incident and in catastrophe; between
the stupidity, pride, and narrow-mindedness of
the father and the worldly wisdom, vanity, and
greatness of character of the daughter something
must be crushed, Sudermann gives what is the
just and natural outcome of the conflict —a tragic
ending, in which the father loses his life, and the
happiness of all the little household is shattered.
That is inevitable, assuming the antecedent con-
ditions. There can be no compromise. The old
father, with his German ideas of sentiment and
purity and duty aggravated by a lunacy which
has taken a religious tendency; the vain and
prattling step-mother, and the artless sister, who
suffers from anaemia and hysterical emotionalism ;
the clever, stormy Magda, her character hardened
to steel by many loves, sorrows and treacheries,
returning home as a prodigal with the Golden
Calf of worldly success—these conflicting ele-
ments thrown together must breed tragedy.
Of course such problems are hardly possible of
occurrence in real life. A wise provision of Nature
makes like, as a rule, breed like and when a
cuckoo life comes into the domestic nest, a tragic
clash of characters is, as a rule, averted by the
fact that the strange-natured child inspires no
deep paternal affection. In short a Col. Sch wartze
does not have a Magda as a child, or, if he does,
he has not for her that deep unreasoning affection
which makes the difference of her character from
his a matter of life and death. The fact that the
conflict of the play is thus not exactly human,
but rather one between types than real persons,
makes the tragedy less bitter. It is to Sudermann’s
praise that he has put his problem quite fairly,
that he takes a strictly impersonal view of the
issue, and allows the type which is Magda and the
type which is Col. Sch wartze to plead each its
own case. The subsidiary characters are equally
well-handled—the pastor, who is a preacher with-
out being a prig ; the sly, unwholesome, canting
politician, who first betrayed Magda and set her
on the path of sorrow and to greatness.
* * * *
“ Magda ” was always one of the best of
Miss Nance O’Neil’s impersonations. The revival
of the play on Saturday night at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), after the lapse of some years, shows
her the possessor of a riper art than hitherto.
She has lost some of her exuberance and
has worked up the finer shades of the part.
M‘Kee Rankin’s Colonel Sch wartze is the same
masterly bit of work that Sydney so well remem-
bers ; it makes one forgive him his Macbeth.
John Glendinning, in the very trying part of the
Pastor, is manly and pleasant; and Miss Jane
Marbury’s Marie is more than successful. Miss
Ricca Allen, Gaston Mervale and Andrew Robson
also contribute to the success of a well-balanced
performance.
#* * *
“The Walls of Jericho” at Sydney
Criterion draws crowded houses. Sydney
“Pointers” follow the ways of the smart set-
ways as devious as those of the serpent on the
rock that Solomon mentioned —with intense
interest, and feel quite complacent in the thought
that “ we and the London smart set have much in
common.” Cigarettes, gambling, cake-walking,
and drivelling belong to both cities alike. And
then the Brough artists take all the vulgarity out
of it by their finished acting. The “Walls” will
endure for many nights to come.
** * *
At Sydney Royal an assortment of virtuous
heroine, comic relief man, comedy girl, and
British Jack Tar is still being saved nightly by a
brass band from crucifixion in the middle of
Africa. As to what happens to them while they
are in the hands of the brass band, the play says
nothing ; perhaps after a longer experience of the
band they regret the interruption of the cruci-
fixion proceedings. “ A Sailor’s Sweetheart ”
draws good houses, and no change is as yet sug-
gested.
* * *
This (Thursday) and to-morrow evenings
the “Bohemian Girl” wall dream at Sydney
Palace of her marble halls, and Thaddeus will
sing of his flat, cold, uninteresting Poland,
where the sledded Polack used to be.
Edward Farley takes his old part of Devilshoof, a
character in which he was always interesting. The
public is cordially urged to attend, partly on
acoount of Devilshoof and partly for the sake of a
grand old opera, which is becoming somewhat
soaroe these days. By way of a sudden descent
from the sublime to the irresponsible and the
degenerate, “In Town” will follow on Saturday.
** * *
The Belle Crome Co., which has been
playing at Sydney’s Centenary Hall, goes into the
Lyceum next Saturday. It is a gay little
troupe. Miss Crome must have much of that
quality that Wise Men have just discovered
in the sun —radio-activity, or something to that
effect. She is as bright and light as a sunbeam ;
she dances, sings, plays on the bells and violin,
and bubbles with comedy instinct, and seems to
walk on air all the while. Mr. Ebenezer Vickery
is to be congratulated on his new departure. The
Lyceum was beginning to have a church-mouse
reputation, and to smell of the tombs of the saints.
There’s a nice Queensland girl in the troupe —Miss
Jefferson—who took the Eistedfodd honors for
three consecutive years in the Banana State,
where they Eistedfodd with some energy when
they start; also there is a bass singer—one
Williams —a young man who was once a soloist
at the Royal chapel at Kevv (England), His voice
is so good and well-trained that writer concludes
from his coming unheralded that he must have
strayed here, or possibly his health was doubtful
and he wanted a new atmosphere and a change of
germs.
** * #
Sydney and the Tivoli are seeing the last
of Kara, the deft juggler whom a long spell of
’citis seems only to have made more frisky.
Nobody would have thought that the most proper
and precise person of three months ago would ever
cake-walk across the stage ; and yet he often
breaks out into that now. ’Citis will have an
added terror if the cake-walk is numbered among
its after-effects. The first result of manager
Rickards’ present orbit among the stars of the
European ’eavens is M. Payen and the little
lady who introduces him in dumb language.
Payer), presented as a mechanical Pierrot,
who goes when he is wound up, preserves the
semblance of automatonism through an act in
which there is a great deal of padding. There
are a few acts on the hanging ropes which are
good, but any amount of men get less money for
more work—the whole strength of the turn lies in
its unique dressing. Bella Perman furnishes one
attractive item, and one banal coon item dressed
in chiffons; and the rest has either been mentioned
before or doesn’t deserve to be mentioned now.

. . The Nellie Stewart season at Melbourne
Princess’s has been a rush of revivals since the
middle of last week, when the opening “double
bill”—“’Op O’ Me Thumb” and “ The Marquis
de Treville”—was supplanted by “Her Own
Way.” In both performances the star shone
brightly, but public interest in the one-part plays
was rather dull. On Saturday came “ Camille,”
the drama which is beginning to rival “ East
Lynne” as an attraction for the moister sex.
Melbourne had not previously seen Miss Stewart
and the Musgrove co. suffer the grief and woe that
befalls everybody in “ Camille,” so the occasion
was marked by some new enthusiasm, and the
heroine’s gorgeous costume, fatal cough, and effec-
tive performance were applauded vigorously.
“ Pretty Peggy ” is due to-night (Thursday), and
“ Sweet Nell ” will close the season to-morrow.
# * *• *
A new comedy—“ The J.P.”—and a new
company enrolled under the new banner of
Meyneli and Gunn, will come to light at the
Princess’s on Saturday. “The J.P.” claims to
be a wildly funny thing, full of domestic interest.
The advt. states that it has left two million
joyous hearts in its wake, which dimly suggests
that they died of laughing at it. J. J. Dallas,
the principal comedian, is somewhat known to
this country by repute, and Miss Florence Lloyd,
the leading lady, is affectionately remembered as
the Miss Lloyd of some years ago. Another
member of the co. who will not want an intro-
duction is Miss Maud Chetwynd, back from Eng-
land and other foreign parts.
%
“The Cingalee ” prospered exceedingly at
Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) last week, and if there
is room for any further prosperity this week the
Williamsonian theatre will get it. An orthodox
musical comedy of the most expensive sort, played
by the R.C.O. Company immediately on its
return from a long tour —well, by any other new
name than “The Cingalee” it would smell as
sweet. The name, and the costumes, are new
features of this very orthodox production. The
prima donna is nice and new, and the unexpected
appearance of Miss Rose Musgrove, confirms the
pleasing announcement that she didn’t die in
Sydney when she was said to do so. To feel
positively assured on this point is worth all the
money charged for admission.
This is the fourth week of “ Besieged in
Port Arthur.” Outside of Bland Holt’s melo-
drama peace has been declared between the
Russians and Japs, therefore a fine opportunity
offers for breaking the news to Brown who im-
personates a Jap warrior. According to the cable
a number of heart-broken brown men wept bitterly
when they heard the terms of peace. There is no
reason why a special messenger from the Mikado
shouldn’t bring the tidings to Brown, whereupon
Brown might exclaim, “ O my revered ancestors ! ”
and then burst into tears.
** * *
Howard Thurston, commonly known as
“ The Great,” took possession of Melb. Athenaeum
Hall last-' Saturday, and for the next few weeks
that quiet-spoken Master of Black Arts will do
the uncanny things he lately introduced to Sydney.
Thurston talks less and performs more than the
average magician. His manner of dealing with
the property he borrows from the audience is excep-
tionally adroit. When he calls certain cards from
the vasty wine-glass they ascend far into space
and remain stationary instead of jumping
out nervously. Also a horizontal lady rises
in the same calm, deliberate manner under
the seeming attraction of the wizard’s finger-
tips. The effect is called an illusion, although
the lady is as sweetly real as they make them.
Another quaint subject for conjecture is a glass
tank, firstly filled with water, which water is sub-
sequently strengthened with a liberal infusion of
girl. The late King George lIT., who couldn’t
understand how the grapes got into the jelly,
would have been staggered at the appearance of
the girls in the tank. Thurston’s manual dexterity
and deceptive contrivances took Melbourne’s
fancy on the first night, but the elaborate stage-
setting for the second part of his show necessitated
a long intermezzo entertainment-illustrated songs
and biograph that need severe editing.
* * *
Miss Jessie Maclachlan—the braw, like-
wise the bonnie, ballad singer—made her re-ap-
pearance at Melb. Town Hall last Saturday, and
was to give an alleged final concert on Tuesday.
There is, however, a divinity called General
Desire who doth oftimes shape the ends of popular
songstresses, and maybe the end of Miss
Maclachlan will be prolonged until later than
Tuesday in the present week, in which case
intending patrons are earnestly requested to buy
their tickets early and relieve the crush at the
doors.

At the Opera House, the first results of
manager Rickards’ pilgrimage through Europe
and America will be submitted to popular taste
on Saturday. In the meantime, several people
are preparing to go away, and when they are
quite gone their spaces are to be more than filled
by selections from the invading army of talent.
The Gilbert and Sullivan Co. at Adelaide
Royal has called up many memories of old casts.
Judged by its own merits, it is a bright and
pleasing combination, doing fair justice to the
plavs, which already seem a little old-fashioned,
so entirely have other writers failed to copy their
spirit. Holly Castles and Aggie Thorne carry on
old traditions by coming only up to their lovers
chins, and Vinia Heloitte is smaller than either
of them. The Frank Clark Co. has left the
Tivoli, and is appearing in Port Adelaide at
present. Harry Shine is as funny as ever, and
.Lizzie Kirk has a wink which deepens by at least
two shades the already cerulean tint of her songs.
Into the Tivoli has come Sheridan with “The
Earl and the Girl,” and the dog-trainer and the
iron-jawed lady and the American bounder crowd
the small house nightly.
The Wild Cat Column.
When N.S.W.’s unfinished and unsatisfactory
Premier finds a State enterprise which is doing well and
making handsome profits for the community, his first idea,
apparently, is to close it up and get the work done in a
worse fashion and at greater cost by private enterprise.
By way of a case in point it, has been decided to abolish
the Treasurer’s State Fire Insurance account. A little
over a year ago the State decided to be its own fire insur-
ance co. as regards the resumed area of Sydney, and
started a new fire insurance institution of which it was
the sole client. The new concern opened without any
paid-up capital, but its callable capital was simply the
whole assets of the State —say £370,000,000 —so it was
eminently qualified to carry the £750,000 of fire risks
which comprised its business, according to latest advices.
The State Fire Insurance business seems to have worked
at a disadvantage, inasmuch as the premiums paid into
the fund appear to have been a good deal less than those
that would have been charged by a private office ; never-
theless it did amazingly well. This was its record: —
Added to Total
June, Premiums. Expenses. Losses. Reserves. Reserves.
1904 .. £BBB Nil Nil £BSB £BBB
1905 .. 1,463 Nil £47 1,416 2,304
The State Fire Insurance Department, therefore, was
doing handsomely, and if its funds had been separately
invested at interest, as they should have been, it would,
by reason of the interest receipts, have done still better.
Also, if the premiums paid to it had been as large as those
charged by private offices —wdiich, apparently, they
weren’t—it would have done better still. It had no divi-
dends to pay, so its funds were likely to accumulate faster
—if it were given fair play—than those of any private in-
stitution. Certainly it had a rather concentrated risk of
£750,000, but its risk, in proportion to the resources be-
hind the institution, was smaller than that of any private
office. It was probably the only fire institution in the
world, bar the Maoriland State Office, whose callable
capital was 400 times larger than the whole amount of its
insurances. And it has already saved the State about
£BOO in expenses, for out of £2351 paid to private offices
fully £BOO would go in inspection charges, directors’fees,
office expenses, advertising and sundries.
The reason given for shutting up the business
and insuring with private institutions is that the reserve
fund isn’t at all commensurate with the amount of risk.
Who expected it would be after only 18 months? What
private office, after only 18 months’existence, has reserves
at all commensurate with the amount at risk ? Of course,
if the State department made a heavy loss in its early
days it would have to do what private offices do, and pay
it out of its capital—that is, in this case, out of the public
funds. Then it would have to trust, as private offices
have to do, to the law of. averages to enable it
to make good the loss, and, after that, build up
new reserves. Possibly the N.S.W. Government,
when it gives the business to private offices, will
offer a share to the Colonial Mutual —now a splendid
institution, with £lOO,OOO paid-up capital (£62,500 of this
wasn’t paid by the shareholders but consists of profits
transferred to capital account) and £71,127 of reserves.
Or rather it would be more accurate to say that the
Colonial Mutual has £37,500 of capital and £133,627 of
reserves, seeing that so much of the capital is really
reserves. Yet some 14 years ago this co. was in a condi-
tion some hundreds of times worse than the N.S.W. State
Fire Insurance Department. Instead of having reserves
commensurate with its risks it had no reserves at all, and
out of its capital of £37,500 it had lost £19,911. It was
carrying an immense business —a business about forty
times the size of that done by the State office which
Carruthers is closing up—with only about £17,589 of
total assets, backed by whatever chance it might have
to collect £62,500 in calls from its shareholders on an
emergency. On Carruthers’ principles it should have
closed up long before things became as bad as this, instead
of going ahead and becoming about the most successful
office in Australia. Premier Carruthers has two probable
motives in shutting up an enterprise that has begun so
well. One is to cast discredit on State enterprise, even if
private enterprise costs the State more, in which case he
is only following out the policy which he adopted in the
locomotive-building'matter. The other is a desire to grab
the insurance fund of £2304 for his hungry Treasury.
The plucky and persistent Sandford, of Lith-
gow, was the only tenderer for the next seven years’ iron
and steel supplies of N.S.W. Government; average prices,
though a trifle higher than those now ruling, work out
lower than those of the past seven years. But even with
a seven years’contract, Sandford, Ltd., won’t undertake
to tender for the whole of the requirements ; it wants to
be assured of the Australian market first, and that can
only be done through a Protective tariff. There is not a
great deal of money in the mere manufacture of pig iron
and unwrought steel; the working up of these raw pro-
ducts into manufactured goods, of which the Government
takes hardly any, would give far more occupation to
both labor and capital, and only high Protection can
secure this work for Australia. The supply of private
people—not merely of the State—must be the main sup-
port of an iron and steel industry. By the way, the crowd
which talks so much of the evils of centralising so vast a
proportion of the population—the “decentralisation”
push of which John Haynes used to be the self-appointed
prophet—ought to be backing up the iron industry
strongly ; yet, strangely enough, it is mostly on the other
side. A great iron industry would, in time, make Lithgow
a rival of Sydney. Nothing attracts population away
from one bloated city like the supply of a liberal amount
of work elsewhere. While Australia is mainly an import-
ing country as regards manufactures, and an exporter of
raw materials, each State will have a bloated seaport
where the imports and exports congregate.
* « » *
History of the Sydney Ferries Co. is interest-
ing. The North Shore Ferry Co. was floated in 1878 with
a cash capital of £20,000 subscribed. In March, 1885,
10,000 new £1 shares were issued at a premium of ss.
£30,000. In March, 1889, 10,000 new shares were allotted
to shareholders, “owing to improved value in freehold
property, etc.” —£40,000. In March, 1894, 20,000 new
shares were allotted as “proceeds of sale of wharf, etc.,”
making- the total capital of the North Shore Perrv Co.
£60,000. When the North Shore Perry Co. became the
Sydney Perries Co. in December, 1899, the old share-
holders received two fully-paid £1 shares for
one in the old eo., making the new co.’s capi-
tal £120,000. In April, 1902, 20,000 shares were
issued at par, £20,000 ; so the present capital of the
Sydney Ferries is 140,000 shares of £l, fully paid. Total
value of Sydney Perries stock (on day when this was
written), at buyers’ price of 565. per share, is £392,000;
at sellers’ price of 60s. per share it is £420,000. The
actual cash paid in the case of the North Shore Ferry Co.
was £30,000 and a premium of ss. on 10,000 shares—£2soo;
and in the case of the Sydney Perries, £20,000. So that
th e total of cash actually paid by shareholders was £52,500,
and the present valuation of the stock represents eight
times the amount of cash originally invested. The
difference between cash invested and investment value
represents an unearned increment due to the develop-
ment of North Sydney. N.S.W. Government built an
expensive railway line from Milson’s Point to Hornsby,
along which thousands of residences are clustered, arid
which brings thousands of people to Circular Quay every
day. On the other side, N.S.W. Government railways
land passengers at Redfern to be conveyed by Govern-
ment trams to Circular Quay, where the Sydney Perries
Co. takes them on board and lands them at- Milson’s
Point. Between the two Government systems there is
thus a mile of water-carriage in the hands of a private
monopoly, which the State feeds every hour of the day.
As Sydney is apparently going to wait twenty years
longer for the North Shore bridge, the profit of the ferry
service should be taken by the State railway systems that
feed it. The Railway Commissioners can presumably run
a ferry as well as they run a train or a tram. Then why
don’t they ?
Further, it is absurd to say that the North
Shore private ferry service is so excellently managed that
the State couldn’t possibly do it as well. Thousands of
passengers are still landed by side-planks, to meet on the
wharf other thousands collected to go on board by the
same side-planks. In New York the passenger ferries are
served by double-ended boats, similar to the present
Sydney horse-ferry. The boat runs right up on to the
pontoon, a barrier falls, and the passengers walk ashore
30 or 40 abreast. Those are the boats wanted on the Mil-
son’s Point service. Further, the turnstile system is
antiquated. It is ludicrous to see a Saturday-night crowd
from North Shore jamming through the Circular Quay
turnstiles one by one. If the tram system can be run by
tickets, then the ferry system can also. And there are
minor grievances.

The position has been somewhat changed during
the year. The £2,000,000 of State-guaranteed stock
was reduced in July, 1904, to £1,000,000; the
State also became a regular shareholder, holding
£500,000 in preference shares. These movements
explain the changes in the columns, “ Interest ” and
“Dividends.” The capital is now £1,000,000 4 p.c.
guaranteed stock, £500,000 in pref. shares, and £452,370
in ordinary shares. Pref. shares (the State’s) receive a
first 5 p.c. ; after that, 1 p.c. rise for every 2 p.c. over 5
p.c. that ordinary shares get, but stopping at 10 p.c.
When ordinary shares get 9 p.c. the prefs. thus get 7 p.c.;
but even if the ordinaries get, say, 20 p.c., the prefs. can-
not go beyond 10 p.c. The £1,000,000 guaranteed stock
falls due in 1914. The ordinary shares (136,217) are paid
to £3 6s. Bd., and are liable for another £3 6s. Bd.

Into this concern the wreckage was cast after the “ right-
thinking people ” had borrowed, b oomed and burst. The
realisation of the old, shady-looking assets has been
helped probably just as much as the new business by the
remarkable prosperity which has followed the reversal of
the old order of politics, and, between the two successes,
there is little of the old ruin left. The current year’s
banking profit will provide more than enough to cover the
whole anticipated loss on the realisation of properties
still in hand. Then, no doubt, with a steady increase of
the reserves (though the £1,000,000 of guaranteed stock
falling due in 1914 must be kept in view), dividends will
move up. Down to the present, with losses from realisa-
tions still calling for help, this is how the combined busi-
ness has shown up :

Financial News (London) July 15, 1904,
writes with regard to the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society Limited : —“ It is especially creditable to the
management that a steady rate of progress has been
maintained during the last few years, when general con-
ditions in all the company’s fields of operations have been
unfavorable.”*

The Waihi Mine management (M.L.), is making
extensive additions to the milling plant in order to be
in a position to deal more effectively with the sulphide
ores which will for the future be the product from their
lower levels. Concentration will be a special feature in
the treatment, and the company accordingly, before
deciding upon the type of concentrator to be adopted,
made exhaustive tests of the different machines pro-
curable in the market and finally decided to adopt the
Wilfley Concentrator, and have placed an order for 25
tables with the Austral Otis Engineering Company, South
Melbourne, sole manufacturers for Australasia. This
firm has just completed and shipped the machines to the

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

tion on the field.*
*' * * *
The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*
“Nothing Succeeds Like Success.” The
Hornsby “Upright” Water Tube Boiler continues its
successful career. Mail advice reports that the Cargo Fleet
Iron Coy. of Middlesbro, after having experience of this
boiler for some time, have now placed a contract with
Messrs. R. Hornsby and Sons, Ltd., for 10 more—alto-
gether 5500h.p. The Strand and Charing Cross Electric
Light Corporation of London, after similar experience,
have installed and on order over 20,000h.p., while the
Municipal Council of Finchley, London, have similarly
placed repeat orders for large installations. In Austra-
lasia, besides the 2500h.p. now being installed for the
Mount Morgan Gold Mining Coy., Ltd., further orders
have been received from both Queensland and W.A.
mines, and for factory work. Messrs. Wm. Arno'tt, Ltd.,
the well-known biscuit manufacturers of Sydney, and
Messrs. W. T. Murray and Co., Ltd., of Invercargill, N.Z.,
are also putting down “ Hornsby” Boiler Plant of 300 h.p.
and IOOOh.p. respectively. R. Hornsby and Sons, Ltd.,
Hay-street, Sydney, and Spencer-street, Melbourne.*

* * * *
East Mexican, Towers, seems by its half-yearly
report to be getting its eye in. Earnings for six months,
£759 ; expenses, £733 ;• profit, £26. Calls (three of 3d. each)
have been made and placed to the credit of the co. for
future working. Tributors are fulfilling labor conditions
at present, and the Brilliant Block hauls the stone. Co.
is content to wait for Mills United shoot of stone to reach
its lease ; which is wise, considering that the School Re-
serve has first call, and East Mexican may on.'y get a
corner. Says the report: “If Mills’ shoot extends
upwards, most of it will eventually come into our
ground.” Exactly—“if.” Anyway, the co. wants a new
straight shaft to start with ; the shaft it has in view to
work from is 850 ft. deep, whereas the reef at the shal-
lowest point seen yet in Mills’ is 1400 ft., and considerably
over 1000 ft. from the 850 ft. shaft. Seems feasible to
work through the School Reserve shaft. It is big enough
to haul all the stone co. is likely to get for months yet.

Net Profit .. £1,149,181
Total .. £1,149,1 SI
Loss on Sales .. £322,483
Written Off .. 389,240
Dividends .. .. 111,445
Deficit wiped out 30,049
Reserves built up 295,364
Total .. £1,149,181
The people who undertook the job of clearing-up were
faced with assets having a book value of £2,731,706 ; and
a loss of fully half of this was predicted. The deficit on
this property account has, however, worked out like this:
Ascertained Loss provided for .. .. £322,483
Book Values written down .. .. 139,988
Further Losses estimated .. .. 158,882
Total £621,353
The other writings-off shown above have been in respect
of debenture conversion account, bank property, Colonial
Bank goodwill, &c.
But though only a further loss of £158,882 is
expected, the assets finally and fully disposed of as yet
amount to only £1,056,569 of the original total of
£2,731,706. Apparently the £139,988 written off is against
the balance of £1,675,137 which remains to be finally dis-
posed of. Of these latter, properties partially realised
have brought in £629,728. Reducing the £1,675,137 by
£139,988, being the amount written off, we get
£1,535,149 as the amount at which the properties
not fully realised still stand in the books ; and
it is expected that the loss will be £158,882.
The ascertained loss on £1,056,569 book value has been
£322,492 ; the loss on £1,675,137, either already provided
for or for which it is thought provision is necessary, is
£298,870. Presumably the Assets Board has sufficient
data on which to base the estimate that only £158,882 in
addition to the £139,988 already provided will be neces-
sary ; if so, and the estimate works out all right, it only
shows what a marvellous recovery values have made
under Maoriland’s progressive policy of the past 10 years.

According to the chairman’s statement, £3262 was written
off “ bad debts and hotel accounts ” during the year ; but
the published accounts do not show these transactions.
Nor (apart from the chairman’s statement) are there any
means of ascertaining how much more has to be written
off hotel account; the managing director says he thinks
“ finality has been reached.” The bad debts account is,
of course, more or less of a permanency. Movements in
detail have been as follows :
1898 1903 1904 1905
Capital .. .. £150,000 £77,037 £77,091 £77,106
Revenue .. .. 28,274 30,096 25,208 22,755
Expenses .. .. 22,308 25,597 20,897 21,772
Debt to Public .. 133,314 68,564 64,862 63,727
Premises, Plant, &c. 140,709 88,525 88,184 90,278
Hotels .. .. 67,846 16,500 16,500 16,500
Stocks & Debtors.. 83,351 56,476 63,729- 50,781
It comes to this, then : since 1898 the co. ’s assets have been
reduced by £134,347, and its liabilities to the public by
£69,587. It has been necessary for the co. to reduce its
assets by £134,347 —though £17,106 has been called up
from shareholders and only three dividends have been
paid out of current profits—in order to reduce the debt by
£69,587. It shows the depth of the bog into Which
Queensland’s old policy of borrow and boom landed this
co. What the true trading profit at present is it is im-
possible to say, because it is not known how much is still
being thrown into the bog and charged to expenses ; but,
assuming that the profit is £5500 a year, that would mean
a return of a little over 3J per cent, on the whole assets,
including hotels, stocks and outstanding debts ; at £4OOO
the return is only 2£ per cent. Either, then, the real
trading profit is a good deal more than £4OOO, or some of
the assets are still overvalued. Which it is there is no
evidence to show. All that can be said for sure is that,
if there was not a large undisclosed writing-down, then
the co. can’t afford to pay 5 per cent, on the basis of last
year’s operations.

Rockhampton Gas and Coke Co. (Q.)
Dividends and Written
Profits. Dividend Tax. Off. Deficit.
June, 1903.. £949
Dec., 1903.. £1614 P o rd'} = £7l3 £747 795
June, 1904.. 1501 9 p.c. pref. „ 116 708 116
Dec., 1904.. 1666 {^c.OTd!} ” 719 831 “
June, 1905.. 1237 Nil 1237
Lighting co.s’ published accounts are in most cases sus-
picions, and the record of this co. shows why. It went
on paying large divs. for years, neglecting to write
off anything adequate for depreciation and provide for the
inevitably necessary renewals, until it found itself at a
standstill, with the auditors refusing to certify to the
balance-sheet. Dividends have therefore to be now hung
up -while the capital that was distributed in dividends
is restored. And a reduction in the price of gas
has been forced on it, cutting down its profits and
prolonging the agony. Even now, but before the writing-
off now provided for, electric light plant, mains and
fittings stand in at £11,241, while the gross revenue
from this branch has amounted in 12 months to
only £714, and the actual loss on working account has
been £ll3. In addition to its own capital the co. carries
£22,000 of debenture debt, which doesn’t lessen its diffi-
culties. The one thing in its favor is that it has recog-
nised the size of its troubles, and drops the dividend
altogether. The directors now recommend that the
electric light works be closed. Works that are not worth
running are not worth keeping in the balance-sheet (ex-
cept at value for removal) ; and in that case the co. had
better do the honest thing- and write off what it has lost—
or, rather, what it has pocketed as divs. that weren’t
earned.
* * * *
As far as it has gone, Coppabella (N.S.W.) has
given much more work to sharebrokers than to miners.
The market for a long while had had nothing in particular
to play with, and Coppabella turned up the very thing
wanted. Its distance from everywhere was in its favor.
The man with the syndicate prospectus in one hand and
the silver slug in the other could talk of a 40ft. lode, and
the bull could talk of “ another Broken Hill,” and the
tape and the assayer’s pot could not knock out the yarns
in an hour or two. “The distant field looks greenest ”
needs re-writing when it comes to mining ; it is the dis-
tant mine that finds the public greenest. Also it hap-
pened that just when the brokers began to play with
Coppabella, the Broken Hill Proprietary was looking back
over its 20 years of silver-mining- and its seven millions of
dividends ; and the papers were recalling the millions
made by the men who got in first; and so between
one thing and another Coppabella played right into
the gamblers’ hands. A small co. of 3000 shares
of £2 each, 2500 issued, was formed in Melbourne, with
Henry Gore as chairman, and prices jumped in a few
hours from £3 to £l6, then on to £26 ; within almost as
few hours they were back to £7, and now joggle between
that and £5 10s. If H. J. Daly helped to knock them out
with an adverse report, Bewick, Moreing propped them
up with a report by Hertzig, on which the firm bought
either 200 or 500 shares—nobody seems to know for
certain which.
Then the public got the idea that there was
another silver rush to Coppabella. It was reported that
120 leases had been applied for, and that the country was
pegged for miles. That sounded like business. But it
was utterly misleading. Coppabella is on a portion of the
Upper Murray, which, being moderately fertile and—for
Australia—unusually well watered, has” been practically
all made private land. It is not all freehold, certainly' •
but under the N.S.W. Mining on Private Property Act
conditional purchases and conditional leases rank as pri-
vate lands. Now, a man hag only to hold a miner’s right
(which costs 2s. 6d.) to apply for as many permits
to search as he pleases. He simply sends the Warden
an application for, say, 40 acres in such-and-such paddock,
and in turn the Warden gives notice to attend and show
him the land he wants. For the one half-crown he can
apply for an unlimited number of blocks without ever
having seen one of them. This is the speculator’s chance
to get a big “possible” for nothing. He whips in his
application, and waits. Presently comes a prospector
who has been wandering over the country without
authority, and lodges an application for something he has
actually found. If he breathes a word about his find the
speculator who has got in first can point the Warden to
that very block as the one he wants. There is no pegging
out ; and the application does not define the area required,
the presumption being that the man hasn’t yet any right
to go on the land, and doesn’t know what is there. This,
then, is the whole explanation of the crowd of applica-
tions. The alleged rush is a pure myth. In the 30 miles be-
tween the field and Germanton, at the head of the railway,
the writer last week passed only three men, and he would
probably have passed two of them anyway ; and the whole
mining population of the field is well under 60. And this
is what a boom has been built upon !
There is another side to this matter, certainly.
With speculators’ applications in and undealt with, the
bona-fide- prospector may be scared off applying for a show
he knows of. He will wait till the other man has first
indicated his own block. Then he may come in. It is
not certain that anything of the kind is happening, but it
is possible. Anyhow, here is a large hole in the law.
Warden Gibson is the first man who has had to deal with
anything approaching a rush of applications under the
Mining on Private Property Act, and it says much for
him that one hears no complaints at all on the field. He
had the advantage of serving during the Barrier’s lively
days as a Mining Registrar, and his experience then has
come handy now under very different circumstances.
In spite of the 140 applications now in there are
only four blocks which show any ore worth mentioning ;
a few others travel on a slug of galena, or a blotch of lead
in quartz, or a little very low-grade lead stuff, such as
may be found at a thousand places in Australia. The
four that alone are worth any consideration because of the
metal they show are Coppabella Prop., Coppabella Blocks
1 and 2, and Mt. Gifford. Of these the only one that has
made any attempt to see what is under the surface is the
Coppabella Prop. This co. has driven a tunnel along the
lode, about 60ft. into Coppabella hill, which rises sharply
nearly 700 ft. The tunnel hugs the footwall, which is
fairly well defined. The face is now about 40ft. below the
surface of the hill at that point, and the lode there looks
better than anywhere else in the tunnel. But it is not
likely that this improvement will continue. The ore
probably occurs in shoots, dipping into the hill, and the
face is in one of these shoots. One judges so from the
fact that, on the surface, just about this point, some
scratching exposes much cleaner ore than is found either
north or south. From the tunnel, which is the southern-
most point where the vein has been seen, to the furthest
point north where galena has been found in the irregular
outcrop, is 325 ft.—surface measurement. Horizontally
this is about 225 ft. That is the proved length of lode. It
has not been picked up either north (towards the brow of
the hill), or to the south (where, however, it may pos-
sibly be hidden by alluvial). North, over the rocky hill,
there is a large number of outcropping masses, mostly
quartz, some of them carrying occasional splashes of lead.
In one of the creeks there is a good deal of tourmaline
(valueless) ; another outcrop, or “ spew,” showed zinc.
The country is much disturbed and faulted ; prospecting
is difficult, and the persistence of a regular lode unlikely.
Coppabella Prop, ore is a rather fine-grained
galena, running up to 220 z. galena and anything at all
for lead—you could get slugs going 80 per cent. But a
slug isn’t a lode. So far you might hand-pick one ton of
ore out of 10 of lode. The gangue is largely quartz ;if
there were enough of it there should be no difficulty in
concentrating it. Whether it will be worth concentrating
depends mainly upon whether the ore-shoots become
better defined. The fact that what seems to be a fairly
clean shoot runs down from the surface to the present
face (or what was the face a week ago) is encouraging.
What the size of the shoots may be it is impossible to say.
The tunnel has gone along the footwall, and the width of
the vein has not been proved at even one point. But
there is nothing whatever to suggest a great mine. A
medium-grade, concentrating proposition, which would
yield moderate profits with metals at a good price, is the
most that one can reasonably look for. It is quite unlike
the Broken Hill mines—much more like those at Thacka-
ringa (N.S. W.) or at Zeehan (Tas.). The vein is, however,
apparently wider than those at Thackaringa—the first
place, by the way, worked on the Barrier, and now em-
ploying a mere handful of men.
Around the Coppabella Prop, are the South,
Central, Queen, and other blocks. The Coppabella vein
runs about north-east and south-west, and if it went on
like that far enough it would go into both North and
South. But lodes always stop somewhere, and the
Coppabella country is disturbed enough to suggest that
these may stop pretty suddenly. At any rate, they are
not likely to follow a bee-line, even to suit a mining syn-
dicate. Coppabella South has put two scratches in its
alluvial, and found nothing. Coppabella North, which is
in rough country, has done nothing but chip corners off a
few boulders. The chipping has proved nothing more
than the scratching. Coppabella Central is apparently
trusting co the underlay from the Prop. It is a fearfully
remote thing to hang even a syndicate upon.
Two miles east are the Coppabella Blocks, C.
H. Kerry’s flotations, No. 1 held in Sydney, No. 2 in Mel-
bourne. These are in comparatively flat country, and
costeens have exposed a very mixed gossany formation,
carrying some carbonates. It is rather nice-looking stuff,
w'ha't there is of it, but the size of the formation is abso-
lute conjecture. It is certain, too, that some of what has
been regarded as lead carbonates is not that at all, but
possibly pyromorp&te or chlorite. Still, undoubted car-
bonate crystals can be small quantities, yet
enough to make it well worth while prospecting. A
prospecting shaft is to be put down as far as w'ater will
allow; then they will crosscut the lode, or where the
lode, if there is one, ought to be. Till then it is a gamble.
In the rough ranges above the Coppabella
Blocks is the Coppabella Boulder, marked by a pile of
quartz boulders, carrying pyrites, some parched iron-
stone in small vughs, and said to assay 2dwt. in spots.
Nothing at all is visible here to hang a hope on. This
show is held by a Sydney syndicate.
Six miles from the Boulder, on the other side, of
Coppabella, is Mt. Gifford, said to be under option with
an ultimate view' to English flotation. There is a thin
seam of galena on what may be the footwall of a lode.
The most that can be said is that it is worth sinking a
few pounds to test this.
In favor of the field is abundance of timber and
water. Coppabella Creek, a permanent stream, though
reduced to a trickle in the summer, is within about a mile
of the Prop. The Creek has no great fall —about 40ft. in
two miles —and not enough to get the water on to the
mine. Ore would therefore have to be sent to a site about
],! miles away. Surveys may, however, show' that, though
a W'ater supply cannot be got on the mine from Coppabella
Creek, it may be got from some other. All this means
money ; and on present appearances, and anticipating
favorable developments, the Cop. Prop, will have to be
worked cheaply to pay. Distance from market will be
quite suffic’ent handicap. Road cartage from the mine
to the head of the raihvay is not likely to fall under 255.
per ton ; and the head of the railway (Germanton)
is 378 miles to Sydney and 238 to Melbourne ; but latter
journey calls for transhipment. For comparison, from
Broken Hill to Port Pirie is 280 miles without tranship-
ment, and with no road carriage.
Present Coppabella Prop. co. has very little
money—only about £lOOO. There are, however, 500 re-
served shares, under option to Goodall, of Melbourne
Exchange. Shortage of funds, no doubt, accounts for the
way the show is being worked. The tunnel along the lode
i* doing very little to test the property. The few pounds
the co. has is being spent in window-dressing the mine,
with a view of getting in more money. The one
permanent work in hand is a main shaft, W’ell up from
the tunnel, but this has only just been begun.
# * * •
Broken Hill South and B.H. Prop, are running
almost neek-and-neck in the matter of profit per ton of
ore ; B. H. Prop, showed 9s. lid. for past half-year, South
9s. lOjd. South, however, charged nearly £5OOO spent on
new main shaft to capital account, while B.H. Prop, paid
for all such works out of revenue. A while ago there was
very keen demand for Barrier lead concentrates, and co.’s
were able to make such good terms that it paid better for
the moment to export than to smelt in Australia. Things
have changed now. Compared with first half of 1904,
South should have made about £25,000 increased profit
in past six months, taking the average prices of metals as
a basis ; as a matter of fact, it was only just half that
much to the good. The Australian mining co. that has
to depend on the foreigner for a market for its raw pro-
duct puts itself in a tiger’s mouth. It may be the most
benevolent tiger that ever wore a stripe ; but it will get
hungry some day.
* * * *
Broken Hill Prop., in the past year, has some-
how improved the value of its ore 2s. 3d. per ton ; either
the ore is that much richer or the extraction is that much
better, or there is a bit of both. Against that there is an
increase in costs of about 7d. per ton, leaving a net gain
of about Is. Bd. per ton—the results of 12 months’ experi-
menting and additions to plant, and possibly (though im-
probably) slightly improved grade of ore. On a 300,000
ton per half-year output, that is worth £227500. Apart
from the improvement in the metal markets, that is the
half-yearly worth of 12 months’ progress, though most of
that progress was made in the first half. In the second
half, gains in extraction were about counterbalanced by
heavier costs.
* * * *
Broken Hill, which went nap on Junction
North, and dropped a pot of money, is getting rid of
the idea that the property is being worked in such a way
as to squeeze out local people. They are making up their
minds now that what is really amiss is the milling plant,
and that it couldn’t do better work if it tried. Co. is
making a small profit, but it is doing it in spite of the
poorest plant on the line—a plant utterly unworthy of
the ore developments and prospects.
* * •* *
Wyalong (N.S.W.) is beginning to bump hard
against the sulphide trouble. At the chief properties the
oxidised zone is practically worked out, and, though the
sulphides are rich in places, the cost of sinking in the hard
country, added to influx of Water, means a big outlay.
Pew of the properties have any cash reserves ; none of
them are in the hands of registered co.’s—a striking fact,
considering the quantity of gold (about 250,000 oz.) the
field has sent out in 10 years. It is certain, however, that
the scrip system will come soon. So far as the field
knows, one man, E. E. Tyler, is owner of the True Blue,
which employs 100 men and is about the best thing on
Wyalong. J. Channon is principal owner of the Junction.
Both of these men own other properties, which, so far,
have simply cost them money ; and it’s the same with
most other owners. The chances are, therefore, that
they couldn’t face any very serious struggle in their main
properties—out of profits put by, anyway.
**- * *
Great Boulder, Kalgoorlie, continues to open up
well at 1900 ft. Up to now 180 ft. have been driven north,
and last 901 t. averages 30dwt. for 10ft. wide ; lode ranges
from 7to 12ft., and from 17 to 47dwt. This is the best
development Boulder has had since No. 12 level. Hamilton
shaft, at extreme north end of property, is now equipped,
and sinking has been resumed. Prospecting at 800 and
1000 ft. from this shaft has proved a ghastly failure, and
it is unlikely that any more will be done till 1200 ft. is
reached. A drill bore" cut ore, said to be 10ft. wide and
worth 25dwt., 280 ft. north of Ivanhoe boundary, and
Hamilton shaft was sunk to work this. If real work
endorses this find, value of property will be vastly
increased, as this shaft is situated about 1600 ft. north of
main shaft and will practically open up a new mine.
« * * *
Great Ophir, Davyhurst (W.A.), originally in
60,000 shares of a nominal 10s. each, has been converted
into 300,000 shares of ss. each —170,000 paids and 130,000
-contribs. Old shareholders are entitled, on application,
to 120,000 paids gratis. One paid is also offered to every
subscriber of two cons., up to 100,000, payable Is. on appli-
cation and Is. on allotment. The remaining 30,000 cons,
will be held in reserve. Scheme deserves the fate it is
likely to get from the public. It is ridiculous capitalising
in 300,000 shares a mine which has so far proved itself a
near relation of a wild-cat, which has no ore opened up,
and which has a plant that has only proved itself a failure.
Co.’s controllers have, however, purchased defunct Lady
Loch’s battery and surface equipment for a song.
* * -k m
Recently Westralia Mt. Morgans directors com-
missioned Nicholson, manager of Ivanhoe, to report on
mine, and propound a policy. Nicholson reported that
there were only 34,800 tons of B’sdwt. ore in sight, and
advised closing down half the mill, and raising fresh capi-
tal to push on developments and thoroughly open up the
mine. But A. E. Morgans vetoes this sane policy. He
argues that ore is so patchy it is practically useless trying
to select it, and the only way to work the* mine is to put
everything through and trust' to chance; also that
shutting down half the mill would materially raise costs.
As Morgans prevails, his policy will be pursued, but with
disastrous results. Sons of Gwalia was run to a standstill
by pursuing a like policy, and revived on an army of
samplers being- installed to sample every face worked.

Northern Blocks Syndicate (W.A.) has exer-
cised option on Great Leviathan, Southern Cross. Price
paid was £BOOO, in addition to £l2OO spent on develop-
ments during option. Mine is on a small scale, so far as
proved, but manager Roberts estimates that profits in
sight will recoup all outlay, including original cost, and
there is a chanpe beyond that. Ten head of stamps are to
be transferred from the Associated, and should be run-
ning by end of year. Worse feature is that there are
700,000 shares; Associated Northern CoV holds one-half.
Ivanhoe South Extended, Kalgoorlie, of which
very little has been heard lately, has nearly completed its
new surface equipment, and will resume sinking shortly.
Main shaft is down 1300 ft., and has to go another 500 or
600 ft. to get within reach of the lode cut by drill some
time ago. As drills have a bad habit of deflecting at all
sorts of angles, it is doubtful whether the lode is not in
Ivanhoe ground.
• * * *
North Kalgurli (W.A.) seems to have very little
hope on its eastern side, as the shoots in the Kalgurli are
all short and cut off well inside Kalgurli boundary 7 . A
crosscut is in progress at 1000 ft. to try and get the con-
tinuation of the Hainault lodes on the west. Some time
ago a lode 4ft. wide and worth Bdwt. was located by boring
238 ft. west of the shaft, and this should be reached before
long. This is the concern’s forlorn hope. Co. is still
treating slimes, but they- are nearly 7 done.
* * * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W. A.), is still a good
sort of property to leave alone. During past six months
it has been worked at a loss of £728, and since half-year
closed things have been going worse, as ore is down to
2dwt. Many 7 of the levels have caved-in for want of tim-
ber, and outlook is utterly hopeless. Co.’s South Gipps-
land leases also worked at a loss, which was turned into a
profit by treating old tailings. The profit on tailings
panned out at £ll6O ; this; was reduced to £1142 by slight
loss in mining and treating ore. As the grade of ore has
fallen from 23 to 14dwt., and most of the stopes are look-
ing poor, outlook is anything but rosy 7 .
■* * * *
Brown Hill Extended, Kalgoorlie, is making
splendid progress with its new main shaft, from which the
east reef is to be worked. This shaft was only started in
June, and is already down over 250 ft. It is to be
taken down to 700 or 800 ft. before any opening out on
the reef is done. A test parcel from this reef, of 224
tons, averaged 18dwt., and the ore was broken for a length
of 174 ft. and a width of 3ft. It looks like a mine.
■X * * *
St. Arnaud (Vic.), once an active mining centre,
but for many years practically defunct, with the excep-
tion of that one good mine, the Lord Nelson, is showing
increasing signs of a Rip Van Winkle awakening-. Lady 7
Nelson, though financially weak so far, is looking up a
little, the 1170 loads of stone crushed so far yielding
6050 z. silver-gold bullion, and 10 head of stamps are going
continuously, with another 10 head to be installed shortly.
Wilfley table concentrates sold have realised £207, and at
least twelve months’ stone is estimated to be in sight.
Work has been resumed on the Lord Nelson North,
adjoining the Lord Nelson, and after installing a new
winding plant, it is proposed to sink the shaft several
hundred feet deeper and crosscut for the Shoot now being
worked in the Lord Nelson, which shoot is generally held
to be dipping right into the North ground. Besides
these, the Lady Nelson Extended has commenced opera-
tions at repairing a good three-chamber shaft on Walker’s
lease, preparatory to driving towards what was in former
years one of the richest bits of ground, down to the
water-level, in St. Arnaud. The flotation of the Lord
Nelson Extended is also announced. The local populace
are beginning to scorn lethargic utterances anent fat pigs
and pumpkins, and are learning to talk “ scrip,” “ clean-
ups,” etc., with surprising energy.
* * * *
_T. J. Greenway leaves Chillagoe, and Paul C.
Kunze, lately managing the Zeehan smelters, and before
that of the Australian (German) Metal Co., takes charge.
Greenway’s optimism has been oozing out for a long
while, and the ghastly failure of the last reconstruction
scheme about tapped all that was left of it. And, any-
way, whatever may happen afterwards, Chillagoe co. will
have no use for an expensive manager for some time to
come.
* * * *
Chillagoe smelters depend considerably on Mun-
gana ore ; a return from 1761 tons of the latter gave 185
tons lead and 18,9250 z. silver (102 oz. per ton of ore).
Mungana has about 11,000 tons in the roasting heaps and
otherwise stacked around the mine ; but aforesaid return
was from easily-treated silicious ore, obtained at shallow
levels ; so it is no guide to contents of heaps. Chillagoe
is to remodel its lead furnaces on lines adopted by B.H.
Prop, and the Zeehan works. Co. seems to have missed
its best chance (assuming it had it) by not acquiring the
O.K. A co. that lias railway and works, and abundance
of fluxing material and not too much ore, should keep its
eyes open for new mines. But Chillagoe is too dazed to
see anything very clearly.

Kelly’s Queen, Towers, has made a discovery.
For months co. has been working a formation, in hopes of
locating the Phoebe reef, and, in despair, a survey was
ordered. Stated now that the Phoebe reef is 30ft. lower
down. The two formations were not similar at any
time. Phoebe’s is kindly, while that in Kelly’s looks as
cruel as the management’s action in spending two 3d.
calls 30ft. up in the hanging-wall country before a survey
was made.

“Den”: Housewives in S.A. are being
scared out of their wits by apparitions of glowing
chops and fiery beefsteaks that shine in the
kitchen what time the marauding cockroach
seeketh his prey. The brief slavey at our house
came screaming from the kitchen the other night,
vowing that the beef-bone for the morrow’s hash
was glowing with a supernatural radiance. I
investigated, and true enough the dinner’s
remnant was decorated with a distinct halo. The
butcher was consulted, but he offered no explana-
tion. Quite fresh, he said, and no real harm in the
illumination. “Ought to see his shop o’ night time.
It was like the fireworks for the Dook.” A
medical friend puts forward the theory that the
lavish use of super-phosphates on grazing land is
responsible. The phosphorus, he opines, becomes
precipitated in the herbage, is assimilated by the
down-trodden sheep and low-spirited bullock,
and manifests itself when these animals become
mutton and beef respectively. But our slavey
suspects the Evil One, and recommends a priest.
• * * *
Melbourne City Health Officer (Dr. Jamie-
son), in a lecture on consumption before the Vic.
Health Society, said that in 15 years the annual
deaths within the city boundaries had decreased
by 380. “ The improved sanitary conditions
brought about by the sewerage system are respon-
sible for a great part of the improvement,” com-
mented the doctor, “but we must not overlook
the Legislature which has done such excellent
work in securing improved conditions of labor
under the various Shops and Factories Acts.”
These are the same Acts, be it remembered, which
all Victorian Toryism united to block. Improved
conditions mean expenditure, and human beings
are the only commodities in the world which, in
the Tory theory, cost nothing and are not missed
when they are dead.

“ J.H. ” : What’s the moral obli-
gation of a man who matri-
monially engages himself 1
Know of one who, in a weak
moment, imagined he had
met his fate in the shape /
of a girl blessed with
everything but brains.
Three weeks afterwards
he found that a pretty
face, which only
opened to eat or drink
or say “How nice ! ”
wasasgood company
as an oleograph.
Just on the point
of escaping his
martyrdom, her
father collapsed
financially, and, to
avoid the long pale
finger of scorn, he
carried out his con-
tract. Know of
another in a similar
difficulty, who is
trying the waiting
game. He has been
engaged for nine
years, and hopes
to wear out the
chain by long wait-
ing. The lady
wears a hungry
look, fit to melt a
Sphinx, but, all the
same, she hangs on
hard.
Up-country in S.
Australia funerals are
made the most of in the
way of excitement. The
death of anybody higher up
in the social scale than a road
laborer is eagerly awaited, and
as soon as the doctor gives his
certificate the undertaker and the
local brass bands and the lodges of
various sorts get ready ; the town’s
vehicles are furbished up, and the citizens’ rusty
black clothes are inked once more. Everybody
turns out to the show, and the procession is
stopped once or twice to be photographed. A
funeral in S. Australia is such a jolly affair as to
suggest that the folk are assembled to congratulate
the corpse on its deadness.

For out-and-out meanness the recent
attempt of the Vic. Co-operative Brewery to sweat
Australian poster artists seemed hard to beat;
but it has been beaten, all the same. Last week
advts. appeared in the Melb. dailies offering a
prize of 10s. for designs for illustrated post-cards
—“ all card-designs to become the property of the
advertisers, and all applications to be accompanied
by three penny stamps !" At first sight, the thing
looked like a swindle engineered by some ingeni-
ous spieler who would keep the stamps and drop
the designs into the river; but at the bottom of
the advt., instead of the expected “ Box ZOOl,
G.P.0.”, appeared the name of the Victorian
Ladies’ Work Association, probably the most
stylish charitable institution in the State, with
Janet Mrs. Clarke at its head, and a membership
roll consisting wholly of National Leaguers.

“ O’Fipp ” : A circulating-library nymph
recently advised me to read ‘ ‘ the latest ” —the auto-
biography of one whom she called “ the celebrated
Colonel Anstruther Thomson.” After struggling
through the two bulky volumes, I am still wonder-
ing what he could have been celebrated for, unless
it was for living eiglity-odd years without any-
thing happening to him. He once saw King Ned,
and the record of the interview is the most exciting
thing in the book. Here it is :
Charlie ran up to me and said, “ The King wants to
speak to you.” I advanced, and there was a stone gutter
just in front of me. The King looked down at it and
said, “ Take care.” He then shook hands and said, “Do
you still go out hunting?” “ Yes, your Majesty.” I
then had a good look at him. I said, “lam so glad to see
your Majesty looking so well.” He smiled, and said,
“Thank you, thank you!” He then talked about the
parade, &c., and bowed. I saluted, and our interview
ended.
Worst of it is that every dull person who looks
at a king seems to think it necessary to announce
the fact in two or three volumes of stodge, which
help to hide the really interesting books !
* * « *
A half-caste aboriginal boy was recently
admitted into the Palmerston (N.T.) Hospital.
Dr. Eulton pronounced it a case of leprosy. There
is no leper settlement in the Territory. The case
was brought before the Board of Health, and after a
long sitting, it was decided to place the youngster
on an island off the coast. At low water he can
walk across to the mainland. A Chow has been
placed in charge. This leper had been living with
a missionary from childhood, and it was not until
one of his fingers dropped off that he was sent to
the hospital. He was cook and general factotum.
Many lepers are known to be at large in the Chow
and blacks’ camps, but no action is taken. In fact,
some thought this boy should be set at liberty
amongst his tribe; others thought smothering a
more suitable mode of deliverance; others again
blame the medico for saying anything about the
affair.

A letter concerning Art in Sydney :
In Sydney, Art is represented by a society which grew
out of the amalgamation of two rival institutions.
The consolidated concern called itself “ Royal,” and then
almost immediately fell under the curse that overtakes
things Australian when they start to flaunt the brand of
the head station. In this country, when anything sud-
denly declares that it is “ Royal,” or makes a great cry
about its devotion to the throne or the empire, the wise
recognise that something has happened. And now a faint
odor of decayed crayfish is making itself apparent on the
premises of the “ Royal Art Society of N.S. W.” First and
foremost, the Society seems to be a sweater ; according to
its balance-sheet, its paid instructor received for his past
year’s services the munificent sum of £92 6s. lOd. It paid
its instructor less than it paid for catering and music
at its entertainments, which ran up to £ll3, and
a society for the encouragement of art, which pays more
for afternoon tea and piano-thumping than it does for
teaching- drawing and painting, is a joke when its
vagaries are perpetrated with its own money, but when
it plays these tricks with the State’s money it ought to be
brought up with a sudden jerk.
The present exhibition is the worst for many years,
and the reason is not hard to And. For some few exhibi-
tions there has been a suspicion that somewhere in the
Council was an inside ring, and the treatment meted out
to the work of pupils who received instruction from
artists other than those in the ring, has told intending
pupils plainly that if you want to get your pictures
exhibited, and your work stamped with the only
hall-mark the Sydney artist can win, it is best
to go to certain teachers, and to them only.
One artist who had successfully attracted the pupils of
several of the Council to his studio, found only one
picture accepted of all that his students had sent in.
Among those students were workers who, while they were
under the tuition of members of the Council, had pictures
hung. It seems strange that only the members of the
inside ring in the Council of the Royal Art Society can
teach properly, and more strange still that to change the
teacher is to lose all the cunning these exquisite teachers
had previously imparted. Look round the exhibition, and
you can count the pictures by the students of men not on
the Council on the finger-tips. And N.S. W. subsidises the
Art Society to the extent of £4OO per annum.
The Art Society holds an annual exhibition, and to have
pictures accepted for that exhibition is the hall-mark of
artistic success in N.S.W. To the lay mind the idea of
allowing men who exhibit pictures of their own, and
whose students are exhibiting, to sit in judgment on the
work of their rivals and the students of their rivals, is
abhorrent. What is wanted is a National School of Art,
run by the State.
* * * *
Will some member introduce a short Bill
into the N.S.W. Assembly providing that, when
it becomes necessary to resume a street or a
locality because the private landlord has allowed
it to become too foul, dilapidated, and unhealthy
to be any longer tolerated, that landlord, instead
of being paid the usual 10 per cent, above the
value of his property for compulsory resumption,
should be paid 20 per cent, below it for being a
public nuisance ? In the case of the Wexford-
street (Sydney) resumptions, the private landlord
will receive nearly £6OOO above the value of his
property as a reward for being dirty, and dirt
doesn’t deserve such recompense.
A Federal public
servant has given Vic-
Premier Bent the bene,
fit of a bright idea.
He says, “Put a tur-
bine at the outlet of
the Yan Yean reser-
voir to generate electri-
city as a motive power
for your new railway,
and erect water wheels
along the stream, and
you will have enough
power to work the rail-
way and light all the
public offices in Mel-
bourne as well, at a
nominal cost.” Though
a trifle sanguine this
genius seems to be on
the right track. Engi-
neer Tbwaites’ scheme
for damming the Yarra
above Heidelberg would
easily supply enough power
to electrically drive all the
suburban railways and light the
city as well, and it has the ad-
vantages of being cleaner and of
consuming nothing. The water
will run away just as at present,
but the millions of horse-power
now drifting to waste will be
utilised. Why men should
rush to dig in the bowels of
the earth for dirty, grimy
coal to generate motive
power, when a cleaner,
cheaper and equally effica-
cious force, with fish in it
and wattle bloom alongside
it, lies at their doors,
passes comprehension.
A cheap-marriage
parson gave evidence in
the Prahran (Vic.) court,
the other day, that he
sometimes married as
many as 300 couples in
three months. No wonder that three and five
guinea men find their registers so thin.

OUR GREAT FUTURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In opening a Sydney exhibition of by-products and of
cattle and sheep, Mr. G. H. Reid said we had a great
country, and should see that it was properly advertised.
He thought there was a great future for Australia, in
spite of its politics and statesmen. “ Nothing,” he added,
“ can prevent Australia from becoming one of the great
powers of the world and the scene of unexampled pros-
perity and happiness.” Continuing, he said that if we
could only get rid of the prejudice against immigration
we would get rid of the greatest stumbling block the
country had in its fight for prosperity. —Daily paper.
Lament no more, O worried few
Who view your country’s state with sorrow :
Just think what Mister Reid may do
To-morrow —or some day’s to-morrow !
True, now he can’t find time to act,
But while his pretty speeches please us
What need to let the ugly fact
That industries are fading, tease us ?
O, cast away all carking cares
That so reduce the fatty tissues,
And leav.e it to your grandson’s heirs
To raise such things as fiscal issues.
Think not how near disaster seems,
With grief anticipating lean times,
But rest in pleasing lotos dreams,
And gaily talk like this between times :
‘ Great country... splendid future... great!
Magnificent... great... fine... great nation ;
Great natural resources. Wait—
Entrancing prospect... immigration.
“ Great destiny of this great land ;
Great principle... great... prospects rising ;
Give colored brothers welcoming hand ;
Great empire... we need advertising.
“ Great evil of town industries ;
Go on the land 1... Freetrade’s perfection ;
Great imports... Each great mind agrees...
Great fallacy found in Protection.
“ Great brains of this great audience ;
Great truths in this great peroration ;
Great gratitude—indeed, immense ;
Give three great cheers... great King... great nation! ’’
If such a wordy web you weave
'Twill cure all ills —although it’s rum how ;
And as you doze you may believe
Things now are sure to go right—somehow !
VIC.
O’FIPP.
