“ Lamore ” : Great is the faith of mankind
in the printed word. A Melbourne news-
paper-man has just related to me the tale
of William Stubbs, sometime A.B. on the
Royal Arthur. The pressman first met
William in the casualty room of the Mel-
bourne Hospital, where William —who
was royally drunk—was having stitches
put in a cut in the head, the result of
hostile movements conducted in Little
Lonsdale-street. William delivered utter
ances which made him worth a para-
graph, and my friend wrote it. Then—
partly because he thought it might help
William’s explanations to his officers,
and partly because it was such an in-
apposite, incongruous lie—he ended up
his par. with the grave statement:
“Stubbs was given by his companions
a high reputation for steadiness and
sobriety.” Two months later, he met
William again, this time in the
watchhouse, clinging to the counter.
He could not let go without falling,
but he appealed pathetically to the
bobby to search his (William’s) pockets,
and find there a proof that he could not
possibly be drunk. “ It’s in the paper,”
he said. The policeman searched,
and found a cutting from the Agegus
containing the par. written at the
hospital. It put so convincing a halo
of virtue round William’s head that
the lockup-keeper promised to allow
him to sleep off his excessive sobriety
and then depart in peace. The police-
man also had a reverence for the
printed word.
Thus “ Two Seas,” from a N.S.W. country
town :
Apropos of that Melbourne art union swindle, worked
with a trick ballot-box. I used to gamble for drinks with
a Crown lands agent per medium of his land ballot-box—
highest marble to win. His phenomenal luck at length
prompted me to devote some study to the mechanism of
the box. One of the results was that, the next time the
marbles were shaken up, I snatched first turn of the
cylinder. I won. It was a very simple trick, too. The
bottom of the box sloped on all sides to a hole in the
centre. The cylinder turned in a sheath under that hole,
and had two cavities bored in it—the size of the marbles.
The agent turned the cylinder until one of the holes was
in line with the hole in the bottom of the ballot-box. The
other cavity in the cylinder would then be flush with the
hole through which the winning marble is dropped into
the tray, so that the agent could, without looking, feel,
while holding the box, when the cylinder was in the right
position for his purpose. He then dropped the winning
marble into the box. It rolled into the hole, and a slight
turn of the cylinder held it fast, ready for dropping into
the tray. The other marbles were afterwards dropped
in, but, turn and shake as much as one wished, nothing
could stop the agent’s marble being first out. Sometimes
he had the highest marble ready set in the cylinder,
sometimes the lowest. In the latter case the victim
could have first turn at the cylinder.
Since that experience I have always looked suspiciously
at the lucky man in a land-ballot, although, of course, no
one could doubt the honesty of any routine connected
with our Lands Administration. Still, the ballot-box
ought to look honest, so that the public would feel at
least as much confidence in it as one feels when “ the
monkey draws the marble ” on a bush racecourse.
* * * *
The Mayor of North Sydney recently
called together the other mayors north of the
harbor to point out the iniquity of the Greater
Sydney scheme—which would wipeout the Mayor
of North Sydney and make the Mayor of Sydney
the one municipal potentate of the whole metro-
politan area. In preference the Mayor of North
Sydney proposed a Greater North Sydney scheme,
which wouldn’t wipe out the Mayor of North
Sydney, but would rather make him the
great municipal potentate of all the northern
suburbs. But the Mayor of Lane Cove and the
Mayor of Manly objected just as much to being
wiped out for the glorification of the Mayor of
North Sydney as for that of the Mayor of Sydney,
and the Mayor of Mosman agreed with them, so
a great quantity of dissatisfied Mayors dispersed,
having done nothing. And until Parliament
passes an Act referring the Greater Sydney ques-
tion to the people, and utterly ignoring the
Mayor of Greater Lane Cove, and the Mayor of
Greater Manly, and the other 38 Mayors, nothing
will be done—nothing, that is, that 39 out of the 40
Mayors, the 39 who would be abolished to make
room for a Greater Sydney—can prevent.
VISIONS, AFTER A SEVERE COURSE OF PENNY NOVELETTES.
About the choicest humor of recent times
is found in newspaper criticisms of the first instal-
ment of pictures and statuary obtained by director
Bernard Hall for Melbourne National Gallery,
under the terms of the Felton bequest. The
Argus, enamored of the good old days when
£1700 used to be given for one such picture as
John Rogers Herbert’s “Moses Bringing Down
the Tables of tbe Law ” (which would probably
bring about 30s. at auction), and when that horrid
pub. transparency, Herkomer’s Queen Victoria,
was bought at a fabulous figure, pooh-poohs the
new pictures. After splashing the color work
with all the damnatory terms it can find in
“ Everybody’s Rapid Guide to Art,” the Argus
comes to the black-and-white, which it regards as
“ impossible to treat quite seriously,” although it
is somewhat taken with Fred Pegram’s “ clever
Grosspatching.” (Crosspatching ! Who can take
the Argus’ art seriously after that ?) The works
that are almost beneath the notice of this
authority include four of Vierge’s famous illustra-
tions to “Pablo de Segovia,” two remarkable
drawings by Edmund Sullivan, drawings by
Charles Keene, Pegram, and Towmsend, and one
of the celebrated wood-engravings after Frederick
Sandys.

A pathetic superfluity of Girl in some parts
of Victoria is revealed by a policeman’s letters in
a breach-of-promise case. From Ballarat he wrote
to his Dear Beattie that the soft sex appeared to
be six times more numerous than the hard sex.
Later on, when lie was shifted to Clunes, he com-
placently remarked that he found “ eight girls to
every man,” consequently they were “rushing”
him, but he was “not taking any just then.”
Reverence for Woman is a difficult doctrine for
Man—especially a policeman—to live up to in a
district where he has all the charm of comparative
rarity. The modest, reticent spinster, who makes
sweetness and light in the world, and fills it with
a fine poetical sentiment, is but a creature of
favorable circumstances after all. When the
circumstances are unfavorable she drops her
maidenly reserve, and makes a bee-line for any
marriageable bloke who will accept her adoration
on sufferance.
About a curious case:—•
Malcolm’s Folly is a well-known building on one of the
most picturesque points of Sydney harbor. It achieved its
name from the fact that the owner and builder, John
Malcolm, 60 years ago abandoned the orthodox style of
architecture and put his kitchen on top of the house.
“ Folly ” would appear to run in the family of Malcolm,
as shown by an extraordinary case just heard in Equity.
The publication of the evidence was prohibited during the
hearing, but the pleadings and judgment disclose the
facts.
Two sisters named Farrell, residents of Manly, married
two brothers Malcolm, who, with another brother
Malcolm and a sister Malcolm, all inherited largely
under the father’s will. The brothers who married the
Farrells were hard drinkers, and died early, leaving
their wives’ a life-interest in the property with re-
mainder to their children. Failing children, the other
brother and the sister would inherit. One widow was
childless ; the other had two children. For 14 years the
mother drew maintenance and education money for the
offspring. Then the brother and sister set up a case that
the children were in a sense bogus, and had been “pur-
chased ” or “ adopted.” It was alleged that Mrs. Malcolm
had been seen at a sports meeting at Goulburn three days
after the alleged birth of the first child, and some few
days after was at a race meeting- in the same town. With
regard to the second child, a lady declared that she went
out to a certain place at the Glebe with Mrs. Malcolm,
and there obtained a child from a woman, who after-
wards, she alleged, claimed it as her own. The defence
was, of course, a general denial. A crowd of witnesses
were examined, including Mrs. Malcolm, but the Judge
said that the plaintiffs had not proved their case, and dis-
missed the suit.
This legal racket probably cost a good deal more than
the original Malcolm spent in building his kitchen on the
roof.
* * * *
One of Sydney’s historic corners changed
hands, recently, at a price not disclosed, Henry
Moses, M.L.C., being the purchaser. The corner
is that north-west of King and Pitt streets, known
years ago as Punch’s Hotel. In its early days, the
corner house was called the Bunch of Grapes, and
was kept by Michael Napthali, who afterwards
kept the Ropemakers’ Arms, in Cleveland-street.
In the very early ’4o’s and until 1867, the house
was kept by Alfred Toogood. Then Punch and
M‘Grath, the well-known oarsmen, took it. Jim
Punch subsequently had it on his own, MGrath
removing to the City Wine Vaults, George-street.
When Jim Punch died, Francis, who acted as
coxswain to Punch and MGrath, got it, and he
sold to the Federal Bank. Since then, its fortunes
have varied. In the very early ’4o’s (’4l, writer
thinks), a vacamt allotment on the north was sold
at £IOO a foot, then considered a fabulous price.
While stout persons are talking about
immigration and making irrelevant references to
the Six Hatters several times daily, there is a sum
of £IOOO going begging in Melbourne as the
nucleus of a fund for a foundling hospital, which
would add to the population by preserving locally-
produced citizens. The money was left by old
Charley Wright, famous as the proprietor of the
Coliseum, familiar to speedy Melbourne of the
early days. _ Although Wright has been dead some
yearS, nothing has been done to carry out his
wishes. His solicitor is now writing pathetic
letters to the papers, pointing out that he is will-
ing to hand over the cash to the Treasurer, but
hinting that, unless Bent does something himself,
the £IOOO will' go a very little way towards main-
taining the State’s lost infant.
* * * *
Wexford-street, in the slum area about to
be resumed by Sydney Corporation, was known
50 years ago as Market-lane, and was then occu-
pied by respectable trades-people. That was
before the Chinese invasion. Exeter-place, off
Wexford-street, now a dangerous haunt, where
the stranger is struck with a bottle at night, had
then some fine houses, and an almost aristocratic
flavor.
J* * * *
udge Eagleson’s remittal of the
sentence of a young woman—convicted of receiving
a five-guinea fur jacket—on condition that she
married the young man, recalls a somewhat simi-
lar case in a N.S.W. mining town not long ago.
A man was charged, before the P.M., with having
stolen verandah chairs and other useful household
articles. He had been caught in the act, and a
search of his house disclosed many other verandah
chairs and a catalogue of sundries. A parson at-
tended to give the man a “character,” and men-
tioned that he had a wife and several small
children dependent upon him (presumably for
verandah chairs). Here the prosecuting constable
interposed that the woman was not prisoner’s wife,
and the numerous children, whom he seemed so
anxious to provide with seating accommodation,
were not his children. The magistrate pondered
for some time, and eventually hit upon the
brilliant scheme of adjourning the case for a fort-
night “ to give prisoner an opportunity to marry
the woman.” Which he did. Then the law for-
gave him all the chairs.
• • • •
Melbourne is in trouble over its attempts
to raise money for charity. First there was tho
St. Vincent’s Hospital lottery exposure. Then
came the police charity carnival business. A sec-
tion of the committee that ran the carnival has
mutinied, and declares that the thing was fixed
up by a few police and military officers, who spent
much carnival money in expenses without reference
to the general committee. Thecarnival raised £I4CO
-for the charities, and it cost £579 to collect the
£I4OO. The big Patriotic Fund carnival, also run
by the police, made a clear profit of £2002, and the
total expenses were only £115; but a different
crowd ran it. The Charity Carnival committee,
or the ruling section of it, is said to have voted
itself £lO from the funds and struck gold
medals to adorn its tummy as a memento of its
valuable work in the cause of humanity. A lot of
other things are alleged, and O’Callaghan, Chief
Commissioner of Police, has been asked to inquire
into the affair with all his might. After all, it
would be cheaper in the end for the State to run
the hospitals. The State has its drawbacks, but
it doesn’t spend £579 to collect £I4OO, and it
doesn’t hang gold medals on its tummy.
* * * *
August 29 was a record day in the matter
of frozenness for South Australia, and was marked
by the largest fal-l of snow ever experienced in the
Mount Lofty hills. Hitherto snow has been a
giddy novelty to the Adelaide folk, and the
apology for a fall which has been experienced
about once in a decade has sent dozens of citizens
rushing wildly into the hills that they might be
able to boast of having been in asnowstorm. Recent
fall covered the hills for many miles, and in places
to the depth of a foot, and is likely to furnish
conversation in Adelaide for months.
* a * *
An art-critic writes :
There are valuable bits in a historiette on the beginnings
of Victorian art, prefixed by W. Moore to the catalogue
of the local Artists’ Show. The first Art Exhibition was
held in the first Exhibition Building, now the site of tho
mint. The Victorian Society of Fine Arts was formed in
1856, James Smith being a member. He was the first to
suggest the formation of the National Gallery. The
society included such artists as Von Guerard, Chevalier,
Gilfillan, and Strutt, with Summers the sculptor. After
holding two or three good shows, with small attendances,
the society died. The National Gallery began in 1862,
with a £IOOO grant. The Schools of Painting and Design
were founded in 1870. Among the first members of the
life class were Cooke, White, M‘Cubbin, Richardson,
Johnstone, and Tom Roberts. They were followed by
Streeton, Withers, Mather, Colquhoun, Gibbs, J. Ashton,
Fox, Tucker, Mackennal, Conder, Bunny, &c. Here, too,
the scholarship-men, such as Longstaff, A, Altson, Quinn,
Coates, Meldrum, and M. Altson, foregathered round the
throne. Buvelot came in 1865. In 1870 the Academy of
Arts was formed. This amalgamated with the Art Asso-
ciation in 1886. The Association had been formed by the
professionals, who broke away from the amateurs. The
afterwards eminent sculptor Woolner was a Melbourne
resident in 1852.

“A SMOKER’S TRINITY.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When filled with glory and conceit,
Say, with a girl beside the sea
(A band makes softest melody),
To make my happiness complete,
There’s one thing yet—
As we coquette with lips and eyes,
And whisper tender honeyed lies —
A cigarette !
The waiter clears the wine and fruits,
And brings the coffee and liqueurs,
A starlit night without allures,
The chaffeur round the corner toots
The motor car.
Soon, through the city bathed in light
We flash. Ah, then ! What keen delight—
A mild cigar 1
Back to the bush. At break of day
The billy boils ; we saddle up
And take a healthy bite and sup,
Then mount and gaily ride our way—
Then, time is ripe
To light and puff with deep content,
Best friend that man was ever sent,
A seasoned pipe!
n.s. w.
DRILETER.
When maidens are but one to eight
Compared with eager men,
How coy and blushful, how sedate,
The sought-for darlings then !
But, oh ! how free with smile and glance,
How full of risky fun,
How quick those girls to seize a chance
When they are eight to one !

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 5, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
Taking a second look at Sydney Art Society’s
show I noticed that Syd. Long, Julian and
Howard Ashton, Alice Muskett and Piguenit do
not exhibit. Percy Spence can afford to neigh
with pride over his horses on a bleak, wind-swept
hill. In Norman Carter’s portrait of his cello-
playing brother the artist has mastered many
difficulties. Legs and arms flung forward or thrust
backward, curved wrist, curled fingers, and face
lowered over an uncouth-looking instrument.
Norman Carter, if that is his beginning,, will go
far in portrait painting. Of Watkins’ pictures I
like his Red Drape best. Fred. Leist has one
extraordinary effort—a nude lady standing under
an arched tree. Her feet rest on top of the grass
blades, and she is “cooee-ing” for a necklace and
a bangle, as some thief has stolen her clothes.
Leist’s other picture—a shanty with pumpkins—
is a dear little bit of color.
Lister Lister has a satisfactory seascape (No.
149). We have all seen ’scapes just like this (you
know Whistler’s retort about “Naturegetting on”),
and it has the “feeling” of the “menace of the
irreclaimable sea.” Henri Tebbitt’s bit of Cook’s
River at Tempe—a dreamy tender thing—
soon found a purchaser. The National Gallery
bought eight pictures, including three of
Minns’. Albert Hanson and George Collingridge
have some pleasant touches of green wood
and cheerful sun-baked lands. Dattilo-Rubbo
is always reckoned the “strong,” uncompromising
painter among the Sydney artists. His “Banzai”
is a Domain-dosser backgrounded by the Jap flag
streaming with blood, and flourishing a property
sword. Miss A. Cusack’s “Narcissi” and “Gum
Blossom and Bottle-Brush” are worthy of her
reputation as a flower painter of skill. Her sister
Edith is a large exhibitor of floral studies and
portraits. Her “ Black Hat ” is a much fancied
study. Miss Muriel Nicholls is a clever damsel,
and her tiny color studies of children (rather after
the style of Elizabeth Shippen Greene) are just
delightful.
Concerning Datillo-Rubbo’s “Banzai.” The
artist, when painting the picture, went- to the
Jap. Consul for a sword so. as to get the correct
thing. The Consul was stiff and proud as became
a brown representative of the conquering race, and
presumed that by this time the Japanese brand of
weapon was well known, or ought to be. Rubbo
went away and painted the gory “ Banzai.” Then
he wanted the Jap. Consul to come and see the
awful damage the Russians had done to his people.
The visit to N.S. Wales of Earl and Lady
Jersey will interest the men rather than the
women of the State. Jersey was a very popular
Governor. He made a good start by speaking in
defence of Australia against its “mosquito critics ”
the day his feet touched N.S. Wales soil. It was
a time when, owing to the shocking habit our
representative citizens had of running building
societies and banks to smash, this country was
having a bad time from the London press. Lady
Jersey hardly shared her husband’s popularity.
She was credited with being a bit of a blue stock-
ing, and knowledge is a thing which Mrs. Potts
Point finds irritating.
The Jerseys patronised “Arts and Letters”
some, whilst here. The habit that Mr. W. N.
Willis had of reciting “Friends, Romans, Coun-
trymen ” and “ Alas poor Yokel,” and other
Shakspearian gems when he wasn’t getting im-
provement leases, earned for him the friendship
of the Vice-regal pair, and on one of his
visits to England the magnetic land-agent
stayed with them. I don’t know how many
castles Lord Jersey has, but he must have
several, or be occasionally over-crowded, for he
is most generous in entertaining Australians
abroad. Mr. Daniel O’Connor, who has been
trying to transmute some of his Demosthenic elo-
quence into cold type, wrote to Lord Jersey
asking his advice as to whether he should publish
in London or in Sydney. “In London,” came
the cabled reply. “Be my guest while here.”
At Mr. Laurance Phillip’s concert the other
night Miss Jean Maclurcan (whose portrait appears
elsewhere) made her debut as a singer. She
suffered much from nervousness and will not do
herself justice until she warbles with the con-
fidence of a canary. Miss Maclurcan has a clear
birdlike voice of excellent quality.
Good news ! Lady Rawson has recovered and
with his Ex. and Miss Alice are giving long,
long thoughts to their return journey to Sydney.
Mrs. Brough opens a fancy-dress bazaar to-day
at the Unitarian church. Now, that’s being
popular! You must be some waratahs in your
country before you are asked to open church fetes.
The rather quiet Darlevs came out of their
cocoons last night, and, with pretty Mrs. Forbes
Sempill and Captain Wilson, made a bright-
looking party at the “Walls of Jericho.”
A curious little law case that went quite un-
reported occurred a fortnight ago. Fuller Golden,
the imported Tivoli star of the moment, invited
theatrical manager William Anderson, and the
latter’s business manager, Skinner, to dinner at
the Wentworth Hotel. Whilst the distinguished
party were eating bird of Paradise cooked in
violets, the overcoats of Anderson and Skinner
were stolen (perhaps by some embittered rival
manager) from the hall of the hotel. The losers
sued Mrs. Maclurcan, the proprietress (and writer
of the best Australian cookery book) for nine
guineas’ worth of lost overcoat. The smart little
widow repudiated responsibility, and when the
case came before Mr. Donaldson the plaintiffs
were non-suited. The losers were not the lady’s
guests, it seemed, but the guests of a guest.
Law’s a tricky thing, and this decision seems to
suggest that Fuller Golden is legally responsible
for the escaped clothes.
Talking of divorce: A “motive” for a
D’Annunzio drama can be found in the suit of the
Kaid Sir Henry Maclean, late military adviser to
the Sultan of Morocco, against his wife. Their
daughter was her mother’s denouncer, and the
girl’s white-hot jealous rage when she concluded
that her suitor was only using his “ engagement ”
to her as a screen for his intrigue with her
mother is a study in elementary passion. All
the parties seem to have been given
over to the primary passions, and there was a
fine setting to the intrigue—romantic gardens,
laurels in flower, and the Spanish woman (Mrs.
Maclean) with her white face and dark eyes and
her fan-flirtings. One seems to breathe a dense
tropical atmosphere. As to “ Patsy ” Maclean,
the daughter, the Judge must have felt that he
was “ assisting at the toilette of a woman’s soul,”
to use an American critic’s telling phrase. Any-
one who wants the basis of a drama has it here ;
all he needs is a desert, and a white-washed city,
and two or three camels, and an insurrection for
local coloring.
Young barrister Curtis and his two collabo-
rators in the writing of the comic opera,
“ The Coquette,” deserve, after all, a sheaf of
good words. If “The Coquette” had come
from London with the names of the writers
of “ Florodora ” and “The Cingalee” on it,
and been presented by J. C. Williamson with
his superb accessories, all our capital cities would
have welcomed it —wearily, perhaps, because of its
so much reminiscence—but welcomed it all the
same. The makers evidently worked most of the
time with their heads turned over their shoulders
and looking at the corpses of other comic operas.
As some Superior Person has said, “We are
handicapped by our self-consciousness. Who
would be original must be a Nonconformist.”
I wish Mr. Curtis would recast his opera and
make it Australian. Or if there is no picturesque
setting for it on this continent—if Mr. Willis
really has all the desirable sites—then let the
scene be placed near the Arctic circle by way of
novelty. We are all sick of Ceylon and India. I
want a chorus of bears and icebergs, with Arctic
foxes loping round, and dramatic personages of
the Esquimaux persuasion, and explorers, male
and female. Enter the heroine sucking a piece
of blubber, whilst the Aurora Borealis plays the
chromatic scale.
Principal girls who appeared in ‘ ‘ The Coquette ”
were pupils of Madame Christian. Miss M. Regan
disclosed a clear, sweet soprano; Miss Clare Lyt-
tleton, who is a daughter of Justice Chubb, of
Queensland, hasn’t much of a voice, but otherwise
Providence has been mighty kind to her. Mr.
Curtis’s acting of an insurance agent will uphold
his reputation as the best amateur character actor
at large in this Lands Commission city. And the
voices raised by Messrs. W. S. Gordon and J. 0.
M‘Keown were pleasant surprises to the majority
of the audience.
I wish Miss O’Neil would give more Parthenias
and fewer Antoinettes. Former is a charming
play, and Nance’s outfit—a flowing classic robe,
flowing fair hair, and a big bunch of violets—is a
poetic one in which to travel mountain defiles to
Ingomar’s robber camp. If she had but entered
carrying a dress-basket (one strap slipping) or an
ancient Greek carpet-bag.
“Cranbrook,” the residence of the State Gover-
nor, is now musical (or noisy, according to your
point of view) with the mewlings of a son born to
the Harrison Smiths. Papa H.S. is private secre-
tary to Excellency Rawson when the latter is at
home.
The three handsomest women appearing on the
Australian stage to-day are Americans—Maud
Jeffries, Maud Amber, and Nance O Neil.
If you are a dissentient to this statement
please speak up. I can almost feel voices saying
“Mrs. Maesmore Morris.” Personally I think
her the prettiest—please note the distinction—and
portrait-wise she’s beautiful. But when it comes
to portraits, can any of ’em be got up to look more
beautiful than those of Nellie Stewart? Talking
of Americans, Miss Marie Narelle has now got
the New York stamp on her face, figure, dress and
coiffure. You take her to be an American born
(from her latest portraits), and she seems to have
the pride of poise that comes of artistic success
and large takings at the box-office. I notice that
she’s engaged right into 1906 in the country wlieie
the dove is now a bigger bird than the eagle.
Miss Bertha Faning and Miss Annie Perry,
well-known Sydney warblers, have returned from
European study. Miss Maggie Sterling is also on
her way out here for a concert tour.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“ Mrs.'Maesmore Morris is just now a shadow
of her former self. In serpentine velvet, when
managing that artful twist of her limbs, she
looks like a stately boa - constrictor up on
end. There is no curve—she is just one
long slender note of exclamation weighed down
with clothes. Tins thinning is said to be
due to anxiety as to that divorce suit, and strollers
in the Gardens the day following the decree nisi
till of the pale and spirituelle beauty with
mournful visage and eyes that had done
much weeping. All of which argues nice
and admirable feelings in the lady, although
she was reported as smiling at certain items
in her cross-examination. The great point
in the beauty’s favor was the sympathy ot
the Morris clan. The ma-in-law has always loved
the fair one.
“ In ‘ Her Way,’ Mrs. Maesmore Morris, as the
wife of the spendthrift, is adjured by a worldly
mama to go and get a divorce—‘a widow having
such a great chance of a good catch ! Each
night, after her own decree, this line caused a
round-mouthed ‘ Oh ! ’ from the audience.
“Dame Forrest fussed off by the Oruba with
the G.-G. detachment on Tuesday. She desires to
personally bow their Exes into the sandy State
and lead the forthcoming kow-tow. In the West,
it is likely the dame’s pilotage will be resented by
the Six Families who have been asking each other
for several seasons if the Forrest pair are to ride
the social whirlwind for ever.
“ The Daffodil Show hurt one’s expectations.
The warm-hearted lovers of the Yere de Vere
blossom experienced a nasty chill on entering the
Athenaeum Hall on Thursday. The growers had
their perfect blooms labelled and set forth like so
many cannibal skulls, or other curios of a dead
and dried museum. The tables were far apart.
So instead of a full golden flush of daffodils, the
eye encountered a speck just here and there, with
drab spaces as a huge parenthesis. Byron
Moore and other amateur culturists did
the rounds of the tables in a coldly
critical spirit. A few haughty culturists dotted
down the choicest daffodil labels for emulative
purposes, and a thin line of grey old Goodies
meandered round the tables. As a daffodil party
the show was a disappointment.
“ A correspondent sends me this, assuring me of
its veracity:—
They are telling a remarkable story about a dog in
Federal circles just now. Lady Northcote has a favorite
pug dog that suffers severely from sea-sickness. Lady
Northcote, who loves that poodle dearly, is going shortly
to Westralia, and, ’tis said, asked one of the Federal
Ministers if he would mind taking Fido over to Adelaide
by rail, so as to save the quadruped as much as possible.
The Minister thought it was a joke, and said he would do
so. But, later on, Lady N. sent the Minister a dainty
note asking whether he would call for Fido, or whether
she should deliver the brief blunt beast at the Minister’s
office per flunkey. The amazed politician replied that he
had thought the request a jest, and that he had some-
thing else to do besides dragging poodles over the con-
tinent. And there is vexation in consequence. It
suggests the necessity for creating a Minister for Portable
Dogs. The portfolio might be given to Mr. Wilks, who
has had experience hauling the Yellow Pup around.
“The Kennel Club, which is run entirely by
the second sex, has as a prize exhibit and a decoy
for the public some dachshunds, belonging to the
State Exes. These particular pets, marked ‘ not
for competition,’ and guarded by a Ston-
ington servant, snored at ease under a
great spread of Union Jacks in the centre
of the circu?. When Dame Talbot hove in
sight, the attendant prodded the pets into
lively attention, and this passed to glad cavort-
ings at sight of their mistress. Miss Talbot, who
had a collie on show somewhere, likewise was
treated to doggie hospitality, and the Cliveden
widow and Mrs. Charles Ryan, as bodyguard to
the pair of tall Talbotesses, said sweet things
about their dogs. The Stonington dame had,
however, saved all her enthusiasm for the
birds. She spent a time that must have
been trying to her followers and the
Private Sec. (who carried a comforter) talking
and whistling bird lingo to the feathered speci-
mens. Miss Talbot tried several times to execute a
crescendo whistling passage at various cages, but
she didn’t accomplish any. The Vice-Regal dame
managed that creditably. She can whistle. Her
early education hasn’t been altogether neglected.
“ A Melbourne bride-elect changes her name in
a lace frock valued at £2OO, without counting in
the under-laces and other frillies. The costly out-
fit is the result of a prosperous bar-business with
an extensive jug-trade.
“ The transparent lace yokes for spring garb are
to be lined with cream or faint-pink chiffon. Such
a lining is suitable either for the raw-chested or
the pallid-skinned. The cream tones down the
leafy aspect, and the pink induces a narcissus
flush.
“ Manager G. Musgrove comes back from the
Big Smoke with a pocket full of plans and plays.
One intention is to make a Yank tour with an
Australian contingent to back up Sweet Nell.
Miss Amy Willard—the saucy brunette with the
wasp waist and the twinkle —is one member likely
to be booked, and perhaps the plump, genial Miss
Singleton. The men of the present co., also Mrs.
Morris and others, will probably say ‘ ta-ta ’ to
manager George in November.
“Perhaps to demonstrate to this city how hale
and hearty and pink-toned she is, Miss Stewart
has been rushing round the shops and giving the
drapery business a stir. She has bought high
stacks of Spring millinery, reams of lace, leagues
of silk, and some of the latest 16-rib brollies.
Sweet Nell knew at. once the feature of the new
gamp. She remarked, ‘My ! what a lot of iron-
mongery ! ’ The sex that counts inaccurately and
generaliv fails in all branches of mathematics
doesn’t know till it is told that eight brolly ribs
once prevailed, but that 16 are now the thing.

tributed gratuitously by the Kennel fraternity—
that oil President of the Cat Club. At Saturday’s
show the placard, ‘ Vote for the Most Popular
Cat,’ led to all kinds of rude remarks.

was given by Mrs. York-Syme to a bride-elect
last week. The fortunate young person to get all
her cambric hankies and tin-pots as an aid to

“ The hunting Lorimers entertained a number
of kids and a sprinkling of grown-ups at a fancy
hop the last Saturday in August. The guests were
beseeched to get up florally, and libels on roses
and other blooms occurred with the excuse of good
intentions.
“A widower of wealth satisfied his relatives a
while ago of his non-matrimonial intentions, much
to the joy of the family, which had been alarmed
at his interest in an ex-chorus girl. Now the girls
say ‘ Papa is amused at the ridiculous things
people say. He may do many foolish things but
he will never marry again. It is such sport to
know this, and see the girls setting their caps at
our dear old Pater ! ’ It would be, I should say,
somewhat difficult to make eyes at a widower
under the mocking inspection of a couple of
daughters. One would need to be a cast-iron
spinster of desperate pluck or a highly experienced
widow for the task. A Papa guarded by numer-
ous daughters is almost inaccessible.
“Mr. Horace Stevens, the melancholy baritone
of the fine range, faced the matrimonial rails with
Miss Chapman, a gentle suburban girl, the other
Saturday. All the world of song rolled up to the
Cathedral for the marriage, and all St. Pauls
choir members, boys past and present, raised their
voices in the Eden chant to do their old mate
proud. But he was as serious as ever, and seemed
to regard the ceremony as an awfully solemn
business. The bridegroom is, among other things,
a fully-qualified dentist. How she-patients must
joy in that sad, sympathetic visage when it cranes
over to inspect a tooth that aches! A Collins-street
medico enjoys a fat income on the strength of a
face full of unutterable woe. ‘He knows, he
understands,’ says the sex that pines for sympathy.
“A sensation broke up the dull morning hours
of a large Melbourne hotel the other day, when a
deputation of dames waited on the manager to
ask that a lady carrying consumption germs
on her person be given a short and sharp
conge. The manager protested that he wasn’t
a Medical Board, and couldn’t constitute himself
a special officer to inquire as to what lady-visitors
suffered from, especially as most of them seemed
to suffer from something or other. The deputa-
tion insisted that something would have to be
done, or they’d leave in a body. He had just
decided to let them leave, when the lady of the
germs coughed herself to another capital, and
cleared the atmosphere of trouble. Fumigators
were set to work, and now peace and hygiene
reign in that establishment.
“A new engagement is that of Dr. F. U.
O’Sullivan to Miss Minna Bourke, the pretty chick
of a wealthy Gippslander. The doctor is a younger
brother of the globe-trotting physician. The two
O’Sullivans have a portentously grave air,
and their manner of showing Off their immense
cliest-areas suggests a pair of colossal pouter
pigeons.
‘ ‘ There is to be another attempt at a Henley on
the Smell River. This time it will be made in
October, when the wattle is in full bloom and the
willows that weep on the high banks of the per-
spective view are in leaf. It’s to be hoped that
the swagger Toorakites who run the revel will
pay for a few yards of art muslin this year to
clothe the nakedness of the boot boxes that serve
them for pagodas.
“ Senator Bobby Best, who not so long ago com-
mitted matrimony for the second time, deplores,
in a Melbourne interview, the wane of domestic
life in the Cold Country. He tells of the flat
craze and love of hotel life, and mourns the old
English custom of extensive families and fireside
circles.
“A bride went weeping to the altar a few
weeks ago because her pa refused to waste a day
at the knot-tying. He attended his Government
post with his Gladstone bag, according to custom,
and merely arrived home when his old slippers
were being pelted at the departing pair. A wiser
parent would have arrived a little earlier and
saved the slippers.

CRIPPLED FOR LIFE
Awful iluscular Rheumatism
Cured, After Doctors Failed, by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ rr«o look at Roy now you would never dream
■ that the doctors gave him up as a hope-
B less cripple a few years ago, ” said Mrs.
Wade,, of 93 Bond-street, E. Ballarat,
“He would have been bed-ridden to-day, and
twisted into all shapes with Muscular Rheumatism
if it hadn’t been for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
They raised him from a cripple’s bed after the best
doctors in Ballarat had failed to do him any good.
He is as healthy and active to-day as any young
fellow of 15 in the town.
“When he was 7 Roy had Scarlet Fever, and
just when he was getting well he caught a chill
which settled in the muscles of his leg. I noticed
that he always lay in bed with his leg doubled up.
When I tried to straighten it one morning he
screamed with pain. Every day it got worse.
At last it was twisted up like aV. I brought the
doctor, and he gave me a liniment to rub into the
leg. He told me to keep Roy wrapped up in-
wadding day and night. I carried out his orders,
but it nearly broke my heart to see how much the
little fellow suffered when I was rubbing him. He
would clinch his teeth, and big tears rolled down
his cheeks before he would cry out for me to stop.
In spite of all the torture, this rubbing only
seemed to make him worse.
“When T went to make up his bed he would
scream if I touched his leg. No one will ever
know all that child suffered. After I had rubbed
him and kept him in wadding for a month, his leg
was doubled up worse than ever and the pain was
twice as bad,” added Mrs. Wade. “The doctor
said he would have to have a consultation, and he
called in another doctor. They decided to
straighten his leg by force, and strapped it down
to a' splint. They' had to give Roy chloroform,
and the operation took them nearly an hour.
They said it was the only way to save him from
being crippled for life.
“ Never to my dying day will I forget the way
Roy suffered after he came out of the chloroform.
For the first three days the agony nearly drove
him mad. • I nearly went mad myself listening to
his screams and groans. He used to beg me to
undo the straps and take the splint away.
‘Mother,’ he cried, ‘it’s killing me.’ The tears
rolled down my face when I told him that he
would never be,able to walk again if I undid the
straps. He couldn’t move. My niece and I had
to turn him in bed. If there had been another
week of it, I think it would have killed him. But
after eleven days the doctors took the splint off,
and the whole thing was a failure. In a few
days the leg was doubled up like a V again, and
the pain was worse than ever.
“ For the next two months, the doctor attended
him. All that time he was an absolute cripple.
The doctor owned up that he was crippled for life.
In despair, Mr. Wade went to a Chinese doctor —
but he asked £2O, and even then he would not
guarantee a cure. Then I got my husband to go
down to Melbourne and consult the best Specialist
that money could procure there.
“ All this time my niece had been trying to
make me try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Roy,”
Mrs. Wade continued. “She kept telling me of
a man they had cured of Muscular Rheumatism
after the best Hospital doctors said he would never
walk another step. When Mr. Wade was down in
the city I made up my mind to give Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills a trial, and got some from King, the
chemist in Sturt-street.
“The very first box made Roy eat the best
meal that had passed his lips since he was taken
with Scarlet Fever. I only gave him half a- pill
after each meal—but I followed the directions and
gave him a piping hot bath every night. A week
later Roy told me that the pain wasn’t half as bad
as it had been. I made him try to stretch his leg
a little further every day. Within two months his
leg was as straight as ever and he could walk with
a firm step. To-day, Roy Wade is as active as any
boy of his age in Ballarat. If it hadn’t been for
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills he would have been bed-
ridden all these years.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills do only one thing, bu t
they do it well—they actually make new blood.
But the blood is the root of all health. If you are
in doubt about your own case, write for free
medical advice to Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Sydney. From that address you can also order
by mail the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills—
2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d. post free.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A member of the Banaualand Parliament
is going so often to the refreshment room that his
friends are concerned.
Died at Singleton (N.S.W.) aboriginal
Booboogoorranygalley, called “ Bandy” for short :
aged 100, according to native authority.
Labor member Dunsforcl, elected to
Bananaland Parliament in 1893, never missed a
sitting until the beginning of the present session,
when illness dragged him from the place where he
had sat till he nearly took root.
Patrick Brougham, who died recently, at
Gundagai (N.S.W.), aged 86, was a nephew of the
late Lord Chancellor who had the vehicle named
after him. Patrick came to Australia in his youth,
and at the time of his death was owner of Hart-
wood station, near Deniliquin.
There was married quite recently in a
fashionable manner and in a fashionable church,
the near relative of an old-time bushranger, who,
having done his time, settled in a homestead of
his own, and became as sedate and respectable as
the most dignified bishop could desire.
Dr. Normand M‘Laurin, Sydney’s political
medico, now aged 70 years, gains £5OOO by the
bequest of his deceased patient, W. E. Sparke.
It is not often that a patient thus evinces his
gratitude after he is dead. More frequently he
forgets to pay his bill when he is alive.
Ex-Justice Foster, Parkes’s old Attorney-
General, stands as the horrid example when the
question of judicial pensions comes up. He
slipped on to N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench in a
way many thought crafty, served with long holi-
days from February 14, 1888, to December 18,
1894, and has since been drawing a pension of
£lB2O per annum.
When Alfred Deakin’s father was book-
keepingfor Cobb and Co. (Robertson and Wagner),
at the Albion Hotel, Bourke-street,_ Melbourne,
Robertson discovered signs of promise in young
Deakin, and, it is said, paid for his education at
the Church of England Grammar School, where
the boy was afterwards teacher for a time. Very
early in life Alfred joined a debating society, and
at once displayed the astonishing powers of subli-
mated verbosity that have filled so much Hansard
since.
An Australian Hebrew—one Esmond
Sinauer, whose grandfather was the late Bingy
Bingy, of Melbourne—has scored another point
for the Chosen by carrying off a whole sheaf of
examination honors at Woolwich (England) Mili-
tary Academy. His total number of marks was a
record. He took two gold medals and eight other
prizes for military engineering, tactics, topo-
graphy, See., and his name is inscribed on a tablet
in the hall of the Royal Military Academy. The
word “genius” is misspelt. It should be
“ jewnius.”
Octavius C. Beale, of Sydney, goes to
Europe shortly on a pleasure trip, and he gene-
rously placed some of his leisure whilst abroad at
the disposal of the Commonwealth Government.
He was therefore asked to collect some facts about
certain foreign poisonous proprietary medicines
imported into the Commonwealth, and about in-
fant mortality. And what a howl there was from
the N.S.W. Foreign-traders in the Australian
Parliament when the fact was disclosed that Mr.
Beale was to be allowed to do this work for his
country at his own expense ! The explanation is
that Mr. Beale is a manufacturer; and an Aus-
tralian manufacturer, in the eyes of the Australian
Foreign-trader, is an habitual criminal.
TAIT, CHIEF COMMISSIONER OF
BENT S RAIL WAYS.
A Tasmanian pioneer with an historic
name died recently. Mr. St. Paul Gellibrand
belonged to an old English family. One of his
name, J. Tice Gellibrand, was Attorney-General
of Tasmania (known then as Van Diemen’s Land)
in the thirties, and was one of the fifteen members
of the Port Phillip Association on whose account
John Batman made his big land bargain with the
blacks. Tice Gellibrand visited Port Phillip, and,
with a friend, made an excursion into the Colac
forest. They were never heard of afterwards.
The blacks were suspected of cannibalism in those
days, and if that accounts for Gellibrand’s disap-
pearance he was probably the only Attorney-
General on record who was eaten.
The late W. E. Sparke was the richest
Australian who died in his own country since
millionaire Tyson passed out. His estate was
valued at £423,777, and provided a windfall for
Carruthers of between £40,000 and £50,000 in
probate duties. Bulk of estate is left to the
Carsons, the residuary legatee being a wine and
spirit merchant, to whom goes the mansion of St.
Monan’s, Elizabeth Bay, Sydney. Deceased used
to say that John See offered to purchase St.
Monan’s for use as a State Government House,
but even the large price offered did not tempt
Sparke. He preferred to end his days at his old
home, and a few more tens of thousands were of
no importance to him.

It is said that one of the bitterest
opponents to the candidature of Meehan, M.P.,
in the last State election, was an outback aider-
man who waxed indignant over the “cheek” of
an uneducated shearer presuming to oppose Nick
Willis. During the campaign the alderman was
elected mayor, and his first joyous duty was to
convene a meeting in opposition to Meehan. Ihe
council clerk drew his attention to the draft notice
re the holding of the meeting, which document
was signed, “John Bloggins, Mare.’ “Well,
ain’t I the blanky mare ?” was all the satisfaction
he got from the staunch upholder of education.
Some tangled obituary :
Accidentally shot in South Africa, while hunting, Harry
Hutchinson Roberts, son of the late R. H. Roberts,
M.L.C. (N.S.W.), aged 36. In the early thirties Joseph
Roberts, of Burwood, married a daughter of William
Hutchinson, “ of London,” the daughter being a Sydney
native. On Roberts’ death, the widow married the late
John Lackey, President N.S.W. Leg. Council. The origi-
nal Hutchinson held the position of superintendent of
Government mills, and afterwards superintendent of con-
victs. He lived in a square brick house on the south-
east corner of King and George-streets. The Arcade
property is still in the family, as may be noted on a tablet
within. R. H. Roberts, M.L.C., married a daughter of
Emmanuel Neich, who, in the thirties, kept The Black
Dog publichouse (now the Ocean Wave), on the Rocks.
Mr. Neich afterwards kept an inn at Burwood for 60 years.,
Wm. Hutchinson was known as “ The Convicts’ Banker,”
inasmuch as before the establishment of the Savings Bank
he kept the savings of such of the “bond” as were thrifty.
‘ ‘ Ku Klux ” writes indignantly
Allow me a corner of your backyard to argue the point
with “ E.” (24/8/’O5) re Bent. I want “E.’s” scalp. So
Tommy “ believes strenuously in his country, the finest
place on God’s earth, etc.” Let “E.” ask the unemployed
ironworker if that is his opinion. When they have a trial
of the new dredge Pioneer would be a good time to put
the question. The idea of holding up the Brighton
Bounder as a model Socialist!—the man who was used by
Irvine and Shiels as a catspaw in the railway strike, the
man who, as head of a suburban gasworks, is now
virtually defying the Commonwealth by importing a
manager who' could easily he obtained here, the man who
imported Tait to undermine the eight hours system on
the railways, the man who was a member of the Govern-
ment which allowed the Waranga Basin contractor to
evade the minimum wage. Let “ E.” do as I have done.
Go down to the Labor Bureau, and, after his name has
been on the register for two months, see a Brighton man
who has been registered two days get a job in front of
him. Yes, Tommy is good, good to himself and his friends.
Miss Greig, the first lady barrister, or
barristeress, in Australia, is a daughter of the
senior partner in the old firm of Greig and
Murray, auctioneers, of Collins-street, Melbourne;
her mother being a sister of the Jate William Bede
Dailey. Greig and Murray in the early sixties
succeeded to the business of Mr. Tennant, the sale
room standing between the old Union Bank and
the Hall of Commerce (“Under the Verandah”).
It requires years to put the veneer upon a
Judge. That may be observed in the High Court
whenever Justices Griffith, Barton, and O’Connor
pronounce successive judgments. Barton
and O’Connor appear to be continually
rebutting, refuting, re-establishing.
They have not yet put on that
coating which long years have
made glisten on Griffith —a calm,
impersonal omniscience to give his
pronouncement the air of utter
finality. Listening to that fiat,
one wonders that any Court of
Appeal ever had the audacity to up-
set it. Such an air of infallibility comes to a Judge
only after the reversing of many of his decisions.
Seeing that so often somebody is wrong, the only
possible attitude for a sensible man is that he is
always right. But it takes time before a Judge
learns to do it automatically.
The worst man to take a beating in Federal
politics is Josiah Symon. Smiling, courteous,
compromising and gentle in his handling of the
Labor party whilst holding the office of Attorney-
General, Reid’s defeat has converted Symon into
a snarling, irascible person, with the manners of a
badly-educated dingo. Not only does the party
opposite suffer by Symon’s frequent exhibitions of
temper, but his own followers come in for it.
Poor old Walker, Tory of all the Tories, but per-
sonally as decent a man as ever sat in Parliament,
tried to move a perfectly tame and harmless
amendment, when Symon gritted at him through
his teeth. “ The honorable gentleman attaches far
too much importance to himself.” Walker was too
much taken aback to reply to the insult. Every-
body expected a subsequent apology, but the
leader of the Senate Opposition made no attempt
to atone for his boorishness to perhaps the most
faithful supporter of his own side.
Comlhwaite Hector Kason, the new
Premier of Westralia, was some kind of an
M.L.C. in that State about 1889, but made
his real political start in 1897, when South
Murchison returned him triumphantly at the head
of the poll with 87 votes. The next man received
80 votes, and the whole four candidates had 213
among them. (Even then Rason wasn’t the most
remarkable specimen of the year, for Wallace of
Yalgoo won with 39 votes in an exciting poll of
77.) In 1901 things improved ; Rason stood for
Guildford, and got 241 votes, beating the next
man by 12.
F. M. Bailey, F.L.S., Queensland’s
enthusiastic botanist, who has contributed
immensely, in his long official life, to the world’s
knowledge of Australian flora, has his declining
years smoothed I>y a salary of £l5O a year from an
ungrateful State. At best he has neyer been
rated on the Estimates higher than a chief clerk,
and for years has been salaried at the rate of a
messenger. Bailey is old, it is true, and at one
time was paid a little better—very little. With
Ur. Maxwell drawing £3OOO a year, and the
Botanist £l5O, Queensland can show two extremes
that don’t meet.
A base trick was played in the Heps.,
the other night, upon Arthur Robinson, the cypher
who gesticulates on behalf of Wannon (Vic.).
Arthur was speaking on the terrific iniquities of
the Iron Bonus Bill, and was in a great state of
perspiration and perfervid language. A low
person in the press gallery perpetrated a vile
caricature of him, and signalled Bill Wilks to
come out into the lobby. Wilks agreed to foist
the caricature upon its victim whilst the latter
was in full explosion. Members frequently aid
one another whilst speaking with notes on points
worth stressing ; and Wilks waited until Robin-
son’s stock of infuriated memoranda was nearly
played out. Then he folded the caricature, and
passed it to the unsuspicious youth from the
Wannon, who clutched it gladly, for the last
apology for an idea had just trickled out of his
head. “Now, Mr. Speaker,” said Arthur, un-
folding the outrage, “now we have to consider
this aspect of the question.” And just then
he caught sight of his own depressing aspect, and
considered it with pain, not to say horror. The
spectacle broke him up, and the Robinson speech
ended right there.
Senator Playford, of S.A., is a remarkable
person. The vast market-gardener had little
schooling, but his stores of knowledge are none
the less extensive. Symon got up in the Senate
the other day, and threw a number of superior
clods at the provision in the Copyright Bill giving
authors 30 years’ protection from piracy after their
death. “Look at the classical writers of old
Greece,” Symon exclaimed; “they got along
very well without any Copyright Act. Socrates
had no other posthumous protection, for
instance, than his genius. Socrates lec-
tured in the Academy, .and” “No, he
didn’t,” Playford interjected, “he talked in the
street or anywhere he could. He was a regular
Yarra-banker.” Symon waved the correction
aside with the paw that has collected so many fees
and proceeded to dilate on Plato. “Look at
Plato now,” he said, “Plato didn’t look forward
to legislative protection when he wrote his
‘Memorabilia.’ Plato” “ Xenophon wrote
the ‘Memorabilia,’ not Plato,” remarked Play-
ford calmly. Symon changed the subject with a
certain air of haste.

The Paddington Council has received from the Govern-

Stick 'em. in the Mayor's stummick!
Died in Adelaide, the other day, Frederick
Mitcham, a street newsvendor, who was never
known by any other name than that of “Pompey.”
He was the most caricatured person in Adelaide—
excepting broker Jim Dick—and was one time a
musician in a street band. He looked exactly like
a man who had blown a trombone so vigorously
that he had never been able to get his face back
into its proper position.

Aboriginalities.
“C.X.G.”: Travelling from IS. A. to
Queensland, via Innamincka, just recently, was
much struck with the prevalence of wild dogs
along Strzleeki Creek. A few years back, when
the S.A. Government was paying so much per
scalp, it was a rare thing to see a dingo. Now
they race alongside the mail coach for miles, and
seem to have little, if any, fear of man. Also, they
came up to the coach in the night, and chewed up
some -of the harness, right alongside where the
driver was sleeping. His language next morning
was of the most degrading character. The same
driver told me that, on the previous trip, he had
lost his horses, owing to the dogs having eaten the
green-hide hobbles off them overnight. I give
this last for what it is worth, merely pointing out
that the man was a Queenslander, and Queens-
landers are a painfully accurate people, who loathe
prevarication, and wouldn’t do an injury to the
Truth on any account, unless it attacked them
first.

“ Nulla ” on various things :
Re “X.Y.” and the rat-’roo. The sudden glare of a
bright light on a dark night affects the eyes of anvthing,
more so those of the rat-’roo, for like the ’possum, native
eat and bandicoot, he is a nocturnal animal. Momentary
blindness rather than inquisitiveness causes him to afford
an easy mark. Sydney trams account for a goodly
number of roof warblers for the same reason. “ Tabby,”
crossing the road, is confused by the glare of the 32 c.p.
headlight, and crouches on the track, and then the wheels
of Juggernaut make one cat less. The trams don’t catch
many cows, so the term “cow-catcher” has been superseded
by “life-shield.” “Fool-trap,” in the drivers’ opinion,
would be an even better word.
“Den” : I once (about the year when
Alfred the Great died) struck a small S.A. sea-
port, and strolled into the police court, the only
place of amusement in the town. A very
fat and very weary J.P. was dozing on the
bench, and throwing in a bored remark now
and then when a witness became too
voluble. Prisoner was charged with stealing
an anchor. .Small boy gave evi-
dence, mostly hearsay. Local
baker swore he met a man on the
beach who told him that he (the
man) had met a man who said
that he (presumably the prisoner)
had “nicked” an anchor. Then
the constable gave evidence
of arrest, and was proceed-
ing at some length to re-
count what “a man” told
him, when he was inter-
rupted by the very tired
voice of the sleepy magis-
trate. “ Yes, yes,
constable, but
you really have
no case, you
know; no case
at all. In the
first place
(yawn), we
have no evi-
dence to show
whose proper-
ty this anchor
really is.” The
polic e m a n
scratched his
head. “ Well,
yer Worshup.
I’m not quite
certain on that
point; but I think
it belongs either to
Air. Cook or yer
Worshup.” Then the
J.P. awakened suddenly
and yelled, “ What ! ”
After that the case went
along briskly and ended in a sen-
tence of six months.
“ Croydon ” : I lately dispatched a cent*
pede by cutting him in two with a chisel. The
head made for the bush, while the tail started
towards the coast. I engineered a collision, when
tailless bucked in and worried headless. Had to
go away to defraud somebody of my next week’s
board, but returned later to find headless gone,
while tailless was exploring the humpy. I got
him into a small box. He kept going for five
days, and wore a beaten track round the box.
Finally he died in sections.

* * * *
Bunny is one more lever to level the gum-
giants along the Vic. inland water-courses. The
pests burrow amongst the giants’ roots, following
the tracks well into the banks, during the summer
and autumn. Winter and spring floods do the
rest. Soon the Big Trickle Country (on the Vic.
side especially) will be as bare of trees as a gallows,
and all because a few State officials cannot be
spared to develop the contents of a few three-
penny packets of guru-seeds, and plant the
seedlings, at the proper time, when they would
grow to shake their whiskers at posterity. Such a
proceeding would be in direct contradiction to
the, present system of forestry that does absolutely
nothing, except issue to wood-vandals licenses to
destroy what they never think to replace.
HE KNEW BUNNY’S LITTLE WAYS.
TEACHER (at bush school): “Now, Tommy, if I
gave you a pair of rabbits this week, another
pair next week, and a pair every week fol-
lowing after for two months, how many
rabbits would you then have?”
TOMMY: “ Thousands on ’em, Miss.”
The “ backbone of the country ” is a very
casual spine sometimes. Close alongside a school
in north-west Victoria, a while ag'o, the backbone
stacked his wheat, 1400 bags of it, waiting for the
price to rise. Meantime, as the mice followed the
wheat, the backbone got in a stock of strychnine
mixed it with flour, and laid it over the stack. The
school used to play on the stack, and dodge the
little heaps of poison, and, for a wonder, no
youngster dipped his finger into the stuff to see
what it tasted like. Then the plague of mice
came and tore the bags in spite of the strychnine,
and w’hen they had nicely opened up the wheat
the rain came and washed the “paste,” if there
was any left of it, among the grain. Just recently
the stack was taken down and sent away to the
mill at Horsham. And when several people in
that neighborhood were beginning to think that
they did notice something peculiar about the taste
of their bread, the local schoolmaster announced
that it couldn’t be the strychnine, because when
he saw his youngsters playing among it he went
round and brushed it off the bags. But that
wasn’t the backbone’s fault.
* * * *
“The Prooshan” : Cocky Hudson was a
deveut bird that commanded the entire respect of
his neighborhood. He threw no tumblers off the
table, neither did he invade the brown hen’s nest,
eat her egg, and then cackle to deceive the owner.
He never complained of the bread, or used pro-
fanity, or said “You’re a liar!” like the politi-
cians’ birds. He was, in every way, an exemplary
cockatoo. At night he sat on his stand in the
corner of the dining-room, with head under wing,
till the family engaged in divine worship. Then
cocky was wideawake in a moment, and took part
as earnestly as the rest. The family took no
notice of him, and saw nothing singular in the
performance ; but to a stranger it seemed droll to
see cockalorum, on hearing “ Hail, Mary ! Mother
of God! ” bow his head with crest reverently
depressed, and hear him croak “ Glory be to the
Father! Glory be to the Father !” The only
time he ever evinced a military spirit was when
Dill Macky was billed to lecture in the local hall.
A tea-agent, not unlike Dill, happening to call,
cocky rose and sampled his leg, but immediately
on discovering his mistake, he wiped his beak and
apologised.

“O’Fipp” : Allow me to doubt whether
“Alba’s” butcher who wouldn’t eat meat, and
his fishmonger who barred fish as food for himself
(B. 17/8/’O5), are typical. Staying once in a big
boarding-house, which both fish and meat sales-
men largely patronised, I found that the butchers
believed in four heavy meat meals per day, and
the fishmongers (not Dagoes, by the way) were
never tired of eating fish. On Sundays most of
the butchers would bike inland, on the off-chance
of seeing some beefy live stock to admire ; while
the fishmongers usually spent the Sabbath in fish-
ing from Port Melbourne pier.
“ C.IT.R,” contradicts the other man, with
some firmness
Notwithstanding all remarks to the contrary, I main-
tain that roast porcupine is a fraud. After the carcase
lias been scalded (you are told to be very particular about
the scalding), it looks as though it had been blasted out
of a rock and then shot at with a Maxim. The skin is
tougher than india-rubber, the flesh as black as charcoal,
and the smell !—my dog took one sniff at the corpse after
I had heaved it out over the gum-trees, and then retired
indignantly to his stump-hole. I have cooked kangaroo,
wallaby, badger, ’possum, bandicoot, and kangaroo-rat in
every known fashion, at all ages, sizes, and seasons, and
am convinced that, disguise the meat as you will, the
strange, unpleasant, marsupial odor sticks when all
others vanish. Wallaby is particularly loathsome, the
animal being mostly a mass of worms. I draw the line at
native devils and tigers, also the marsupial-eat tribe.
However, I know a man who fished a drowned tiger-cat
out of a creek and stewed it up for breakfast. He said it
was very toothsome. This man’s camp was easily located
by its aroma. I have myself detected it at two miles. He
was affectionately known as Stinker Smith. He is still
living, I believe ;'but no ordinary mortal can hope to own
a digestive machine as effective as Stinker’s.

“ Robert Hap ” : A lot of “ Abo.’s ” have
been explaining the different methods of tracking
the bee that leads to trees and trouble, but none
of them tells what happened when he found the
nest. The person who led me into the business
didn’t trade the bee at all. He just roamed
about the bush in an aimless way, and stumbled
over logs and things till late in the afternoon. I
carried the 71b. axe. Suddenly he stopped at the
foot of a large tree and considered the foliage from
different points. After a while he sighed, like a
man whose labors were ended, and ordered me to
“wire in.” I applied the axe with great vigor ;
my enemy sat by and smoked. When the tree
began to sway he hastily gathered a couple of
handsful of dried grass and leaves, and commanded
me, when the tree fell, to light a fire at the hole
he indicated in one of the limbs. Then he took
the axe and delivered the few final strokes.
I made a dash to carry out his instructions, but
was met half-way by all the bees in the universe.
Talk about specks before the eyes ! I couldn’t
see an inch of landscape for bees. I was bushed
in a new world, and above the buzz and excite-
ment I could hear a distant voice urging me on.
I dropped the combustibles and dived headlong
into the branches and leaves, and wished for
peace. In time matters quieted a bit, and I
emerged swollen to twice my usual dimensions.
My late friend asked me if I didn't think it was
exciting, and I told him that it was, and that I
had enjoyed myself and would come again. Then
I went and sat in a creek till the sun went down,
and a great bee-less silence fell upon the land.

LOST IN THE MOUNTAINS.
A newly-married couple who set out to traverse the
Federal Pass, near Katoomba on Monday morning, a three
hours’ trip, did not return that night. The police were
informed, and a search was made without success. It is
feared that the couple missed the track, and have
been lost.

“ Huffin-Guffin ” : The latest form of
architectural development is wire-netting nailed
either side of Gin. timber, and the recess filled up
with mud mixed with straw, thus forming walls.
This style may be seen on the tenant farms in the
Wickli if e-road (S.W. Vic.) district. The land-
lords were opposed to the tenants using so much
real estate for their buildings until it dawned on
them that it might be an advantage to have the
land stood up on edge, as it were. After the rack-
rented farmers are starved out there will be the
more space for the sheep to feed upon. They can
graze on the sides of the houses, and if the
ordinary sheep fails at that kind of work it is
proposed to cross the jumbuclt with the domestic
fly, and produce a species of wool-bearing blue-
bottle that will eat grass off the ceiling.
HOPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Hope springs eternal ”? Well, it has a try ;
An’ him as said it might ha’ bin a digger
Who’d pottered round fer years at “ wet ” an’ “ dry,”
Dish, sluice, and “jigger.”
But if he’d had my luck on “ tucker ” fields,
An’ hoped as oft as we that he would strike a
Good thing, he’d cut “ eternal ” out; the yields
Bein’ mostly mica.
“ Hope springs eternal ” ? P’raps it do—but listen !
Yer water-bag’s bin empty ’alf a day,
An’ there’s a lake ahead that’s all a-glisten—
Hope’s dead, y’ say ?
You pads the hoof towards them great big drinks,
An’ hopes for tea and tucker at the halt ;
You dumps yer swag an’ on yer stummiek sinks—
To find it’s salt!
“ Hope springs eternal ” ? You’re a sleeper-getter,
An’ you’ve bin graftin’ on a’ ironbark patch,
Makin’ two quid a da3' —p’raps ten bob better—
A dead sweet catch !
Then, when yer cheque was growin’—in yer mind —
The Guv’mint bloke popped up and hawed and
hemmed,
An’, clearin’ out fer town, leaves word behind
They’re all condemned !
“ Hope springs eternal ” ? When all’s done an’ said,
I re’lly think the bloke wrote partly true ;
But though he hoped-a lot, he’s long since dead,
An’ me an’ you
Are still alive, and, it may be, before
We foller him, we’ve got long- .years o go,
An’ then we’ll strike him on the golden shore
(Let’s hope it’s so !).
M.L.

“ Ande ” : While hunting up information
for a spring song, I interviewed a farmer, who
told me that—
This ’ere spring-time’s all right enough in its way, but
it’s us poor farmers has to suffer. Now, look at my place,
f’rinstance. See that yeller patch in that next paddock ?
Well, three weeks ago that was a lovely straw stack ; now
there ain’t enough to bed a horse. Them blessed sparrers
set to work and pulled it to pieces,, an’ they’ve come an’
been an’ went an’ gone an’ built their bloomin’ nests in
ther house until the blarsted place creaks with ther
weight, an’ fitted up lodgin’s in the barn until we can’t
get a bag o’ stuff into it. But, first of all, mind, they
took out 70 odd bags o’ wheat I was savin’ against a rise
in the market, an’ they’re stuffin’ themselves with my
wheat, wherever they’ve stowed it. An’ there’s times
when a crowd of ’em comes home together, they darkens
the place, so we has to light the lamps an’ chase the fowls
in for fear o’ foxes. An’ we all wears waddin’ in our ears
—look ’ere—to keep us from being - deafened by the
wretches. Wot ther bloomin’ place is cornin’ to 1 don’t
know ; and it’s no good shootin’ ’em. Jim fired a barrel
into a crowd the other day, an’, of course, the dog runs
in natural-like, an’ when they’d finished failin’ we couldn’t
see no dog, an’ it took us ’arf-an-hour to get ’im out.
Poor little beggar, ’e’s lame now. An’ then ther crows
was that bad we just had ter stop. An’ then again-;
But I had to tear myself away and get back to
town.

“Ed.” : Re fighting qualities of the horse
(B. 2/7/ 05). When the Dutch first took horses
inland in South Africa, the blacks (having been
always used to animals whose business ends were
in front) repeatedly came to grief through fooling
round what they supposed to be the harmless de-
partment of the mokes.

“Jimmie Pannikin”; “ Robin X.” (B.
6/7/’O5) says “few women will forsake the heathen
for the Christian ; nine out of ten of the hawkers
hereabouts are Hindus or Syrians.” I have thrice
travelled the (50,000 square miles that lie West-o’-
Darling ; and during my many tiresome trips I
have seen only one colored hawker—an Afghan
driving a van and pair down the anabranch.
All other hawkers (and they were many) were
hard-working white men. I think the Asiatic owes
his success not so much to the weakness of the wives
of miners and coclcies as to his microscopic cost of
living. He exists largely upon the smell of an
oil-rag, and on begged or borrowed or bounced
flour. The women West-o’-Darling have not yet
forsaken the Christian, though a few in Silver
City do buy a bit of Afghan camel-carried
firewood.
“C.H.R.” ; The theodolite which couldn’t
shoot a line straighter than a bee’s flight to its
home is deplorably out of adjustment. As far as
my experience goes, a bee loaded for the home-
ward journey rises slowly to about 15ft., after
which it wheels rapidly in ever widening circles,
three or four times, and finally darts rapidly for
the nest as straight as circumstances will permit.
It all depends upon the scenery behind, or rather
that in front of, the bee, whether you can line its
flight or not. I have managed it a couple of times
successfully against a white-cloud background.
In other cases, when I have previously located the
nest, I found that a homing bee does not fly
straight. Often he puts in a 10-chain curve ; at
other times he steers an erratic course to avoid
timber, etc.
MISS JEAN MACLURCAN A YOUNG
SYDNEY SINGER.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney.
“ Wondah ” : The damming at intervals of
our inland creeks would do more to protect our
native fishes than a dozen by-laws as to the
number of hooks and the breed of worm to be
used when fishing. The Colombo Creek, near
Narrandera (N.S.W.), is an anabranch of the
Yanko, which is itself an anabranch of the
Murrumbidgee. It is dammed at intervals of
about six miles, so that there is a constant supply
of water ; and that water is teeming with Murray
cod, bream, perch, and catfish. In the Yanko,
which is not dammed, and out of which the ana-
branch comes, it is a difficult thing to catch fish,
even with a net. Besides furnishing a constant
supply of deep water, the dams prevent the sudden
freshes from stirring up the mud, and thus they
save the lives of thousands of fish, which would
be suffocated in the ordinary course of events.

“Two Seas” : “X.Y.” (B. 17/8/’O5) evi-
dently knows the ’roo-rat when he says, “ His
great crime is that he digs out the maize as soon
as it appears above ground ” ; but, in my opinion,
the beastie’s criminal masterpiece is digging up
potatoes before they appear above ground. The
first seed I put in on our selection—where ’roo-
rats were more plentiful than sheep—disappeared
in a single night. Whether they knew from ex-
perience the distance apart that spuds are usually
planted, or whether they can smell them out, I
don't know ; but I do know that they sunk their
shafts dead on to the spud every time, leaving
rows of holes which were beautiful to contemplate
for their neatness if I had only been in a fit state
to appreciate scenery. ’Koo-rats are seldom seen
by day unless the dog puts one up, or unless you
tread on the nest in a tussock. At night they are
easily shot. They make a whizzing noise when
alarmed at night, but seldom hop far away—they
just stay round and whiz.
“ 8r.J.1.” :
In Maoriland
the Chows have
taken to ‘ ‘ lab-
biting.” T’other
clay a Chinky,
having dis-
covered that
“lab biters”
were making a
“ plown ” a
day, decided to
start on the
millionaire busi-
ness. A more
opulent coun-
tryman financed
him to the
extent of 12
“tlaps,” which
he planted
carefully. Go-
ing round them
next day, the
first three held
a rabbit each,
but in the fourth
was a huge tom-cat
with which the Chow
reasoned thus-wise : “ You
keepee quite, me letee you go.
You makee trouble, me killee you.” The tom,
however, held the situation, and John had to
shift the other 11 traps elsewhere. His de-
scription of his subsequent luck was: “Me
settee tlap, labbit comee alongee. Him smellee
flap, him sclatchee tlap, then him laughee and
go away. Dam ! Changawanga-hi-ya ! Dam ! ”
Rabbiting used to be at least a free and
easy occupation. Now it drifts to wage-slavery.
In one township in N.E. Victoria local laborers
who have not capital enough to obtain an outfit of
traps are employed by landowners digging out
burrows at £1 a week and find themselves !
♦ * * *
“ Gippslander ” : Re paragraph (B. 20/7/’O5)
about a horse living eight days without food or
water. I have a bullock which lived without
either from 7th June to 28tli June, being jammed
all that time between two logs, where he could
neither move backwards nor forwards, nor
lie down. With the exception of numerous scars,
which make him look like a gridiron, he seems
none the worse.
* * * *
Samuel was a man of few words, a good
shearer, but a beggar to scamp his work. The
boss, also a man of few words, sacked him after
the second day ; but next morning Samuel was in
his place. The boss said nothing but kept handy,
and when the engine whistled and Samuel seized
his sheep, the boss was waiting for him at the
door of the pen. The boss caught the hind legs
of the sheep, while Samuel held the fore legs.
Both pulled hard in dead silence, fell and got up
again, banged against posts, rolled over, got
tangled up so that it was hard to figure out which
was boss and which was sheep, and which was
Samuel, unravelled themselves, bumped against
shearers, upset the tar-boy and his bucket, fell and
rose again, and through it all each gripped the
sheep and said nothing. At length Samuel got
the boss and the mutton under way, and by a
supreme heave rushed them in a heap into his own
stand. Then he said politely: “Thanks, boss, I
can manage him now.” “Go on, then,” said the
boss, and the men of few words dropped the sub-
ject there.
The Argus has as good as stated that it
would be satisfied with the single chamber system
of State Government in Victoria if the Upper
House were'the sole House.
Ah, my Beloved, fill the Cup. You may
just as well fill it with the purest spirit available,
Wolfe’s Schnapps is the liveliest stimulant and
best tonic known for mind or body. If your back
aches it may be your kidneys— Wolfe’s Schnapps
cures kidney diseases.*
The excuse for buying Government stores
abroad, and giving the work to outsiders instead
of to our own people, is generally that “ there was
no time to get the goods made locally.” With
locomotives, with dredges, and lately with the
£OOOO worth of infantry equipment (leather work)
required by the Australian Government, it is the
same invariable tale. There is always plenty of
time to get the goods made locally if the giving of
the order isn’t postponed to the last second, but it
seems to be left to the last second most of the
time. Then the cable become red-hot with
frenzied orders, and “there was no time to
get the work done locally.”
Commonwealth and Foreign patents ob-
tained. Collison and Co., 483 Collins-street,
Melbourne (and Adelaide.) Twenty-six years
practice. Write us for Inventors’ Guide.*
In May, 1900, there were 217 paupers
out of every 10,000 people in London ; in May,
1904, there were 238 ; in May, 1905, the figure had
gone up to 253. All over England it is stated
gloomily “ the present total of paupers is without
parallel in the records of the poor law statistics,”
and of London it is said : “Never before in the
history of the city have so many people been in
workhouses.” Freetrade has had three separate
experiences in England. First it fought against
comparative Freetrade in other countries and did
well; then it fought against low Protection and
did middling ; now it fights against High Protec-
tion, and finds its poor, busted superstition a very
thin rag to tie round its fiscal bones.
RETROSPECTIVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah, here’s the charming little spot
Where we were camped one Christmas hot,
To spend an easy holiday,
And, coming over, lost our way.
Observe this bower of maiden-hair
Decked all around with verdure rare ;
In this sequestered, calm retreat «
We buried all our gamey meat.
Behold the lovely, fern-bound creek,
How blossoms kiss its soft, cool cheek !
Here on its banks, in fairy walks,
We dug for worms with tomahawks.
And notice how, from ridge to ridge,
That fallen tree creates a bridge,
Just where the waters foam and spin
That’s where one night I tumbled in.
That grassy patch, so fresh and green,
Where flows the stream with smile serene,
Where starred clematis hangs about,
Is where our bunks were flooded out.
The clearing, over to the right,
Is where I raised a fox one night,
And firing from behind a bough,
I missed it and I shot a cow.
Ah, visiting - old scenes like these
Brings up once more sweet memories,
And makes us think, with feelinsrs glad,
Of that grand, joyous time we had.
VIC.
PERCIVAL C. COLE.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. .Name and
address must -appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted , whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
C.M. : Letter much too long. The gist of it

foot square and one square foot are the same size, though
not necessarily the same shape. A foot square is supposed
to be a square of 144 square inches. A square foot is 144
square inches of any shape. Two feet square are four
square feet; three feet square are nine square feet....
