POLITICAL
POINTS
Queensland Home Minister A irey threatens
to municipalise the milk industry as the only
effective means of preventing adulteration.
Barlow, in Queensland Leg. Council: “On
some of the sugar plantations of this State the
white employees are fed like dogs and housed like
hogs. ”
In South Africa it has been found neces-
sary to allow the white population to lay in arms
to protect their lives from the peaceful, harmless
Chinaman.
Like N.S.W., the Northern State is
having a little land trouble. Queensland’s matter
is microscopical by comparison, but yet exudes a
gamey flavor.
S. Australia is now engaged in the manu-
facture of its own locomotives. Ditto Victoria
and Queensland. But N.S. Wales is still repre-
sented by its unpatriotic press as too little
developed to get up to that manufacturing stage.
Australian defence is not being wholly
neglected. The Australian fleet, and the citizen
defence force, and the local manufacture of arms
and ammunition may still be far, far away, but
two imported guns are being mounted at Fre-
mantle.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers spoke for
hours in introducing the proposal to make
locally fiO engines—mostly a work of fitting to-
gether imported parts. How long would he
speak if it were a question of starting steel mills
to mako from local ores about 180,000 tons of iron
and steel per annum, as Canada does?
To Prime Minister Deakin, deploring the
Stinking Fish yarns about Australia in the
London papers, Josiah Thomas, by way of inter-
jection : “You ought to appoint a High Commis-
sioner.” And therein Thomas spoke the exact
truth. The need is for a man in London who will
battle for Australia—not merely for some State of
Australia.
N.S.W. Government has at last moved to
pay old age pensions through the post offices
instead of through the Bank of New South Wales
as heretofore, a saving of about £9OOO a year being
thus effected. Hitherto the fear that the Federal
Post Office would make a trifle out of the business
made the sour State Government adopt the far
more expensive device of paying through a private
bank.
THE BAYONET AND THE BOOR.
One clerical gentleman in Melbourne says it will be
necessary to bayonet the whole of Parliament to secure
Scriptural teaching in the State schools.
Well, this method has been very efficacious with
the little heathen abroad. Why not try it at
home?
Bent proposes to turn the Vic. Government
Labor Bureau into a General Employment Bureau
for private employers as well as public ones, with
a general registration fee of one shilling. That
would drive into some useful occupation a lot of
parasitic bleeders of the helpless unemployed. It
is certainly a “gross interference with private
enterprise,” but nobody has ever yet explained
what business private enterprise has to interfere
between an employer and his workman or woman,
or why it should charge a week’s wages for such
interference, as it usually does.
The Tasmanian As-
sembly has shown a proper
self-respect by fixing the
retiring age for civil ser-
vants at 70 years. In other
States we have the ab-
surdity of a law, enacted by
the aid of many legislators
of 00 and upwards, which
prohibits younger men from
serving in public offices on
the ground that they are
too old to be useful. An
aged Treasurer may seek
the vote and interest of a
younger brother who was
compulsorily retired
through old age from a
clerkship in the Treasurer’s
office several years previ-
ously. The Tasmanian
grey-beards have seemingly
been struck by the injustice
of this popular political
anomaly, and in Tasmania,
where venerables of nearly
90 protest against hasty
legislation and want Bills
hung up till they are 100, the anomaly was glaring
enough to strike anybody.
Another would-be seceder has arisen in
Queensland State Parliament—one Plunkett,
who, in all apparent seriousness, asked Premier
Morgan if he was going to take steps to sever the
State’s connection with the Commonwealth.
Morgan put on his most inscrutable countenance
and replied that he wasn’t going to start any
immediate revolution, and Plunk, wondered why
the members laughed.
Q. Treasurer Kidston’s success in bursting
up the (-reek fish monopoly in Brisbane is now
practically assured. He has got the two largest
local governing bodies to agree upon the subject,
and the other metropolitan councils have practi-
cally approved the scheme for a central market.
Kidston has a short Bill ready to trim the busi-
ness up legally, and, when it goes through, fisher-
men will get a fair deal.
A one clause Bill going through Q. Par-
liament provides that any alien’s application for a
hawker’s license is to be first submitted to the
Home Secretary, who may grant or refuse the
application. And while Q. keeps the proper sort
of Home Sec. that will be all rivht. But in view
of the fact that it may not always have the right
kind, it is much better to disqualify the colored
alien by Act of Parliament.
Premier Bent has got his State brick-
works proposal through the Victorian Parliament.
The Leg. Council tried to make trouble, but
the vote for the works was included in a
large Appropriation Bill, and the Council dared
not reject the whole measure, and could not
amend it. Thomas thus scores by a simple little
“tacking-on” device. The Tories were furious
that he did not bring down the brick-works pro-
posal in a separate Bill, but Thomas knew too
much of the ways of bears to try that.
Now that Premier Bent is at last moving
in the matter of building
electric tram-lines as
feeders to the railways,
the monopolistic Mel-
bourne Tramway Co.
raises a fearsome moan
about “ gross interference
with its rights,” “breach
of faith,” “lessening the
confidence of investors,” and
so forth. As a matter of
fact, there is in every Act
dealing with concessions to
the Tramway Co. a section
expressly authorising the
construction of as many
more tram-lines as may
be wanted by any person
or company, provided
the authority of Parlia-
ment is first obtained.
The long-headed co.
grabbed most of the
main arteries to and
from Melbourne city, but it
couldn’t grab all. Had an-
other private company
started to build an opposition
line on the proposed St.
Kilda route, from El wood to
St. Kilda station, the big
monopoly would have
fought it tooth and nail;
but, directly the
Government takes on
the contract, it wails
about a “breach of
faith ” and talks of
“compensation” for-
sooth !
It is reckoned that Free-
trade London will soon have 1000
motor busses running in its streets, 750 of
them made in Germany.
Thus Wilks, M.H.R. during the Home
Rule debate in the House of Reps.:—
The grievances of Ireland have nothing to do with us as
a nation.
Yet Wilks was returned to Parliament chiefly
because he said he was shocked at something that
happened in Ireland, over 200 years ago. Will he
now cease screaming about the Walls of Derry,
and devote himself to matters that have something
to do with us ?
N.S. W. Assembly has appointed one Select
Committee that is, at least, fairly justifiable. Its
object is to give parson Woolls-Rutledge a chance
to furnish particulars of his lie about that alleged
N.S.W. public servant who lost his position, and
couldn’t get back into the service till he joined the
R.C. church, whereupon he was promptly re-
appointed, and got a fine, large job at £lOOO a
year. The Woolls-Rutledge lie amounts to a
charge of gross corruption in the public service,
and so it is a public matter ; the usual Select
Committee to consider whether a ganger named
Jones was justified in speaking harshly to a main-
tenance man’named Smith, is in a different cate-
gory.
NERO FIDDLES WHILE ROME BURNS.
A symbol of Parliament —its jokes, jibes,
personal explanations, and want of progress.
The Russo-Jap treaty represents almost
the only case in history in which the losing party
presented the winner with an ultimatum cutting
down his huge demands almost to nothing, and
got it accepted. But apparently Japan’s funds
were getting low; it had lost tremendously in
men ; after 18 months fighting it hadn’t driven
the Russians back even into their own territory,
and it was still 5000 fearful muddy miles to
Moscow.
The Anglo-Japanese treaty has been re-
newed, on an extended basis. According to the
cabled information, the Jap now guarantees to
•protect Britain in India, and to help to maintain
British influence in Persia. There was a time
when Britain asked protection from no one, and
never dreamed of requesting any Asiatic to guar-
antee its territories, but things have changed, not
so much with the people, perhaps, as with the
“ governing classes.”
How high Japan’s ambitions are may be
guessed at from the fact that even the ingrained
habit of reverence for the Mikado, and the fero-
cious press censorship, couldn’t prevent some
papers offering the insulting suggestion that half-
mast flags should be hung out as a sign of grief at
the acceptance of the Russian peace terms. The
chief Monkey may be a gorilla of moderation, but
the great multitudinous Monkey isn’t necessarily
the same variety of ape.
When the Q. State estimates are on, there
will be wigs on the green over Tozer’s salary.
“ Stormy Petrel ” Lesina vows he will fight to the
last against the old slanderer’s re-appointment,
also that 24 others are with him on that point.
When Tozer was in Queensland recently he got a
very cold shoulder indeed from Morgan and his
colleagues, but Horace has a thick hide, and he
pocketed the affront with as much grace as possible.
Besides, the apostle of Stinking Fish knows that
£1250 per annum is not to be despised.
Gundagai (N.S.W.) Political Labor League
has passed a resolution calling upon the Labor
party to expel from its ranks any Labor M.L. A.
found to have received fees from leaseholders for
services as a land agent. Public life, it is held in
Gundagai, must be reformed. Meanwhile, muni-
cipal life in Gundagai is in a state of chaos. The
remnant of the ratepayers have struck, on the
ground that no effort is made to collect arrears
from defaulters, and those who have hitherto
paid up regularly decline to carry the whole bur-
den any longer. The Municipal Council rarely
meets, and the salaries of municipal employees
are three months in arrears. The Gundagai
P.L.L. can find matters to reform pretty near its
own door.
N.S.W. Agent-General Coghlan has been
writing to the Statist in refutation of a state-
ment that- the commercial tone of Australia is
very low clown. It would have been almost better
to have left that matter alone. In a country where
the merchants rise so extensively to protest
against a Secret Commission Bill, because it will
prevent them swindling those who trust to their
honesty, and against the Commerce Bill, which
simply prohibits a merchant selling goods under
false and lying pretences, commercial morality
has got down about as low as it can get.
If all the rest of Australia were as morally rotten
as its bumptious traders and importers, the com-
munity might almost as well give itself up as a
bad job.
“ Alba’s ” view :
The Jap., after all, is only an amateur bull-dog, some-
what slack of jaw. Look at his magnificent first three
rounds, his perfect strategy, equipment and diplomacy !
His very temperament and elan blinded the Continental
newsmongers. The illiterate Colossus of the North
was a joke; no one saw elan or strategy in him ;
there was only the old limp and the snarl,
and the infinite capacity for sitting down hard
and paying nuffin’. “ Arrest the brute,” cried the
top-note strategists. But on August 28 the Mikado and
his councillors had a visitation from the gods; and
the sword of ice, that cut Napoleon small at Moscow,
entered their vitals. And they wept. There was no
money, no wine, and a vast hlizzard-rent continent to
cross'; large spaces wrapped in Polar silence and impreg-
nable frosts. Alas for the gentlemen whose feet are sore,
and whose pockets are full of empty hands ! But history
will remember their Banzais.
Attorney-General Isaacs gave an admirable
definition of the principles of his Secret Commis-
sion Bill, when the Melbourne Chamber of Com-
merce sent along a deputation asking him to let
butter swindles and the like continue. If A hires
B to make the best bargain possible with C on
A’s account, B can take any bribe he likes from C
to betray A’s interest, provided he lets A know
that he is also paid by C, and to what extent. If
A doesn’t object all is well. If he does object,
and thinks that an agent who isn’t paid by _C will
probably make a better bargain for him with C,
then he can find another agent. If the trans-
action is clean enough to be made known the Bill
won’t interfere with it; if it is too dirty to be
revealed, then it should be made illegal, and
Isaacs intends to have it made illegal.
S. A. is hustling a little under its new Labor
Government. It proposes, when the present
Governor’s term expires, to endeavor to do away
with the expensive and useless imported State
Excellency. It has an idea of giving the long-
paralysed transcontinental railway—which now
ends in a shrieking desert at Oodnadatta —a shove
along to the more fertile regions of the M‘Donnell
Ranges. Also, it has on hand a workable Factories
Act,"a new land-settlement scheme, a measure for
increasing the graduated land-tax on large estates,
and a Bill to reduce the Legislative Council fran-
chise. Further, a private member has intro-
duced a Bill for the abolition of cigarettes.
Melbourne Argus hopefully prints a
decision by Justice Anglin of Canada that
the Dominion has no power to deport an undesir-
able, arguing that therefore the Commonwealth
has no right to deport Kanakas and may be pre-
vented from doing so by the Imperial Govern-
ment. The poor old lady will be sadly dis-
appointed if she holds to that hope of keeping her
beloved nigger. Apart from the fact that Mr.
Anglin’s decision is not yet tested by the
Canadian Full Court, the power to deport
Kanakas from Australia is expressly given in a
statute which has received Imperial approval and
is, so far as one can judge, absolutely unchallenge-
able.
According to the petition of the Employers’
Federation—
The Union Label is a weapon which may be used to
effect an unlawful discrimination against non-union
workers.
Which might be put the other way—
The absence of the Union Label is a weapon which will
he very handy as a means of effecting a discrimination
against union workers.
It is simply that one side has the courage of its
opinions and the other hasn’t. The Democratic
party reckons that people on the whole prefer
union goods, and it is willing to back its. opinion
by stamping its goods, so that if its idea is wrong
the public can refuse to buy these goods, and thus
burst up unionism. The Tory party says that the
mass of the population regards unionism as tyranny,
and would rather buy non-union goods, but it
doesn’t want to back its opinion by letting the
people know which are non-union goods. It says
the peoffie would rush non-union goods if it could
distinguish them, but it hates to let the people
have a chance to rush.
A STATUE.
The Morning Post warmly approves of the idea of a
Federal supervision over the liabilities of the Australian
States, and of the non-borrowing policy favored by the
Federal Labor Party. These proposals, it adds, will have
the general sympathy of the public in the motherland.
Not for many years have the material prospects of Aus-
tralia been so promising.— Cablegram.
Bent Thomas is straightening himself out
of late. The monopolistic Tramway Co. had lieen
talking “compensation” because His Brighton to
St. Kilda electric railway was to run past the
terminus of its line straight to the city and
Thomas was annoyed at its impudence :
The Esplanade line was not in the original Tramway
Act, and it was only when the company found what a
good thing it had missed that it got an Amending Act,
and thereby took £20,000 a year from the St. Kilda rail-
way line. Did they offer compensation to the State ? . . .
Thev talked of the Tramway Co.’s rights. . . . His troubles
what the lawyers said. Did they mean to say we were to
have no more communication except what the Tramway
Co. provided? . . . He was getting tired of these “rights.”
What would the Tramway Co. say if Parliament exercised
its right and made the company give penny sections?
The Tramway folk here shivered. There is still
plenty of time for Parliament to carry out the
manifest intention of the Tramway Act and “re-
vise ” the fares to suit travellers better and the
company’s dividends less.

Making Money
I was born on a Maine farm. X have made some
great successes in business. My greatest through
Co-operation.
I took $1,500 (.£306:2:6) capital, supplied by
Farmers, Women, Physicians, Clerks, Clergymen,
etc., in Belfast, Maine, and in an honest, but very
profitable business, earned and paid them through
BELFAST NATIONAL BANK, $5,000 (£1,020:8:2)
in Cash dividends in the first six months, $25,000
(£5,102:0:10) in cash dividends within the next
year, and in 18 months I paid them in round
numbers $330,000.00 (£67,346:19:2) CASH.
Every 4s. Earned £ 44:17s
This is HISTORY. Beads
like a fairy tale but it is
TRUE. I made poor
people rich. They helped
me earn the riches. Just
read that over again. I
had $1,500. I earned
and paid my stockholders
$330,000 in cash in 18
months. Every stock-
holder got their share.
That’s my Way of doing
business. You must be-
lieve this statement is
TRUE,for, if I were lying,
I wouldn’t tell you the
place where I earned the
money, and the BANK
where it was paid.
I have learned the great
Value of the right kind of
co-operation, learned how
to make money fast in an
honest, profitable busi-
ness from which millions
are made every year.
I now have a new busi-
ness of the same kind,
only my field is the World.
It is incorporated under
the laws or the State of
Maine and the U. S. Gov’t. The Stockholders are
absolutely protected from any liability. They are
safe guarded by law. My plan one of extended co-
operation. Stockholders everywhere who can give
me information and lend their INFLUENCE. The
dividends must be large.
I already have 4,000 stockholders in the TJ. S.,
Canada, England, Cuba, Mexico, Sandwich Islands,
Gibraltar, etc. I want a few more. The shares
are going fast. You can invest 4s. or £lOO.
Monthly payments, if you wish. It will be safe
and we will make it grow. This is no gambling
or “shady” scheme. It’s legitimate business.
You will he treated squarely and honestly.
I place 30 years of untarnished business record
behind that statement. I only ask you in your
own interest to INVESTIGATE. You shall have all
the Proof you want. References, Bankers, Bus-
iness Men, Church and Public Officials, etc. Send
your address on a postal card. It will pledge
you to nothing. It may mean your fortune. I
will send a 24-page book,
h Guide to Full Pockets,”
FREE. I will pay the postage. Don’t be “A Brother
to the Ox.” Stop plodding. Lift your head long
enough to ask me to prove every statement in this
ad. This is your opportunity, don’t miss it. Don’t
Wait if you want something betterthanyouhavegot.

Rushcutters’ Bay and Paddington was opened
with a flourish of rhetorical trumpets by the Jaw-
bone Warrior as Neild Avenue; and now the
irreverent inhabitants thereabouts call it Sewer
Road, and the greatness of its origin is forgotten.

the General Manager of the Bank of New Zealand, Wel-
lington, New Zealand, and marked “ Tender for Shares,”

All Shares carry the right to any dividend hereafter
declared for any period subsequent to 31st March, 1905.

have been complied with.
We report that we have verified the Cash, Investments, Bills of Exchange, and other Assets in London, and have

ana the Books and Accounts of the London Office, we are of opinion that the above Balance Sheet is a full and fair

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Australian Rugbvites met the first All-
Maoriland team in Tahuna Park, Dunedin, Saturday - last.
It was the only decent football ground that wasn’t under
water, and 36 hours of Dunedin’s own special brand of
downpour had made even it very boggy. There was a
cold drizzle, and a dreary wind howled straight down the
ground, and helped the Australians in the first spell. In
spite of the wind and rain in their faces the Maorilanders
attacked strenuously, and smothered in an amazing
manner, and at half-time the score was 3 all. When
the Maoi danders got the wind and the rain behind them
they started out in earnest, but the Australians made a
much better defence than was anticipated. In the scrums
they - seemed to be able to hold their own, but the drib-
bling charges of the Maorilanders went through them,
and the game ended in a win for Maoriland by 14 to 3.
It was bad enough, but not so bad as was anticipated.
* • * *
The hardest work clone in Sydney just now is
playing Rugby football these summery Saturday after-
noons. Last week-end, whole teams threatened to melt
away, and the tired feeling was much in evidence. South
Sydney still leads the competition with 27 points, and
Glebe is second with 26. There doesn’t seem to be much
chance of the position being altered in any case. South
Sydney has still to play Balmain, which will not make a
decent killing, and North Sydney, which has no possible
human hope of holding its own against the Southerners.
Glebe, on the other hand, has to meet the formidable
Newtown Club, and anything may happen. It has also
to meet Sydney District, but the City has no hope. How-
ever, the crux of the position is that neither North Sydney
nor Balmain has a hope against South Sydney, and, un-
less someone can down S. Sydney - , Glebe is out of it; so,
under these conditions, and considering the difficulty of
getting playing grounds, it is possible that the round may
be declared closed, with South Sydney premiers for the
year, without further cruelty to animals.
* < . * #.
As previously indicated there were signs of
apoplexy among the Rugbyites on Saturday - afternoon.
The match that was looked forward to was that between
Glebe and University - , but the heat and the weight of the
Glebe forwards broke the athletic y'oung gentlemen from
the Uni. right up, and they were bowled over, trampled
on, and rubbed in the dust, Glebe wiping out all former
defeats with the crushing score of 31 points to 11. The
rest of the clubs played more or less half-heartedly in the
warm sp:ing weather, and public interest seemed dead
and cremated, and its ashes bottled. Western Suburbs
downed Sydney by 13 to 3, and North Sydney went under
to Balmain by 6to nil; and out at Erskineville Newtown
rolled over Eastern Suburbs by - 16 to nil.
* • * *
By its defeat of South Melbourne, on Saturday,
Carlton reft from the red-and-white all hope of com-
peting in the semi-finals of Vic. League Football; these
lie now with Collingwood and Carlton, and ’Roy and
Essendon —winners to play off. The whole eight clubs
play next Saturday, but to four of them it doesn’t
matter a land-agent’s reputation who wins or loses.
South’s downfall was a rout, half-time scores being
Carlton 5 goals 13 behinds to South’s 3 goals 2 be- .
hinds ; finish 92 points to 49. Some 24,000 spectators,
expecting a close struggle, suffered the heavy showers
up to half-time, then anathematised all and sundry
and went home. The heavy red-and-whites could
make no show against Carlton’s lighter, trickier,
and smarter men, and when they finally tried
to rely on main force the game became a mere
rough,’ unscientific scramble. ’Roy sent a strong
team down to Geelong, which club, having
already lost all chance, skirmished up a number of
supposed second-raters —and was considerably surprised at
winning, by 68 points to 66 ; but then ’Roy had no induce-
ment to “ burst itself.” The day previous the League had
perpetrated the pleasing joke of suspending a South
play - er for one week for striking an opponent during a
match ; and in the Richmond v. Footscray Association
game the umpire had to stop the game twice while he
rebuked players who imagined they were in a 24ft. ring
instead of on a football ground. (A dirty left is always a
desirable thing in an umpire—his decisions may not
be too sound, but they are much better respected.)
In the Association “North” still leads, while Rich-
mond, Williamstown and “ West” strenuously endeavor
to keep within coo-ee, with a view to getting in the semi-
finals. By the way, the brick combine seems to have
made those articles so scarce around Preston —where the
works are—that the barrackers have no handy means of
slaying the local players. Preston has so far won one
game out of 17, and on Saturday erected the noble score
of 7 points against Williamstown’s 75. But possibly the
scarcity of employed brickmakers is responsible for the
scarcity of footballers. Ikey Mo. fills a fair portion of his
book with football wagers just now ; writer took 2 to 1
against South on Saturday, and was sorry afterwards, but
he couldn’t see the force of laying 5 to 2 Carlton.
Another Ikey offered 10 to 1 against Melbourne—playing
Collingwood—which piece of generosity was sufficiently
amazing to paralyse all backers.
X * * *
Of the Sydney British Association clubs, Pyr-
mont takes the football premiership with 25 competition
points. Behind are Rozelle, Glebe and Balmain, with 22,
19, and 16 points respectively. Somewhere behind them
are four other clubs that don’t count, as only the leading
four play off for the Gardiner Cup, and the destination of
that bauble will be decided during the next three weeks.

The great Australian race problem seems mostly
how to stop the favorite.
* * * *
“Oxbury” Spring meet, at Clarendon (Satur-
day), was only fairly attended. Sydney didn’t supply its
customary complement, and local people, as usual, stayed
away almost to a man. Frost-killed grass and evidence
everywhere of want of rain rendered the trip less cheerful
than usual, and the flint-like nature of the race-track
possibly made much difference to some horses, but ancient
Lucknow, winner of the big event (the Hawkesbury Spring
Handicap, ljm.), never showed better. Lucknow (hump-
ing 9.12) started second favorite (5 to 2 against) and won
all the way, beating first favorite Tatterdemalion by half
a length, a performance that makes him appear a more
than possible winner of next Saturday’s Epsom or
Monday’s Metrop. He has 9st. in one and Bst. 101 b. in the
other. Lucknow’s is another case in support of the con-
tention of observant people that horses last longer and
shape better if not raced too young. Lucknow’s early
days were spent between the shafts of a Western N.S.W.
clergyman’s buggy. ■ -
M.L.’s great hack-race performer Maniopoto is
voted by many particularly choice for the Epsom Handi-
cap, but several good judges won’t have him spite of his
hair-raising track gallops, generally accomplished with
light shoes. Last year Grand Rapids (another Maori-
lander) did as well as Maniopoto is doing now in his w - ork
—he covered the mile (fairly wide out) in 1.44 J, and yet
was not placed in the race. If it was certain Machine
Gun could go the distance, he would look a specially good
thing on his display in Tramway Handicap (6fur.), at
Tatts., a fortnight or so back, for his Spring distance
doings place him in the same class as were Marvel,
Sequence and co. But, great goer though he be oyer
6fur., there is considerable fear that the other quarter
mile will beat him. Lord Fitzroy has been doing at least
as good work as any other nag at Randwick ; his gallop
last Thursday had more merit in it than Maniopoto’s, but
he is not reliable. Lucknow’s Randwick, Rosehill, and
Hawkesbury form compels attention, and it will be no
surprise if he upsets the favorites. Warroo (improving
fast) is another who must be considered, ditto Famous
and Boabdil (last Saturday’s easy winner of Caulfield
Hemsie Stakes). All things considered, Maniopoto or
Lord Fitzroy should win, with Famous, Warroo and
Machine Gun close up.
*•• • .
At moment of writing there are 34 still in
the Metrop., to be run second day (Monday) of coming
A.J.C. Spring meeting, but it is certain that demand
for final forfeits will see some weeding out. S.yd-
ney Cup winner Tartan looks the pick of the lot. No
other horse has gone through a more thorough prepara-
tion, and he is still thriving so well that owner and
trainer expect him to strip better than ever before in his
career. This is saying a great deal. P.K. is another
sure to run well if he starts. Marvel Loch is more
likely to succeed in the Metropolitan (which event
she won easily two years ago as an outsider) than in the
Epsom, and must be classed among the most promising of
those in the long race. Fitz Grafton’s track doings do not
recommend him, but he shapes a lot better with the
colors up—as evidence his good race in Marvel Loch’s
record-breaking Rawson Stakes a few weeks ago. So far
Victorian trainer Scobie’s contingent has not been backed
to any extent, but the one the stable comes for (perhaps
Sylvan King) will get a heap of support from punters
generally. The winner appears to be among those men-
tioned, and Tartan, P.K., or Marvel Loch may scoop the
pool.

Torah’s win of S.A.J.C. Derby (Friday) was
anticipated. Previous performances made him clearly
the best of his age in S. Australia. Torah appropriated
Fulham Park Plate, Turretfield Stakes, Richmond Park
Stakes, S.A. Stakes, and Parkside Stakes, and was only
twice unplaced. For the Derby long odds were laid on
him—investments totalling £464 10s., while next best
backed was Staccato £6O Iss. Practically Torah was
never out of a canter at any stage, and he won with
absurd ease. Tote paid £1 3s. for every £1 specked.

A Wellington bookmaker had a treble on the
three big things of recent Maoriland Grand National
meet, and the correct trio (Kremlin, Innislrillen, and
Creusot) were not struck till 1300 bets had been made. It
isn’t stated how many hundreds of wagers were laid after
the 1300 bets were made and the correct combination
picked. The man who tries to back a treble is in need of
a protector.
« » * •
Ancient John Young, “the father of bowls,”
has again been nominated as president of theN.S.W.
Bowling Association, and anyone who would have the
temerity to oppose the Nestor of the game deserves to be
dragged at the tail of a wild animal round Sydney from
one bowling green to another. It was Mr. Young, who
went to England and placed the game upon an interna-
tional basis, and subsequently captained the first Aus-
tralian team of bowlers to invade the Gold Country. On
the subject of bowls the evergreen veteran can talk till
the milk-boy calls in the gray hours of the morning, and
when the Sydney City Council recently ukased that the
clubs must pay rent for the greens which had been laid
down in public parks, the old gentleman made “ a pro-
nouncement ” on the matter that showed how wide was
the knowledge he possessed of the game. He pointed out
that the London County Council had established eight
bowling greens in various parts of the imperial city,
that all through Scotland special facilities are granted
to clubs to establish greens on municipal and public
lands, and in Ireland the city of Belfast has allowed two
bowling greens to be established free of cost in the public
parks. Also in the U.S.A., New York, Boston, Chicago,
and ’Frisco, all have greens, free of rent, in their public
parks, and all through the Canadian Dominion the clubs
have been encouraged to lay down greens in the people’s
breathing spaces, gratis. Even the gentle Jap. plays
ye ancient game, and in Kobe and Yokohama there are
greens in the civic domains, for which no rent is collected,
and the same rule obtains in the English part of Shanghai,
and in Kowloon, opposite Hong Kong. In none of these
cities, of course, are the bowlers allowed to charge for
admission, or prohibit the entry of the public, but the
caretakers have power to prevent Vandals in hobnailed,
or any other kind of boots, playing kiss-in-the-ring on the
green.
Bowls is an ancient game, but it is only in com-
paratively modern times it has managed to clothe itself
with the atmosphere of respectability that makes it the
ideal pastime of old gentlemen of unusual development
about the bullion. It was played in England as far back
as the 12th century, and in those days seems to have
ranked with two-up, and for several hundred years a
bowling-green or alley was a “ place ” within the mean-
ing of the Act. In the reign of Henry 111. it was among
the games “dishonorable, useless and unprofitable”
which were forbidden the clergy. Richard 11. didn’t
object to the toffs of the day bowling a little—they had
no morals, anyhow—but the common people, who had to
be kept in the path they should go, were forbidden to
indulge. Henry IV., Edward IV., and even dissolute old
Harry VIII., legislated against the game and strove to stamp
it out. Then Harry weakened, and allowed “ artificers,
servants and the like ” to play during Christmas time in
the grounds of and under the supervision of their masters ;
and the masters paid a license of £IOO per annum to
be allowed to have a private bowling green. Mary,
who seems to have been over-zealous in caring
for the spiritual welfare of her subjects, cancelled
all these licenses, but, like two-up, the vice seemed
to thrive on adversity. Old Harry himself seems t,o have
trundled a bit when he wasn’t busy marrying, or murder-
ing his wives, and Charles the Headless was an enthusi-
astic bowler, so the laws against the game fell into disuse
till the reign of George 11. Then they were revived with
a bang, but only put into operation against bowling
alleys ; open greens were not interfered with, for the very
good reason that only the aristocracy could afford to con-
struct greens. The present-day system of bowling clubs
dates from the beginning of the 19th century, and the
pioneer was the Willowbank Club of Glasgow.

The opening day of the world’s cycling cham-
pionship meeting at Antwerp was a triumph for England,
which annexed the sprint and long-distance amateur .
championships, and the 100 kilometres amateur-paced
championship. The last was won by Meredith in the
record time of lhr. 80min. 45 I-ssec. One hundred kilo-
metres is 62 and one-tenth English miles.
* * •» *
The big Maoriland Dunlop cycle road race
(Timaru to Christchurch) saw 171 starters put their heads
down and struggle through'the rain and wind over heavy
roads. J. Arnst, the scratch man, burst a tyre, and
was apparently thereby oiited; and Mudgway, the
Wellington rep., broke a chain; so the fastest time and
first place went to the same man—P. M. O’Connor, of
South Canterbury. The distance is 100 miles, and
O’Connor got over it in 6hr. 57min.
The world’s hour motor-cycle record now stands
at 54 miles 523 yd., which was put up by C. R. Collier,
one of the English team of motor-cyclists who recently
had such a run of bad luck in the “ Little Gordon Ben-
nett race ” in France.
* * * *
“ Maorilander ” : As you say in a recent issue,
thej’ are road racing mad in Maoriland ; 274 entries were
received for the Dunlop race from Timaru to Christchurch
on Sept. 2. For weeks before that date, the road was
obliterated by the seething mass of riders. It looked like
the Black Hole of Calcutta on wheels. There were seven
eliminating tests to select representatives ; in these there
were over 400 competing. The road to Timaru was one
huge track. Cyclists lived on it, and patted down every
little lump, and picked the stones off. In the roadside
pubs enormous quantities of beer were drunk and world’s
records put up. It is rumored that another Tyre Cos. is
going to start road racing in Maoriland over a longer
course than the Timaru-Christchurch one. Prizes will be
all golden sovereigns. It would be good sport if the two
companies ran their races same day, same course and
same time. Then it would look as if the whole Island
was crawling and the road a mammoth centipede.
Cyclist MacFarland is bringing with him as
riding companion the new Amurkan “ marvel,” W. S.
Fenn, who, by the latest turn of the wheel, is the “ Han-
dicap King.” He holds nearly all the world’s competi-
tion records up to 10 miles, and has only been beaten by
the mighty Kramer himself. Indications are that this
season Austral tracks will be over-run by an army of
hungry free-lances from the five corners of the earth.
* * * *
Kieran’s winning of 500 yd. supremacy of Eng-
land at Leeds in 6min. 7sec. should close the mouths of
carping Britishers who scoffed at Australian watches and
watch-holders. The time is 3 3-ssec. better than the
same swimmer’s previous world’s record, accomplished at
Bronte baths, Sydney, less than seven months ago.
Though the cable is silent on the subject it is certain
Billington, Forsyth (w! iner last year), and Taylor were
behind Kieran, as late English papers contain references
to their prospects in the 500 yd. event, based on what each
did in the 440 yd. Northern Championship. Only other
big race to be decided now is 220 yd. supremacy at Hornsey
on the 26th inst. ; then Kieran will probably return to
Australia, accompanied by Billington, whom English
swimming people talk of sending out here. *
Annette Kellermann has diplomatically post-
poned her third attempt to swim from Dover to Calais —
until next year. Asa matter of fact, none of the recent
attempts have amounted to more than half of Captain
Webb’s great feat.
* » * *
A Licensed Victuallers’ Tournament, although
it comes late in the season, is attracting some attention
at Alcock and Co.’s billiard-hall in Melbourne. A
Markers’ tournament at the same hall was a failure as a
show. The men had no followings of friends and admirers
as compared with the “bungs,” many of whom play a
better game, and have a better nerve than most of the
markers. Besides, there is a sporting element in their
competition, whereas a struggle between two ordinary
billiard-room Chorleys is viewed with deep distrust if a
few pounds depend on the issue.
* * * *
The Melbourne publican’s Indian minah, which
acquired some celebrity a few months back by bringing
about the identification of the hotel cook as a person
entitled to a considerable fortune, proves himself to be a
veritable mascot, and is so much desired on that account
that the proprietress begins to see the necessity of con-
fining him in a burglar-proof cage. Luck has even come
the way of the youth who was recently in difficulties over
the stealing of the bird. After doing the term fixed by
law, he called round at the pub., and complained
bitterly that he was a misused young man, having been
entirely guiltless. He asked for compassion, and a little
cash to help him on his way. The bar lady sprung a few
shillings, five of which the youth invested in a sweep
ticket and promptly landed £4OO. The result is that the
bird’s price has gone up to £IOO, and superstitious backers
of horses are rushing the place just to touch his head or
try and steal a feather.
* * * *
“Melb.”: A day of “Bizarre Sports” is
advertised at Sandridge. One is the German picnic game
of “ Winning the Rooster,” when a he-fowl is put under
an earthern pot, and the ladies throw sticks at it.
Smasheress gets him.
* * * *
Up to the middle of July the Australians had
only three batsmen included in a list of the best 25 aver-
ages for the English season. Armstrong and Noble were
second and fifth on the list respectively, and Trumper
figured at the tail end. But, as showing that averages
dealing with big - figures only are not reliable guides to
“ form,” it may be mentioned that neither Jackson nor
Maclaren, at that time, had made sufficient runs to be
listed at all. The bowling averages are a truer indication
of merit. Here it appeared that among the men who had
done most work, Haigh and Rhodes were somewhat better
than Laver (the best of the Australians), and Lees, Ker-
mode, Brearley and Relf were a little better than Arm-
strong.
* * * *
“B.” : Recent discussion re alternating the
choice of innings in test matches instead of tossing each
time, has received further point by the fact of Jackson
winning five successive tosses. Admitting that the English
cricketers are this season better than the Australians, it
is no sort of a “ test ” when one side has the choice of con-
ditions right through the season. When the new idea
was mooted out here, one plea advanced against it was
that the clever push might first rig the betting market,
and then (knowing who was going in first) proceed to rig
the wicket also before play started. It was a foolish plea,
for the same rigging of the wicket is possible on, say, the
second or third day, when it is equally known what side
will be batting. On reaching England the Australians
are reported to have raised the question again, but this
time the Englishmen objected—and quite naturally.
* * * *
The I. Zingari cricket club, which models itself
on an English namesake, still exists. Originally formed
at Potts Point, the club was intended to be the refuge of
the pure-minded amateur of good social standing, and
promised to be somewhat of a force, many good players
joining at its inception. The success of the electorate
system, however, clipped its wings, and the I. Zingari has
now no real playing strength. It follows the common or
plebeian cricket club in one respect by nominating 16
vice-presidents.
The baseball clubs in and around Sydney that
struggled for the first-grade supremacy numbered ten,
and Petersham carried off the doubtful honor with 24
competition points, Woollahra being second with 20.
After that the rest were nowhere, Paddington, which
has four times carried off the premiership, scoring
only eight competition points. Even then it was third on
the list. This coming Saturday a Melbourne team will
try conclusions with Sydney's best. The redoubtable
Dr. Twiss will be among the invaders, and the diaphragm
shields, the frying-pan gloves, and the rest of the weird
paraphernalia are being carefully tested before Victoria’s
terrible pitcher gets loose on the wearers.
* * • *
Thirsting for fresh conquests, Vic. cross-country
champion G. Blake went over to Tasmania the other day
and mopped up the ’Speck’s ten miles cross-country cham-
pionship in 63min. ISsec. Schofield, of Hobart, and
Thompson, of Launceston, were second and third respect-
ively.

As a sport boxing is booming just now in
Sydney. All over the place there are amateur clubs,
calling themselves by euphonious names, such as the
Something Pastime Oiub, the Sons of Rest Athletic Club,
and so forth, in which fairly big purses are fought for and
large side-bets are announced, and this writer never doubts
the word of any professional, or even good amateur,
fighter about side-bets.

That cheerful coon slugger, Joseph Walcott,
Esq., was not in his usual condition on the occasion of his
disturbance with Mr. Paddy Martin at the Gaiety on
Saturday night last. The gentlemen who knew all about
it laid 6t04“0n ”in favor of the colored brudder. For
the first four rounds Walcott’s superior knowledge of the
game seemed to keep him slightly in front, and then the
white man began to land a few in the vicinity of Wal-
cott’s food gateway. By the eighth round Walcott was
done. He was badly battered, and he was exhausted, but
he managed to avoid a knock-out, and kept going some-
how till the 16th, when his seconds waved the towel of
defeat.

“Snell” : Jack Wren, Melb.’s Napoleon of the
totes, isfiguringnow as a patron of pugilism, and hasthreat-
ened to dig the sport out of the bin of obloquy, and make
it popular and respected again. He secures the Exhibi-
tion for a heavy-weight thumping match where others
have failed, and the winner of the mill is to meet the
world’s champion, Jimmy Jeffries, whose terms for an
Australian scrap—£soo for ex’s, and a £2OOO purse—have
been accepted by the Oollingwood sport. It is not easy
to see how Wren is going to get his money back out of
this enterprise, but that is his own affair. There is
nobody in Australia who seems to be within three moves
of Jeffries’ class, but the world’s champion would draw in
exhibition spars up against Squires, Felix, M‘Call, and
perhaps a couple more. In a serious engagement Jim
would probably have anybod3 - we have to offer beaten
before he entered the ring. He had announced his per-
manent retirement from the game when Wren’s offer
reached him. Perhaps he does not regard what he will
have to do in Australia as calculated to break in on his
permanent retirement.
* * * *
Victorian golf championship struggle ended on
Saturday under adverse conditions —a hurricane of wind
in the morning, and pouring rain in the afternoon. Fri-
day’s play left W. Bruce wish an advantage over Scott and
Riddell, and three holes from the finish of Saturday morn-
ing’s round with Scott, Bruce had added one to his lead.
Then he broke up, losing three strokes through a bad
drive and mistakes at the 16th and 17th holes. Afternoon
saw Scott start with three strokes lead on Bruce and five
on Riddell, and, in spite of weather and range of holes, he
went round in 78 —which was one more than his Victorian
record—-and won handily. Bruce was 17 behind on the
aggregate.
* * * *
“Two Seas”: The owner who scratches his
horse because he is not offered a “ cut ” in the sweep is
becoming so frequent that the question arises: “ Why
does ‘ Tattersall ’ play into his hands by continuing the
absurdity of making his chief prizes depend upon the
result of a horse race ? ” Why not have all cash prizes.
The advantages to those who support the consultations
would be obvious enough. First and foremost, the man
who, after drawing - blanks for twenty years, has the luck
to draw first prize could not be blackmailed by owner or
jockey. He would get his cash —all of it —straight awajq
without the danger of dropping dead with the excitement
of seeing his horse lose by a nose. There is at least one
policeman less in Victoria through the present absurd
system of including horses in the gamble. He dropped
dead. If there is any reason why the horses, owners,
jockeys and “whisperers ” should be included save to give
them all a chance to blackmail, I—a subscriber from the
first —would like to hear it.
Effete Yankeedom runs British aristocratic
inefficiency pretty close. When New York’s
crack regiment (composed mainly of wealthy
noodles) recently encamped for training purposes,
each two privates had a tent between them, the
tent was electrically lighted, each warrior had a
valet and shoeblack in attendance, and the '‘mess”
was presided over by gorgeous chefs from the
Brooklyn clubs !
Bigjohn Forrest, in delivering his Budget,
gave a nasty slap in the eye to his old Tory friends
when he said that the men who were continually
libelling Australia were those who owed most to
it. John is quite right there. Nearly all the
leading Stinking Fish personages are of humble
origin. But they went into retail trade before the
invention of cash registers, or boodled a contract,
or were lucky in a land deal, and now are rich, and
raucously abuse the land that gave them wealth
when, in many cases, it should have given them a
big stone gaol.

The Public Prosecutor has now become
Eart of N.S.W. police court system, and the
igher courts have always possessed such an
official. But there is no sign yet of the Public
Defender. While this is so the legal system is out
of balance, The accused who cannot pay a
defender has the machinery of the court against
him, and magistrate and Judge are, in the main,,
driven by machinery.
Penrith district (N.S.W.) has put up some-
thing of a record in deceased inhabitants. Last
week, there died Wm. Moxham aged 73 years,
resident in the district 55 years ; Martin Eisenhult
aged 77, a 50 years resident; George Clissold
aged 79, a resident 50 years; Daniel McLaugh-
lin aged 84, a 40 years resident; and G.
B. Minton, aged 72, who had dreamed away
53 years in that locality. M'Laughlin was
once weigh-master at the Haymarket, Sydney,
when all produce was taken there. The “ market ”
was an open space from George-street to Elizabeth-
street, the eastern end being the Pound. The
Market house, a red brick building, was on the
western end ; it had a clock tower, the clock now
doing duty at the Fish-market in Woolloomooloo.
The market house was sometimes used as'a theatre.
In those days Belmore Park was a dust heap with
a stream running through it from Surry Hills.
If the Deakin Government insists on a
census being officially taken before the House of
Reps, makes changes in State representation, it
will fall into line with the practice in the United
States. There the number of Reps, is based on
the last official census, not on interim estimates
of statisticians.
A Sydney obituary — that of Septimus
Stubbs Garling—recalls the first Garling, an
English solicitor, whom the British Government
induced, with W. H. Moore, another Gentleman
One, to come to Sydney in 1814, as there were
then no “respectable” members of the legal
profession in Sydney. Up to that date the
lawyers practising in Sydney had come out in
bond.
The Yic. municipal elections were remark-
able for the complete rout of the Labor party’s
candidates. This was most conspicuous in such
democratic and Labor centres as Richmond, Col-
lingwood, South Melbourne, and parts of St.
Kilda municipality. In face of the special appeal
of the Political Labor Council, this repulse re-
quires the attention of the Labor party, and seems
to show a reaction against the extreme views
which so marked the Victorian delegation at last
Political Labor Conference.

“ O’Fipp ” : Bent s dominance of the Vic.
State Ministry is shown by the fact that only
about one Victorian in 20 could tell you anything
further than that Bent is the Ministry. One in
50 might be able to add the names of Murray and
Swinburne, though Swinburne is looking up now,
and soon one person in 25 will know about him.
On the other hand, the tail fragment of the
Ministry is growing even more remote from public
knowledge.
The 1904 return under the Aliens Immi-
gration Restriction Act is not an encouraging
document. During 1904, 118 colored folk were
asked to pass the test; one, a Chinese, succeeded ;
117, including 78 Chinese, failed, and were ex-
cluded. No Japanese ivas asked to pass the test.
In all 2802 persons of colored races were admitted
without tests, and these included 847 Chinese, 461
Japanese, and 469 Malays. The majority of the
Japs (419) were admitted as pearlers.

ALL BRANCHES AND BOOKSELLERS.
“ G.H.” : The shriek that Joke Cook
raised in the House of Reps, because Attorney-
General Isaacs accepted a retainingjfee from the S. A.
Government in connection with the Murray River
reminds that the wage of the Australian Attorney-
General doesn’t represent the income of a first-
class lawyer, or anything dimly resembling it.
Isaacs has practically lived in harness from the
day he took office. Look at his work in connection
with the Trade Marks Bill, Secret Commission
Bill, &c., &c. And his remuneration amounts to
less than a sixth part of £12,000 a year. The
British Attorney-General gets £7000 a year in a
sold lump, and other fees and emoluments bring
the total up to about £13,000. Of course Britain
is a richer country, but the work of its Attorney-
General is no heavier.
Osborn and Jerdan, of Sydney, the other
day got a letter from Donoughmore, Ballyragget,
Ireland, asking for ‘ 4 a catalogue of your phono-
graphs, which I have seen advertised in The
Bulletin.” Not bad business to drag a phono-
graph order 12,000 miles to Australia.*
Everybody has noticed how vastly Tommy
Bent’s politics have improved of late. But everybody
does not know that said improvement dated from Thomas’s
first visit to Daly’s reconstructed Turkish Baths, Royal
Arcade, Melbourne. Thomas breathed fervent blessings
on the improvements in the Baths, as he lay smiling on
the marble slab. Since then the blessings have returned
to him and sweetened his political career. What’s the
old adage about one clean politician being nearer to
godliness than a whole row of Wesleyans?*
When the terms of peace were announced,
the papers, which had been lying so persistently
about the real drift of the struggle, had to explain
their lies by saying that Japan had yielded so
much because of its tremendous chivalry and
humanity. Now they are reporting that Jap
leaders are prophesying that they will be stoned
or dynamited when they go home. Stones and
dynamite—chivalry and humanity !
AT POVERTY POINT.
Ballarat’s South-street noise factory shows no sign of
wear. Entries for the forthcoming competitions in
drones, yowls, howls and blares are said to beat all
previous records. N.S.W. entries alone total over 400,
including a choir of 00 voices and a band of 50 performers
from Newcastle, another from Bathurst, and several from
Sydney. The amount of energy and cold cash wasted in
these foolish affairs is enormous ; the only return is the
encouragement of the tiresome, precocious singing or
reciting kid.
Miss Georgie Musgrove, a daughter of Harry Musgrove,
well-known in theatrical circles, gained her opportunity
in Adelaide and stepped from the chorus ranks into the
part of Zelie in “ The Orchid,” which Miss Nellie Wilson
had vacated through indisposition.
Melba associated herself very actively with the benefit
matinee given at Oovent Garden to Madame Bauermeister,
the “ old woman ”of grand opera in London. The sou-
venir programme had portraits of Bauermeister and
Melba, and it was “the Australian prima-donna” who,
after carrying the bouquets to the heroine of the hour,
held a bunch of crimson roses over her head. Anything
in the nature of a “spree” appeals to the tempestuous
Queen of Song.
A recent Bulletin item about an old-time Melbourne
clown, who was drawn across the stage in a chariot to
which were harnessed a multitude of cats, has evoked
replies from two well-known actors. No. 1 states that the
clown was Fred Darbyshire, who afterwards joined Stanley
and took a juvenile co. to England long before the Pol-
lards were heard of. The cats were stolen for him by
Billy Edinger, property man, who died in ’75. (It was
Billy who told the story to No. 1.) Correspondent No. 2
remembers distinctly that the clown in question was Tom
Lewis—this second correspondent was playing in the
panto, in question and thinks it was “ Riquet of the
Tuft,” if Tiie Bulletin has deciphered it aright. He
offers no theory as to who stole the cats, in which
respect he is less interesting than No. 1. Anyhow,
there were only six or eight cats (Thomas variety),
and they didn’t draw the chariot ; it was dragged
by an invisible rope, and the cats were dragged also,
though they dug their claws into the stage and held on,
and sassed the management, and called wildly for their
hack salary. The act was abandoned after the first night,
according to correspondent No. 2—correspondent No. 1
reckons it was after the second night—it being con-
demned by the manager as an evidence of man’s in-
humanity to cat. Correspondent No. 1 adds as an addi-
tional reason that almost every woman in Melbourne who
had ever lost a cat descended on the theatre to demand
the restitution of her pussy. Anyhow, it is a good cat
story.

Edward Dyson.

Managers of the Yiddish theatres in the Bowery, New
York, have found a way of pleasing playgoers who want a
“happy ending” to a drama as well as those who prefer
consistent, tragedy. In their version of “Romeo and
Juliet,” for instance, the curtain falls on the regulation
last act with both lovers poisoned, and the “artistic end-
ing” playgoer leaves the theatre. Then Friar Laurence
presumably administers an emetic, for the curtain rises
on another act of pure farce, wherein Mrs. Romeo Mont-
ague (nee Capulet) joins with her husband in the cake-
walk and two-step.
Premier Carruthers, setting Paddington
Town Hall clock going the other day, fixed one
eye on the local brewery and the other on the
inspector of nuisances and maundered
Alluding- again to the Local Government measure,
Mr. Carruthers asked them in their consideration of the
proposal to take up their “ Pilgrim’s Progress ” and read
the chapter referring to the Man with the Muck Rake. His
head was always down, whereas if he had looked up he
would have seen an angel. The measure would give a
crown of glory to people who wished to practise self-
reliance and self-help.
The man who talks this kind of Pecksnifflanguage
is always worth avoiding.
Michael Norton, the patriarch of Gunda-
gai (N.S.W.), who has just died, aged 85, was,
although present at the great Gundagai flood of
1852, as shaky about the exact number of persons
drowned as most of the records of the time. The
number is Variously stated at from 60 to 89. Gun-
dagai had another great flood eight years earlier,
when some lives were lost, and the oldest inhabi-
tants gradually came to merge the two floods and
the two lots of corpses, and often multiplied both
the waters and the dead by two.
The N.S.W. Liquor Bill makes the licensee
responsible for drunkenness or quarrelling on his-
premises, and doesn’t allow the defence that he
W'as.unable to prevent the trouble. A man may
enter in a drunken condition, and a fight may
start without any adequate notice. And how
about those times when the push comes in and
demands free drinks, and then wrecks the premises
and half kills the landlord? Will the battered
licensee be arrested in the hospital because there
was a disturbance in his house and he was under-
neath, with the push on top of him ?
Motorists are in arms against the N.S.W.
Inflammable Liquids Bill. The quantity of oil
which the Bill proposes to allow to be stored is so
small and the conditions of storage are so stringent
that they reckon the first motor that passes will
buy up ail the oil in a bush town, and the second
one will be left stranded there. After all, the
need for the proposed Bill isn’t very apparent.
. When oil is the cause of fire it seldom happens
that the fire arises through the storage of 60
gallons of the fluid ; mostly it is through someone
capsizing the half-pint in the lamp.
A distinguished trainer of lions says that the performer
with them must be an individual of high moral character
in every way, or the animals will find it out, and the per-
former will not be able to control them. The Man Who
Did and the Woman with a Past can’t tame lions.

“H.” : The burning of two of Sydney’s
churches in one day is a reminder of the ex-
traordinary destruction by fire of the bluestone
East Melbourne Trinity Church of England last
year, on the very Sunday that the new incum-
bent, pastor White, took possession. He preached
there in the morning—not a combustible sermon,
either—and the fire broke out about an hour after.
I have not heard of another stone or brick church
being burnt in Australia, except St. Mary’s,
Sydney.
THE KISS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Divorce Court was rendered temporarily in-
capable of performing its work when Mrs. Maesmore
Morris, who secured a decree nisi, administered a full
luscious kiss to the court Bible on entering the witness-
box.
We’d seen her in the “ drahmmer,”
But her dainty lips were cold,
And their emphasis was calmer
As they kissed the hero bold ;
Now we know she just was shirking,
And we realise what bliss
Is beneath the surface lurking
In the great and thrilling kiss.
For, oh ! what an unexpected
Wealth of passion there was hid !
We had never once suspected
She could do it as she did.
Not the gentle peck belated
Of the bread-and-butter miss—
There were glories congregated
In that warm, impassioned kiss.
Thoughts that no one can describe ’ll
Hedg-e about that joy misplaced ;
How we love that germy Bible,
Where such good things went to waste
And its place we would be taking
If the last oath went amiss,
For our hearts are hourly aching
For just such another kiss.
VIC.
A.B.

w. A. Holman’s Bill to compel N.S.W.
writers to attach their names to newspaper
articles is in essence the Governor Darling Bill of
1827. The Governor’s Bill was designed to pre-
vent “persons not known” from issuing news-
papers. One of the penalties in Darling’s Bill
was that an offender might be fined, imprisoned,
or banished from the colony and its dependencies.
Julian Salomons, several times within the last 20
years, tried to legislate against “unknown”
pressmen, but failed.
Thus S.M. Herald’s Wellington (M.L.)
correspondent, concerning Premier Seddon : —
He has grappled with many complex political and social
problems, and has placed on the Statute Book a mass of
unusual and experimental legislation. . . . He has easily
negotiated the rock of compulsory arbitration which
wrecked two Federal Governments ; he has made a bold
attempt to adjust the differences of capital and labor ; he
has established the principle of compulsory acquisition of
private estates for close settlements, and its ally, advances
to settlers ; he has provided old age pensions ; he has
established State fire insurance and opened State coal
mines—and he and his colleagues and their constituents
have seen the total trade of the colony grow from a valve
of about £16,000,000 in 1890 to something like £29,000,000
in 190h.
Still in every other issue the Herald is of opinion
that the inevitable result of Socialistic legislation
is to “paralyse trade.”
“ Wat Tell ” : As a teacher I have been
using the Australian School Readers almost daily
since they were first published, and agree with
many other teachers in thinking Reader IY.
about the worst of the series. The Bulletin
(20/7/’05) says : “ In a school reader there should
be no more chance of error or misprint than there
is in a bible.” Yet Reader IV. has many mis-
prints and grammatical blunders. Take, for
instance, the following : —
He got up and went to Pomponianus, and the rest of
his company, who were not unconcerned enough to think
of going to bed, they consulted together. —Page 89.
In the position, in which he was now holding his head,
I ought to have hit him between the nostrils and the eyes,
which was impossible.—Page 34.
This hornet has the reputation of being a very ugly
customer, but I found it no trouble to live on the most
friendly terms with them.—Page 178.
“ Its base rose and sunk," and “ A strong breeze sprung
up.” —Lesson XXVI.
I called out to them, as best I could in their language
and also made signs to them that I was willing to give
myself up to them.—Page 266.
And many more silly blunders of a similar
description. In bush schools Reader IV. is
repeatedly read from cover to cover by the higher
classes, and the elder children become utterly
wearied with the constant repetition, and lose all
interest in their work.
News comes from Stringer’s Creek vicinity,
Gippsland, of an attempt on the part of a zealot
named Hodgeson to wake the dormant religious
feeling of the natives, and get up a boom in
baptism by immersion. He so worked upon the
feelings of several foolish people that they agreed
to be immersed in the open before a ribald crowd,
with the result that the penitents were shockingly
afflicted with colds. However, the victims think
they are even now, as Hodgeson is himself in a
bad way. He was leading his latest convert into
the creek on a dull afternoon about a fortnight
back, when suddenly he went out’ of sight. As
he did not re-appear within a reasonable time,
well-meaning people began pawing about in the
»ud and slurry at the bottom of the creek in
quest of him, and at last a local pig-breeder found
his hair amongst the mud. Half a dozen volun-
teers helped to pull the revivalist out, but it took
a deal of hauling, as he had crowded Into a
hole that had once contained a pile. He was
only revived after the rules for the apparently
smothered had been vigorously operated for half-
an-hour. The revivalist is . still a-bed, and the
bottom has to some extent fallen out of the
baptism-by-immersion boom.

Arnold White believes profoundly in the
Japanese menace to Australia. In a recent review
article he writes :—
Mastery of the Pacific means that Australia, the richest
European possession in that ocean, will be attacked and
may fall a prey to the Japanese. Expansion is necessary
for both the Chinese Empire and for Japan, and cupidity
will be the guide of invasion. That Japan will suffer the
competition of British and American trade in the Far
East a day after she feels herself strong enough to exclude
it, is incredible.
Signs of the times ! The first pawn-
broker’s shop has been opened in Bloemfontein
(Orange Free State). Under the old Republican
Government pawnshops were not allowed and not
required, but they always follow the Flag.
Vic. geological director Dunn reports the
discovery of a quaintly thrifty person dwelling on
the Taedto Creek goldfield. This man owns a
handy little reef which runs 3oz. to the ton, and
whenever he wants to settle with the butcher or
grocer he steps into his back paddock, dollies off
a few hundredweight and squares up. At other
times he simply saves up the reef as an asset,
having no hunger after riches. As a specimen of
that Socialistic tendency which prevents capital
being brought into the country he is unique. His
obvious duty is to float his reef into a big com-
pany, sell his shares for a large sum, and then lose
the money in a shaky bank.
Referring to the debate in the British
Parliament on the Aliens Bill, Glasgow Evening
News says
Mr. Emmott spoke of this “ alien immigration bogey.”
If Mr. Emmott will go to the East End of London he will
find notices on the Underground Railway printed in a
foreign language, a theatre where the plays and actors
and actresses are foreigners, where the language on the
stage is foreign, a foreign newspaper, notices in the shops
in a foreign language, and if he asks the first person he
meets which is the way to Epping Forest the response
will probably be a shake of the head, as the person may
not understand a word of English. While all this is
taking place in the East End of London, thousands of
English artisans and laborers are leaving Liverpool every
week for America,
The Sydney Labor Council intends to sup-
port the Shop Assistants’ Union in an endeavor
to gain a universal Saturday half-holiday. As
arranged at present the holiday business is a
muddle. Wednesday afternoon is of little use to
anyone, while Saturday would give a worn-out
shopman a chance of a few extra hours consecr,
tively from labor. If Thursday were made the
fixed pay day for wages, shopkeepers would suffer
no loss, as housewives would make their weekly
purchases on Friday. Thursday would be a better
pay day than Saturday in any case, as the hard-
drinking father would not then have a clear half-
day in which to drink his wages, and the sporting
father would not have the same chance to immedi-
ately lose his pay on “ stiff” ponies.
. The British Australasian (London),
which is on the spot and knows, or ought to
know:—
Mr. Reid’s and his friends’ anti-Socialistic campaign has
effected no other object than to prejudice Australia still
further in this country.
The same paper says that the best thing that has
happened in the way of smashing the Six Flatters
lie and the Petriana perjury in England is the
action of the Westralian Labor Government in
taking out a small party of assisted immigrants.
Bushels of figures and gallons of argument were
helpless to smother the Stinking Fish lie that
immigrants were liable to be tossed overboard at
the first Australian port; but Westralia’s practical
move has knocked out the old fiction wherever the
two have met.
The State Governments appear to have
adopted a policy of systematically harrying the
Federal Government for larger and larger pay-
ments for the carriage of mails on the railways.
They already get £250,000 a year, and are far
from satisfied. Of course it makes no difference
in actual cash whether the States get £100,000
more for mail carriage, and consequently the same
amount less in the division of the Federal surplus.
But it makes the earnings of the State railways ,
look more. (Chorus of State Premiers : “See how
the railways are improving under our manage-
ment!”) Also, it makes the Federal expenses
look more. (Chorus of State Frights: “Owing
to gross Federal extravagance the expenses of the
Postal Department are now very much greater
than when it was under State control! ”)
“ Alone ” sends this :—
A sneerful superior person throws large pieces of scorni
at Bent re his Elwood swamp scheme (B. 3/8/’05). Bent
does not deserve to be hit with them. I claim to know
at least a little about that vigorous Parliamentarian, and
a good deal about the unbeautiful bog. In the past, full*
of enthusiasm, buns and lemonade, I chased the joys of
various Sunday-school picnics round about its landscape ;
and of later years I have lived and inhaled ozone in its
vicinity, and fallen into it continually. I rise with plea-
sure to say that Bent deserves nothing but credit for try-
ing his hand on its reclamation. An absolutely useless
bog is being converted into more or less drv land (quite
as dry and habitable as a good deal of Port Melbourne,
South Melbourne and the city itself), and, even if a littl®
money should be lost, the man is more than justified.
There would be just as much sense in shovelling con-
tumely on the men who reclaimed the bogs and gullies
that used to make the landscape about Elizabeth and
Flinders streets, Melbourne. Some people, having
acquired a habit of throwing things at a man for being
an unsuitable bank director, keep on throwing, and dis-
courage him, even though he takes a shovel and goes to-
work filling up fens.
The Bulletin doesn't know who Neili
MacGilp is, but it publishes this letter on sound
general principles:—
That utterly forlorn and futile body, the miscalled
Political Reform League, has latterly been dragging
wearily around the back districts of Otago (M.L.) seeking
to win converts to the most disconsolate (and not the-
least dishonest) political gospel ever preached for the en-
snaring of ignorance and greed. The preaching is very
badly done, and the chief apostle of the gruesome gospel
is John MacGregor, of Dunedin. So far as Otago is con-
cerned, the Political Reform League consists principally
of John MacGregor ; away below him, it is diluted in the-
average to about the intellectual flavor and consistency
of Neil MacGilp. MacGregor is regarded by the simple
leaguers as an orator. He isn’t. He is applauded in the
back parlors of muddledom as a genius. He’s not. He’sjust-
a lanky, political delusion with a wagging jaw and a badly-
diseased ambition ; and because Richard Seddon found,
him out he’s incurably sore. MacGregor’s object, and
the object of his snuffling league, is to down Seddon, It-
is much as if some heckling old dowager in busted
goloshes set out to down Mount Cook. There need be no-
special consternation among the admirers of the mountain.

Yes, these Japs are learning English very quickly.
The Foreign-trader who says that the
natural industry of this country consists of turning
some sheep out into a paddock and letting them
breed, might do worse than chew the report of the
which inquired into locomotive
building. The first 10 locomotives built at New-
port cost £3364 each, the second 10 £3048. The
difference is the gain through training men and
finding out how to do things. A third 10 would
probably show a further gain. But the Foreign-
trader says that, if a country that hasn’t trained
its men, and doesn’t know just howto do things,
and doesn’t know exactly what plant is wanted,
can’t compete from the jump with countries that
have been at the business for years, they shouldn’t
compete at all—they should just go on turning
sheep out into a paddock and smoking on the
fence while they watch the wool grow.
