Buy a Gun.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN you’ve reckoned up yer losses, an’
your winnin’s backin’ ’osses,
When you’ve ’ad ernough o’ football
an’ o’ wine;
When you’ve read the scores o’ cricket, tired o’
talkin’ o’ “th’ wicket,”
Will you listen to this little song of mine?—
I’m a sojer o’ salvation, an’ I wanter save the
nation,
Let the music play, an’ wave the Flag on ’igh !
Let the people come an’ listen, an’ let nobody be
missin’,
For a sojer wants to preach and testify.
An’ it ain’t erbout the “Father an’ the Son,”
But Australia’s got to fight an’ wants a gun !
Wants a gun—she’s empty-’anded; wants some
togs—she’s nearly stranded—
Won’t you ’elp your bloomin’ country—won’t you
buy yer land a gun ?
O ! the belt won’t go no tighter, an’ the ’eathen’s
goin’ to fight ’er,
Ay, the Japs are goin’ to plug ’er, an’ the Chil-
dren o’ the Sun ;
An’ she’s stranded, empty-’anded, an’ she badly
wants a gun !
There’s four million people playin’ and they
ougliter start a-prayin’,
Get converted ’gainst the day o’ wrath to come;
An’ I don’t want to alarm yer, but you’ll ’ave to
go an’ arm yer-
Selves —you’ll never keep off bullets wi’ a drum.
An’ you’ve done a ’eap o’ drummin’, but the enemy
is cornin’,
Would you be a cur an’ turn your back an’ run ?
If you’re not a mug you shouldn’t, and if you
know you wouldn’t—
Don’t you think you’d better go an’ buy a gun ?
If Australia’s got an empty water-bag ; if she’s
trampin’ round’ the country wi’ a swag,
Still be men. What are ye made o’ ? What on
earth are ye afraid o’ ?
Can’t ye give up backin’ ’orses ? Can’t you buy
yourselves a gun ?
If you’ve got a kid an’ love ’im, by the blazin’
stars above ’im,
Won’t you give up smokin’ cigarettes an’ buy a
smokin’ gun ?
There is you that’s made some money in this land
that’s wide an’ sunny
(Clear the crowd away an’ let the carriage pass—
Bow before the Customs rotter; give Au stralia to
the squatter,
An’ don't disturb ’is sheep upon the grass !)
But you’ll ’ave to guard your plunder when the
’ills are belchin’ thunder,
Or you’ll want a mighty motor-car to ride away
from ’ell !
Wi’ your country s blood and vitals you are
buyin’ empty titles,
But you ought to buy an army corps, an’ ships
and guns as well!
Can’t you see she’s got ’er back agin the wall ?
Ain’t you got no bloomin’ principle at all ?
Oh ! you’d pilfer an’ you’d pawn ’er, an’ you’d get
’er in a corner,
An’ you’d dress ’er up in titles when she mostly
wants a gun !
0 ! the belt won’t go no tighter, an’ th’ ’eathen’s
goin’ to fight ’er—
Ay ! the Japs are goin’ to plug ’er, an' the Children
o’ the Sun,
An’ you’ll want to sell your motor-car an’ buy a
motor-gun !
Buy a gun ! buy a gun!
By the writin’ on the wall we’re wantin’ one.
Buy a gun ! buy a gun !
O ! Australia, sleepin’ lazy in the sun !
Cadgin’ rations day by day
At the stations by the way,
Ain’t you game to tackle honest graft an’ try an’
grow a gun ?
tas.
C. LOUIS RANDALL.
“Business is Business.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EONEY and Son’s clearing sale was in
full swing, and Mr. Roney, senr., stood
at the door, rubbing his soft, unhealthy-
looking hands together, and smiled beautifully.
The autumn sale was an annual institution in
the establishment of Roney. Once a year he
sought to rid the firm of much old-fashioned or
faded stock, by means of judicious ticketing, and
other contrivances.
It was a time of much business for Roney, senr.;
a time, also, of much business for the Recording
Angel up above, but that is another matter.
Two new customers approached the busy shop.
They walked one behind the other like two sheep.
The first in file was a stalwart, strong-minded
farm-woman, whilst, endeavoring to hide behind
her, walked a gauche young girl, apparently a
dependent or poor relation, The girl wore a home-
made hat and gloves that weren’t mates, and
looked about as self-conscious and miserable as
though she didn’t wear anything at all.
Roney’s eye shone with pleasurable anticipation ;
and he bowed and smirked, and purred around
them with the affection of a sleek kitten greeting
the rabbit-man.
“What’s the price of this yere?” demanded
the farm-lady, indicating a brown jacket cut of a
period outside the remembrance of fashionable
woman.
“ The sale price of that article, madam, is 14s.
fid.”
“Go on ! The bloomin’ thing’s all out o’ date.
Wuth about eighteen pence it is. I wanted some-
thing che*p, just to wear in the yard.”
As a business man, Roney, senr., had a fault,
and this annoying remark called it into active
service. .
“Now, that just shows your ignorance,” he
spat, fiercely. “That jacket cost £2 10s. origin-
ally, and I’ve knocked off 355. 6d. just to please
my old customers. It’s just a dead loss to me.”
“ Keep yer dea* loss,” snorted the farm-woman,
“ I won’t deprive yer of it.”
“I wouldn’t sell it to you, indeed, to wear in
the cow-yard,’’ cried Roney, in childish rage. “A
lovely thing like that—fit for a countess. I prefer
to keep it for people who know the proper uses of
things, and can appreciate a great sacrifice when
they see it.”
This outburst was greeted by a murmur of ap-
plause from two prim old ladies, who gazed in
cold horror at the farm-woman, and afterwards
bought the jacket themselves. Roney, junr.,
hastened upon the scene, to make peace with the
farm-woman.
Roney, senr., swallowed his temper with diffi-
culty, and turned to the unwieldy girl who stared
timidly at him.
“What sort of gloves did you say, my dear?”
he cooed softly. “French kid ?”
The girl blushed guiltily, and tried to hide her
wretched hands, upon which his eyes were fixed.
She hadn’t said gloves or anything else, but the
shame of her shabby appearance was strong in her
heart, and she had not courage to resist.
“ I-I will see s-some cotton gloves, if you
p-please,” she stammered, nervously; “but I
really don’t want any.”
But the cotton gloves were in a box on the very
top shelf, and Mr. Roney was a little frightened
of the steps, and a deal more frightened of the
trouble.
“No young ladies ever wear cotton gloves,” he
remonstrated, sweetly—“ only extremely com-
mon people, such as washerwomen. Now there’s
a pair for you. I can do you those for 7s. 6d., or
we’ll say 6s. 6d. for a pretty girl like you.”
The pretty girl cleared her throat with a terror-
stricken sound, and answered in tremulous tones :
“ I don’t like that color—a-a bright green, they
wouldn’t look nice.”
“ A girl with a complexion like yours could
wear a rainbow, or a bird of Paradise, or any-
thing,” Mr. Roney assured her in an oily voice.
This piece of delicate flattery increased the
poor relation’s confusion, and she nearly fell dead.
The farm-woman was now fighting Roney, junr.,
to the death over a drab jacket at 6s. 9d.
“ I’d really—l’d—l’d rather not,” she pleaded,
helplessly.
Roney shrugged his shoulders in a pet. It
seemed as though he was never going to get those
hideous gloves off his hands.
“Try these,” he lisped, softly, pouncing upon a
lavender pair belonging to a size that sold very
slowly.
They were three sizes too small for the girl, and
his practised eye knew it at once, but “ business
is business ” was Roney’s motto. He made a pre-
tence of fitting them over her knuckles, purring
happily all the time, and exuding flattery as
mechanically as he breathed.
“ A splendid fit,” was his verdict, and he folded
them hastily in a piece of tissue paper, and fell to
rubbing his hands once more, till the girl thought
they must certainly strike fire.
“Now, anything in the way of perfume, my
dear? Of course, all beautiful young ladies love
perfume. What is a rose without its exquisite
fragrance ? ”
“Oh, please, thank you. I don’t want any. I
really don’t want anything.”
“But, at least, you will let me show you. See,
now, this dainty little French box, only 15s. 6d.,
but,” lowering his voice to the confidential level,
“ I’ll let you have it for 10s. 6d., only you must
not breathe a word about it.”
The girl protested that she didn’t want scent,
but old Roney rushed it on to her while she waved
her hands, and tried to explain, and fried in her
own blushes. The farm-woman and Roney, junr.
were now fighting over a dirt-colored jacket at
ss. 10|d.
The blushful girl was as clay in the hands of a
potter, and Roney hurriedly sold her some lace,
and stockings, and studs, and sundries.
“The boss seems to have a fairly good thing on
over there,” said Roney, junr. in a pause to the as-
sistant. ‘‘ He must have sold two or three pounds’
worth to that girl, and I don’t believe she wanted
to buy anything. Holy smoke ! What’s hap-
pened ? ”
The shy young lady had at last induced Mr.
Roney to cease supplying her with goods she
didn’t want, and he paused with a beaming smile.
“ Two pounds two,” he murmured gentlymaking
out a little bill and handing her a parcel.
The girl hung the parcel on her wrist in an absent-
minded manner. An obstruction rose in her
throat. She stood staring at Mr. Roney with the
look of a dead girl and tried unsuccessfully to
speak.
“Two pounds two,” repeated Mr. Roney, re-
placing his pen gently behind his shell-like ear.
The girl, thus admonished, fished out her purse
with a shaking hand, and taking out sixpence
laid it on the counter.
“ Two pounds two, two pounds two! Dear, dear
me, two pounds two ! ” Roney raised his voice
almost to a snap.
“It’s all I’ve got,” stammered the girl, and
moved to sudden terror by the expression of
Roney’s face, she backed like a horse into a small
table of artificial palms, upsetting the whole
ricketty show, and smashing the earthenware pot
which held the plants to fragments.
Uttering a shriek she plunged forward again,
but tripped upon a banana-peel that some criminal
had dropped, and fell clawing wildly into the lace
counter near by, tearing off a whole knoll of half-
unfurled lace.
Startled by the sudden clamor, Roney, junr ,
and the assistant ran forward whilst some people
who had just entered strolled up curiously.
“Keep her back—don’t let her get away,”
shrieked Roney, senr.
His words suggested a plan of action to the des-
Eerate girl, who already, in imagination, felt the
and of the Law on her collarette and heard its
harsh exhortation to “ come quiet.”
With a few strong bounds she reached the door,
overturning the puny assistant on her way, and
followed more or less closely by half the contents
of the lace counter, with which she had become
entangled.
Pausing for one moment at the door to kick her
feet free of their lacey trammels, she dashed into
the street, the hue and cry at her heels, and the
tiny parcel still unconsciously hanging from her
wrist.
A few doors up, a fine horse was tied outside a
pub., waiting impatiently for his thirsty boss
within. To gain him she felt was her one hope of
safety. But the feet of Roney, junr., were patter-
ing on the pavement very close behind.
But she ground her teeth together and strained
every muscle, and with bound after bound, like a
wallaby in flight, reached the startled horse,
and, breaking the rein from its ring, sprang into
the saddle like a bush girl pursued by furies. She
rode astride, just like a man, and walloped the
horse with the parcel like a demon.
A policeman, who suddenly approached, clutched
frantically at the bridle rein, but the brute, in-
fected with the excitement of his rider and
delighting in his freedom, wheeled about on his
hind legs, flinging the bobby in the road, and de-
parted in a cloud of dust.
Who owns that’orse ? ” roared the dignitary,
arising from the earth in wrath.
“ Him,” responded a small boy, pointing to a
man who propped himself up against the door-
post, and smiled weakly.
Roney, junr., didn’t wait to see the end of that
play. He took a last look at an object far up the
road, which was fast becoming a speck on the
then hurried back.
k 1 his serves the old un dash well right,” he
remarked to the assistant as they entered the
shop. “He’s too jolly smart for anything.”
In the shop Roney, senr., sat alone, glaring in a
species of fascination upon the sixpence. The
expression of his face had cleared the establish-
ment of customers in 10 seconds. The farm-
woman had cast the jacket m the dust and gone
to look for her lost drudge and poor relation.
“ Have you got her ? W-where is she ? ” Roney
whispered hoarsely to his son.
“ Quite safe. Don’t you trouble, dad,” tittered
Roney, junr.
“ Where —where ? ”
“Very near home, I should think, by the way
she cut it out.”
Roney raised his soft white fist and struck the
sixpence again and again in the impotence and
blackness of his passion; while from his tongue
flowed inarticulate sounds that suggested a very
large cat spitting.
. But the glass doors swung open and admitted
six customers all in a bunch.
Mr. Roney adjusted his eyeglass and tried to
arrange his disordered smile; but the creature
that glared into his eyes from the mirror opposite
was too much even for his feelings, and he sneaked
off into a back room with a stricken, weary air.

IT is well to remember that at this season, of all others, the human system needs to
be toned up and put in thorough condition to withstand the change of the season.
BILE BEANS for BILIOUSNESS are the very best medicine that can be taken
for this purpose, as they cleanse and regulate the liver, an organ to which may be
traced nearly all the minor ailments so many people suffer from. If the liver is not in
thorough working order the blood is impure, the circulation
sluggish, the digestion impaired, the sleep disturbed, and the
complexion sallow. The consequence is that there is a lack
of energy, and the sufferer becomes a victim of “that tired
feeling.” To be out of sorts in the Spring is most trying, as
many people know. If you are feeling “run down,’ and
without any energy for work, take BILE BEANS for
BILIOUSNESS, and they will put you in a condition that
will withstand the changeable Spring weather.

Melbourne Revisited.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HERE, but four iron ships and two long
“ liners,”
To speak the wealth of nations over-sea,
And much regretted all those brave designers
Whose wide-winged clippers first in ’fifty-three
Bore o'er two oceans tons of gold our miners
Wrested from Mother-Earth when all was free.
No more our “flying-kites,” our racing packets,
Our “Marco Polos,” “Lightnings,” and “Red
Jackets.”
Yet why so much regret a Golden Past
To-day but grey Tradition —nothing more ?
Here now the “land-fall ” and the Port at last,
The wished for and the loved, familiar shore ;
No desert coast-line whereon to be cast
Spells Desolation as in days before,
As Selkirk on his island sadly landed
To find himself alone, a Triton stranded.
And here we are once more, our voyage ended,
The trams roll past; bright flash the nickeled
“ bikes,”
With forward-leaning riders ; broad backs bended,
Their crank-like legs akimbo ; much it strikes
The calm beholder’s eye, those lank limbs blended,
Met but to part again, and crossed like pikes
In weird confusion at some scene of slaughter,
Or microbes dancing on a drop of water.
What buzz of automobiles ’tis that hums
Careering on their way up hill and down
Oil German-sausage tyres; had I the drums
That Santerre beat his monarch’s voice to drown
I’d utilise them here in streets and slums
To silence such disturbers of the town,
And folk should sing my praise in strophes
Sapphic
For signal services to city traffic.
And Collins-street is Collins-street —the same
Big buildings, little buildings, cabs and trams ;
Though none too lively, not entirely tame.
The self-same stuff, the self-same window crams,
The self-same “posters ” on the hoardings flame,
Footballers, cricketers and gross prize-rams
Stare from the walls in every picture-paper
To charm, arrest and trance each languid gaper.
The crowd’s still very much the crowd we knew ;
Her pale, familiar face the Town Hall clock
Still wears, and stares down on the motley crew
That swarms along the pavement past the Block.
Some on the right side, some the wrong ; ’tis true
Most take the wrong with now no Duke to shock;
For, who would stop a lady to correct her
For rule ignored ? Not e’en a street-inspector.
See into Mullen’s rolls the same gay tide.
The self-same tide is rolling out again,
And murmurs as it’s spreading far and wide
With books upon its bosom ; food for brain,
And some for Fancy ; some to pleasure Pride,
Or please the pious ; while, in endless chain,
Reels out the pictured lore that much enhances
The joy of all that love our old romances.
These be the Melbourne streets ; see two, see all;
The one shows shops, and fashions, journals,
clubs,
Some leading doctors, dentists, and a. Hall;
The other, work-shops, warehouses, and pubs,
The humbler restaurant, the “house of call,”
And breweries with vats and foaming tubs ;
The greater, boastful, chic, aristocratic ;
The smaller, quite as rich, but democratic.
I ne’er yet loved a city . . . no, not I,
Half well enough to choose it for a home,
"Where smoke and piles of brick obscure the sky,
And asphalt only tempts one out to roam
For exercise or something he must buy :
Give me the trackless bush, the salt sea foam—
. Although indeed down here the sight of shops is
.Not quite beyond the scope of my synopsis.
\IO.
G. G. M‘C.
De Umbris
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT is a horrible day. All yesterday it
rained, all last night, and all this morning,
and everything is drenched. A little while
ago it stopped, and two of the chaps went out into
the soaking bush to try and get a pig. We are
out of meat.
I don’t know how they stand the racket, year
after year; always shifting about, getting wet,
swagging heavy loads, and living roughly.
They are back without anything—except the
theodolite with the box full of water. A wonder
it did not float away. The theodolite is my chief
instrument of torture, and I will rejoice exceed-
ingly when it floats out of my life for ever.
A man that takes on the Government Survey in
Maoriland is one of the biggest fools on the
planet. You get back into this cursed bush with
a gang of wretches like yourself, and year in and
year out you explore for ro*ds, run back-lines,
traverse ridges and cieeks, receive circulars, eat
old boar and spuds, feed mosquitoes, and slog your
soul out in isolation—and all for a beggarly screw
that no merchant would offer a decent clerk.
You never leave the bush, except for about six
weeks in winter, when they bring you in to do
your plans. Time was when they promoted the
older men, but now office-men generally get the
high billets which used to go to the senior sur-
veyors, anu the grey-haired “field” men are left
to hang out miserably in the bush, like spectres
roosting on damp tombstones.
Let every man who has sons keep them away
from the Survey—awry from the bush. Let no
man be deceived by any flapdoodle about a
fine healthy, natural life.” To send a boy into
the’Maoriland bush at the present time, m any
service or capacity, is both cruel and wicked.
The diet is meagre, unwholesome, and badly
•cooked ; the conditions are terribly severe, and
the whole life is hideously barren, unhealthy, and
, unnatural.
PAKEHA.
The Twelve-Quid Show.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JIM SUDELEY was foreman engineer
on the Outpost mine—silver, lead, etc. —
and considered that he earned his money.
Jim was not a Scotchman—a race to whom,
according to accredited stories, boss-engineering
jobs are a natural heritage—but an Australian.
That he ought to have been Scotch and the inevi-
table Mac something is obvious ; but anyone who
said he was a Caledonian might meet the fate of
Ananias at any moment. So things must needs
remain as they are.
Jim was smoking musefully one afternoon shift,
and watching the driver oiling round. The driver
was a new man on the mine, and Jim noted with
sub-acid amusement the interest he took in that
disgustingly familiar knock which thudded so
relentlessly at each revolution of the mill engine.
When the man had finished, he gave his hands
a professional wipe with a piece of waste, and re-
turning the same to his jacket pocket, approached
Jim with the obvious intention of friendly con-
verse.
“Knocks a bit, don’t she?’’ he remarked,
cheerfully, as he produced his pipe and a cake of
tobacco.
Jim nodded.
“ Where did they get her ? ”
“ Oh, over at the Hill, of course,” replied Jim,
nodding towards where, above the horizon, a dark
smoke, blue by day and a pallid flare by night, be-
tokened the locality of Broken Hill.
“ Picked her up pretty cheap, I should judge?”
suggested the driver sagaciously, as he lit up.
“ Got her off the bargain-counter at one of the
big drapery sales, I believe. Know old M‘Minn?
Mac reckoned he would have had her himself only
he had no small change about him.”
The driver opined that Mac was a tough propo-
sition, and Jim continued in reminiscent vein.
“ She was a high-pressure winder in the old days,
somewhere, and when these people got her they
were advised by their ‘consulting engineer’ to
compound her. I’d as soon compound a felony.
’Twasn’t quite a fair deal, you know, but honest
men don’t plant their beastly virtue in mining
transactions. If the Outpost crowd were as wise
as they are willing, they’d have got a real service-
able engine for what that clattering old dispensa-
tion has cost them, first and last.”
At this juncture a new knock suddenly became
apparent to the trained ears of the two men. A
knock which sprang from nothingness as it were,
and neatly bisected the pauses between the old
knocks. It grew louder, and Jim walked over and
pulled the mill bell rope, and waited. The driver
put his knife handle between his teeth, the tip of
the open blade on the cylinder, and putting his
thumbs to his ears and his little fingers to each
side of the knife blade, listened intently. _
At this moment the mill shift boss arrived like
an allegorical figure of Haste, inquiring, “What
in all ’L is the matter now ? ”
“ Get your load off, Billy,” said the imperturb-
able Jim. “ I’m going to stop.”
“Well, she is hot stuff !” remarked the vexed
shift boss as he flew back to his mill, and got all
feed shut off, belts run on to loose pulleys, &c., so
that the mill might run itself empty, thus prevent-
ing the choking of jigs, vanners, &e., when the
engine stopped. The stopping of a concentrating
mill without ringing the load off is the star crime
in this class of work.
Presently he reappeared and nodded to Jim.
Jim nodded to the driver who was “standing
by,” and who immediately shut off steam; Jim
directing him, as the engine slowed down, to
“stop her on the inner centre, low-press side.”
In a few minutes a fitter and some laborers
arrived, a tackle was slung, the cylinder cover
whipped off, and the slackened nut behind the
piston head tightened home.
“You’ll never make a lady of her, Jim,” said
Billy West, the shift boss, as they stood watching
the men hurriedly tightening the cover nuts.
Jim assented.
“ And that’s her good side, too,” he remarked.
In a few minutes everything was ready, and,
after a preliminary pull at the bell-rope to warn
the mill hands to stand clear, the driver got his
engine running as before, minus the new knock it
had developed.
Billy West was explaining elaborately to Jim,
from inspiration obtained, that if he “ had the
running of the cow ” he’d have her “ lined up,”
also “re-bedded,” as well as provided with “new
brasses—and a bigger flywheel,” he added, gener-
ously, as an afterthought.
Before Jim could acknowledge these vital hints,
he was summoned by a profane blast of entreaty
from Lijah Trevor, underground boss, who in-
formed him that the cage was at the poppet-heads.
Jim softly remarked “Dam,” and went with
him to see about the matter.
As luck would have it, the damage done was
very small, and things were easily put right, so
that Jim lent an indulgent ear to the driver’s
chronic curse upon the debilitated brake. He was
then condemned to acquire a good deal of specu-
lative knowledge as to the correct method of
handling a winding engine, gearing the drums,
&c., from Lijah Trevor.
Jim listened in silence inscrutable, and bade
Lijah good night.
“ I’ll be back in a bit o’ while, ef yourn mind to
wait, an’ shaw ’ee wot I mane,” roared Lijah after
the fleeing Jim.
Passing the mill engine-room Jim looked m to
see how the driver was getting on. He found
him standing at the door to get the breeze, and
polishing his iron-grey curls with the usual hand-
ful of waste.
“ How’s things ? ” inquired Jim, looking round.
He noticed an oil drum slung over a main bearing,
with a trickle of water running from it, and a con-
fusion of spanners lying near the crank.
“ Real good ! There hasn’t been a thing wrong
since you went but a hot main bearing, the set
screw out of the big end cotter, and the main belt
slipping. I say, she’s a bit of a surprise packet,
isn’t she ? ”
“ Well, some might look at her in that light.
“ Think she has anything else up her sleeve ? ”
“I should say—yes. She’s generally pretty
giddy by the end of the week, but seeing that
you’re a stranger, I suppose she’s studying appear-
ances.”
“ Well, that’s thoughtful of her, anyhow. Oh,
I forgot to tell you. The fireman was here a
while back, looking for you. Reckons that one
of his boilers is leaking like billybedam—l think
he’s an Irishman—and his feed pump’s dead
crook. Contemplates stealing his boy’s squirt
to-morrow to supplement the feed pump.”
“ I wish he’d thought of it before. The feed
pump’s not in very good nick.”
“Well, Pm blowed if you’re not a—an optimist
or something. What sort of a show do you call
this, anyhow ? ”
“ A twelve quid show.”
“ And what may that be ? ”
“ She only runs when lead is twelve quid or
over, and not always then.”
